Classification of Divine Attributes by Existence and Non-Existence
Enumerates three categories of divine attributes: mental, negative, and existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of divine attributes based on their relationship to existence and non-existence, and be able to distinguish between attributive, negative, and existential attributes.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are classified into mental, negative, and real.

The author presents a systematic classification of Allah's attributes by considering whether they have existence in the mind, are pure negations, or are real positive attributes additional to the essence. This division helps clarify the nature of attributes that even opponents of extra attributes affirm (the negative ones) versus those that are debated. The passage sets the stage for deeper discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the three categories and their definitions.
Pay attention to the terms: i'tibariyya, 'adamiyya, wujudiyya.
2
Identify the three categories
List each category and its definition as given in the passage: (1) i'tibariyya - mental existence only, (2) 'adamiyya - pure negations, no existence, (3) wujudiyya - real additional attributes.
Also note the subcategories mentioned: the essential attribute and entitative attributes under i'tibariyya, the five negative attributes under 'adamiyya, and attributes of meanings under wujudiyya.
3
Understand the relationship to thubutiyya
The passage mentions that 'thubutiyya' (affirmative) attributes include i'tibariyya, ma'ani, and ma'nawiyya. Reflect on how this terminology overlaps.
This shows that 'thubutiyya' is broader than just existential attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is this classification important? It shows that not all attributes are real positive additions; some are mental constructs or negations, which even those who deny extra attributes affirm.
This classification helps reconcile different theological positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا يمكن أن نقول : صفاته تعالى تنقسم من حيث الوجود والعدم إلى : صفات اعتبارية : وهي الصفات التي لها وجود في ذهن المعتبر فقط، وليست زائدة على الذات خارجاً، بل ذهناً فقط، وهي الصفة النفسية (الوجود)، والصفات المعنوية السبعة عند القائلين بها . صفات عدمية : وهي التي لا وجود لها أصلاً (بمعنى أنه لا قيام لها بالذات)، بل مفهومها محض تفي، ولكونها كذلك لم يتحرج نفاة الصفات من إثباتها، وهي الصفات السلبية الخمسة. صفات وجودية : وهي الصفات الزائدة على الذات خارجاً، وإنما هي قائمة بها، وهي صفات المعاني . ويقولون : صفات ثبوتية، ويريدون بها الاعتبارية وصفات المعاني والمعنوية، وسيأتي لهذا مزيد إيضاح في موطنه إن شاء الله تعالى .
Here we can say: His attributes, may He be exalted, are divided in terms of existence and non-existence into: Attributive attributes (sifat i'tibariyya): They are the attributes that have existence only in the mind of the one considering them, and are not additional to the essence externally, but only mentally. They are the essential attribute (the existence) and the seven entitative attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya) according to those who affirm them. Negative attributes (sifat 'adamiyya): They are those that have no existence at all (meaning they do not subsist in the essence), but their concept is pure negation. Because they are like that, the negators of attributes did not hesitate to affirm them. They are the five negative attributes. Existential attributes (sifat wujudiyya): They are the attributes additional to the essence externally, and they subsist in it. They are the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani). And they say: affirmative attributes (sifat thubutiyya) and by that they mean the attributive attributes and the attributes of meanings and entitative attributes. And further clarification of this will come in its proper place, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes according to their relationship to existence (wujud) and non-existence ('adam). It is an enumeration with three categories. The first is i'tibariyya (attributive) attributes: these have existence only in the mind and are not extra realities; they include the essential attribute (al-wujud) and the seven entitative attributes (ma'nawiyya) as per those who affirm them. The second is 'adamiyya (negative) attributes: these have no real existence and are pure negations; they correspond to the five negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) which even those who deny extra attributes affirm. The third is wujudiyya (existential) attributes: these are real, additional attributes subsisting in the essence, namely the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani). The passage also notes that the term 'thubutiyya' (affirmative) encompasses i'tibariyya, ma'ani, and ma'nawiyya. It promises more detail later.

Key Terms
الصفات الاعتبارية
الصفات العدمية
الصفات الوجودية
الصفات الثبوتية
الصفات السلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes are real positive attributes added to the essence.
Correction: This classification shows that some attributes are only mental constructs (i'tibariyya) or pure negations ('adamiyya), not real additions.

Checkpoint
Which category of attributes includes the five negative attributes (sifat salbiyya)?
A) Attributive attributes (i'tibariyya)
B) Negative attributes ('adamiyya)
C) Existential attributes (wujudiyya)
D) Essential attributes

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are classified into mental (i'tibariyya), negative ('adamiyya), and existential (wujudiyya) based on their relation to existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson divides attributes into mere mental concepts, negations, and real qualities. When you think about God's attributes, do you ever reduce some of them to mere concepts in your mind rather than living realities that should shape your awe and worship? Which ones, and what will you do to restore their weight?
?
The passage notes that even those who deny extra attributes affirm the negative attributes. How does this challenge you to take God's negative attributes (like that He never needs help or never tires) as seriously as His positive ones? Can you name a negative attribute that you have neglected in your daily awareness of God, and how will you bring it to mind tomorrow?
?

The Definition of Existence as a State (Hal)
Defines existence as a necessary, uncaused state of the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition defines the attribute of existence as a 'state' (hal) that is necessary for the essence and uncaused, and how this definition relates to the broader concept of attributes.

Core Idea
Existence is a necessary state of the essence, not a superadded attribute.

This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary on al-Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin introduces the contested nature of existence as an attribute. It begins by clarifying that an attribute can be merely conceptually distinct, then presents al-Razi's view that existence is a 'state' (hal) that is necessary for the essence without being caused. This foundational definition sets the stage for a deeper discussion of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening remark about attributes
Note that the author says an attribute suffices if it is conceptually distinct, even if not extra. This is a key principle that allows negative attributes like pre-eternity to count as attributes.
This leniency is important for understanding the subsequent definition.
2
Identify al-Razi's claim
Find where al-Razi says existence is other than the existent. Understand that this is based on the general rule that an attribute must be distinct from its subject.
3
Absorb the definition of existence as a state
Read the definition clause by clause. Note the three components: state, necessary for essence as long as essence subsists, uncaused by a cause.
This definition is built on the theory of states (ahwal).
4
Analyze the explanatory notes
The commentary explains 'state' as an intermediary, 'necessary for the essence' as lasting the duration of the essence, and 'uncaused' as excluding caused states like being powerful. Notice how this guards against misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفةٌ تسامح؛ لأن الصفة يكفي فيها مغايرة الموصوف وإن لم تكن زائدة في الخارج، كيف وقد عدُّوا السُّلوب صفاتٍ كالقدم والبقاء؟ وقال الرازي وجماعة: الوجود غيرُ الموجود ضرورة مغايرة الصفة للموصوف، وعليه فقد عرَّفوا الوجود بأنَّه: الحالُ الواجبة للذات ما دامت الذات، حال كون تلك الحالِ غير معلَّلة بعلة، والمراد بكونها حالاً أنَّها واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم على القول بثبوت الواسطة التي هي حال، ومعنى كونها واجبة للذات ما دامت الذات: أنَّها ثابتةٌ للذات مدَّةَ دوام الذات، وخرج بقولنا: (غير معللة بعلة) الحالةُ المعللة بعلة؛ كالكون قادراً، فإنَّه حال معلل بعلة؛ أي: لازم لملزوم؛ وهو القدرة، ورجع بعضهم الخلاف لفظيًّا، فحمل كلام الأشعري على أن الوجود ليس زائداً في الخارج، فلا ينافي أنَّه حال، وهو مراد الثاني، وجرى على ذلك المصنف في الشرح، وقيل: الخلاف حقيقي، فقول الأشعري محمول على أنه أمر اعتباري على التحقيق؛ وقول غيره محمول على أنَّه حال.
An attribute is lenient; because it suffices in an attribute that it be conceptually distinct from the described [subject] even if not superadded externally. How? They have counted negative attributes as attributes like pre-eternity and everlastingness! And al-Razi and a group said: existence is other than the existent by necessity of the attribute being distinct from the described. Upon this, they defined existence as: the state necessarily [belonging] to the essence as long as the essence [subsists], that state being uncaused by a cause. The meaning of its being a state is that it is an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, according to the position affirming the intermediary that is a state. And the meaning of its being necessary for the essence as long as the essence [subsists] is that it is established for the essence for the duration of the essence's subsistence. And by our saying 'uncaused by a cause' is excluded the state caused by a cause, like being powerful, for it is a state caused by a cause; i.e. a necessary consequent of a necessary condition, which is power. And some considered the disagreement verbal, interpreting al-Ash'ari's statement that existence is not superadded externally, so it does not contradict that it is a state, which is the second intended meaning. The author followed this in the commentary. And it was said: the disagreement is real, so al-Ash'ari's statement is understood as that it is a mental consideration, while the other's statement is understood as that it is a state.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine existence and presents a dispute about its nature. It begins by remarking that an attribute can be merely conceptually distinct from its subject even if not extra in external reality, as with negative attributes like pre-eternity (qidam) which are real attributes despite negating. Then it introduces al-Razi's view: existence (wujud) is other than the existent (mawjud) because an attribute must be distinct from its subject. Based on that, al-Razi and his followers define existence as 'the state necessary for the essence as long as the essence subsists, that state being uncaused by a cause.' This definition rests on the theory of states (ahwal) which posits an intermediate between existence and non-existence. The explanation clarifies: 'state' means intermediary between existent and non-existent; 'necessary for the essence as long as the essence subsists' means it is established for the essence for the entire duration of the essence; 'uncaused by a cause' excludes states like being powerful, which are caused by an underlying attribute (power). The passage then reports two attempts to reconcile al-Ash'ari's denial of the superaddedness of existence with al-Razi's view: some say the disagreement is merely verbal, interpreting al-Ash'ari as meaning not extra in external reality while not denying it is a state; the author of the commentary follows this. Others say the disagreement is real: al-Ash'ari considers existence a mental consideration (i'tibari), while the other party considers it a real state.

Key Terms
الوجود
الموجود
الحال
الصفة النفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think existence is a superadded attribute like power or knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows existence is a necessary state of the essence, not an extra attribute subject to causation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's definition, the existence of a thing is:
A) An attribute superadded to the essence in external reality
B) A state necessary for the essence and uncaused by a cause
C) Identical to the essence with no distinction
D) A purely mental concept with no reality

Key Takeaway
Existence is a necessary state of the essence, not a caused attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is a necessary state uncaused by any cause of yours, then what does that imply about your complete dependence on the One whose existence is necessary by itself? Do you truly live as if your very being is a gift held by another at every moment?
?
You distinguish between your power (which is a caused state) and your existence (which is uncaused). In what areas of your life do you mistakenly treat your existence as if it were a product of your own striving?
?
The passage says negative attributes like pre-eternity are real attributes. If Allah's pre-eternity is not a superadded quality but a necessary state of His essence, how does that reshape the awe you feel when you say 'Allah is Eternal'? Are you content with a superficial understanding of His names?

The Verbal and Real Dispute on Existence
Reports and analyzes the dispute over existence as a state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways scholars have reconciled the differing views of al-Ash'ari and al-Razi on the nature of existence, and why some consider it a verbal dispute while others see it as real.

Core Idea
The dispute over existence is either verbal—focusing on external extra—or real—about mental existence.

After presenting al-Razi's definition of existence as a state, the passage turns to a famous disagreement between al-Ash'ari and al-Razi. Some scholars argue the disagreement is merely verbal, while others insist it is real, concerning whether existence is a mental consideration or a real state. This lesson unpacks those interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions in the dispute
Notice that one group says the disagreement is verbal (lafzi), interpreting al-Ash'ari's denial of extra existence as not contradicting that it is a state. Another group says it is real (haqiqi), taking al-Ash'ari's view as mental consideration and the other as real state.
2
Understand the verbal resolution
See how they reconcile: al-Ash'ari meant existence is not extra in external reality, while al-Razi meant it is a state (hal), which is not external. So they mean different things.
This is the author's preferred view in the commentary.
3
Grasp the real dispute
The other interpretation holds that al-Ash'ari considered existence a mental concept, while al-Razi considered it a real state. Those are genuinely different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

An attribute is lenient; because it suffices in an attribute that it be conceptually distinct from the described [subject] even if not superadded externally. How? And al-Razi and a group said: existence is other than the existent by necessity of the attribute being distinct from the described. According to the passage, one way to reconcile al-Ash'ari and al-Razi is by interpreting the disagreement as:

?
When you encounter a theological disagreement between revered scholars, do you instinctively take sides, or do you pause to consider that their words may be reconcilable? What would it take for you to adopt the humility of searching for a verbal resolution before assuming a real rift?
?
The passage shows that even a seemingly deep dispute can be reframed as merely verbal. Is there a conflict in your own life—with a family member, friend, or community—that you have assumed is irreconcilable, but which might actually stem from different wording rather than different hearts? What one step can you take this week to test that possibility?
?
Al-Ash'ari and al-Razi are both giants of Islam, yet their disagreement over existence is here shown to be potentially verbal. Does this unsettle the certainty with which you hold some of your own theological opinions? Name one belief about Allah that you hold strongly—how confident are you that it is not merely a verbal nuance? What would it mean for your worship if it were?

Understanding the Self-Attribute (Sifah Nafsiyyah)
Defines the self-attribute and distinguishes it from other attribute types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a self-attribute (sifah nafsiyyah) and how it differs from negative attributes, meaningful attributes, and allusive attributes.

Core Idea
A self-attribute describes only the essence, not an extra quality.

This lesson introduces the concept of the self-attribute (sifah nafsiyyah), a key term in Islamic theology. The passage defines it as a positive attribute that indicates the essence itself without adding any extra meaning. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins by stating that existence is a self-attribute.
2
Identify the three parts of the definition
The passage breaks down the definition into: (1) 'attribute' as a genus, (2) 'positive' to exclude negative attributes, and (3) 'indicates the essence itself' to exclude attributes that imply extra meaning.
Each part narrows the scope of what counts as a self-attribute.
3
Understand what is excluded
The definition excludes negative attributes (like pre-eternity), meaningful attributes (like power), and allusive attributes (which imply meaningful attributes). Ask yourself why each is excluded.
Exclusion is based on whether the attribute indicates an extra meaning beyond the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الوجودَ صفةٌ نفسيَّة، وإنَّما نسبت للنفس - أي: الذات - لأنَّها لا تُتَعقَّلُ إلا بها، فلا تتعقل نفس إلا بوجودها، والمراد بالصفة النفسية: صفةٌ ثبوتيَّةٌ يدلُّ الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها، كأن يُقال: الوجود صفةٌ لله تعالى. فقولنا: (صفة) كالجنس، وقولنا: (ثبوتية) يخرجُ السلبية؛ كالقدم والبقاء، وقولنا: يدلُّ الوصفُ بها على نفس الذات معناه: أنَّها لا تدلُّ على شيءٍ زائد على الذات، فقولنا: (دون معنى زائد عليها) تفسيرُ مرادٍ لقولنا: على نفس الذات، ويخرج المعاني؛ لأنها تدلُّ على معنى زائد على الذات، وكذلك المعنوية؛ فإنَّها تستلزم المعاني، فهي تدلُّ على معنى زائد على الذات لاستلزامها المعاني.
And know that existence is a self-attribute, and it is only attributed to the self - i.e., the essence - because [the essence] is not conceptualized except by it, so no essence is conceptualized except by its existence. And the meaning of self-attribute is: a positive attribute by which the description indicates the essence itself without an extra meaning upon it, such as saying: existence is an attribute of God, exalted is He. So our saying 'attribute' is like a genus; our saying 'positive' excludes negative [attributes] like pre-eternity and everlastingness; and our saying 'by which the description indicates the essence itself' means that it does not indicate anything extra upon the essence. Thus our saying 'without an extra meaning upon it' is an interpretive gloss for our saying 'upon the essence itself'. And it excludes meaningful [attributes] because they indicate an extra meaning upon the essence, and likewise the allusive attributes because they necessitate meaningful [attributes], so they indicate an extra meaning upon the essence due to their necessitating the meaningful [attributes].
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'self-attribute' (sifah nafsiyyah) and clarifies its scope. It begins by stating that existence (wujud) is a self-attribute, explaining that it is attributed to the essence (nafs or dhat) because the essence cannot be conceptualized without it. The definition: a self-attribute is a positive (thubutiyya) attribute that, when describing something, refers only to the essence itself without adding any extra meaning. The example given is saying 'existence is an attribute of God.' The passage then breaks down the definition: (1) 'attribute' is the genus; (2) 'positive' excludes negative attributes like pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'), which are considered attributes despite being negations; (3) the clause 'indicates the essence itself' means it conveys no extra meaning beyond the essence; the phrase 'without extra meaning' confirms this. Then it excludes two other types: 'meaningful attributes' (sifaat al-ma'ani) which indicate an extra meaning separate from the essence (e.g., power, knowledge), and 'allusive attributes' (sifaat ma'nawiyya) which, although not directly extra, imply the existence of those meaningful attributes, thus also indicating an extra meaning indirectly.

Key Terms
صفة نفسية
صفة ثبوتية
صفة سلبية
صفة المعنى
صفة معنوية
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a self-attribute is just any attribute of the self (essence), including those that add extra qualities like power or knowledge.
Correction: A self-attribute specifically indicates only the essence itself without any extra meaning; other types of attributes (meaningful, allusive) add extra meanings.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is excluded from the definition of a self-attribute?
A) Positive attributes that indicate the essence alone
B) Negative attributes like pre-eternity
C) Existence attributed to God
D) All of the above are self-attributes

Key Takeaway
A self-attribute is a positive divine attribute that solely indicates God's essence without any added meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Introduction to the Five Negative Attributes: Al-Qidam
Introduces the five negative attributes and begins with al-qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the negative (salbiyya) attributes are and the meaning of the first one: al-qidam (pre-eternity).

Core Idea
Negative attributes protect God's perfection by negating what is unbefitting.

After establishing existence as the first necessary attribute, the text turns to the negative attributes. These attributes do not affirm something positive but rather negate imperfections. The first of these is pre-eternity, which negates that God has a beginning. This lesson introduces the concept and the first of the five.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the opening word 'رب' and the enumeration starting with '١'.
The passage is short but foundational for the entire section on negative attributes.
2
Understand the concept of negative attributes
The passage explains that negative attributes indicate the negation of what is not befitting of Allah. Contrast this with positive attributes like existence.
3
Focus on al-qidam
Al-qidam means pre-eternity, i.e., existence without beginning. It is an obligatory attribute for Allah. Ask yourself: Why is it negative? Because it negates having a beginning.
This attribute is coordinated with existence in the poem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رب. الصفاتُ السلبيَّةُ خَمْسٌ: ١ صفةً القِدَم ودليلها] قوله: (وَالقِدَم) أي: واجب له القدم، فهو معطوف على الوجود. وهذا شروع في الصفات السلبية؛ أي: التي دلَّتْ على سلب ما لا يليقُ به سبحانه.
Lord. The negative attributes are five: 1. This is the commencement of the negative attributes, i.e., those which indicate the negation of what is not befitting of Him, glory be to Him.

This passage begins the enumeration of the five negative (salbiyya) attributes of God. It states that there are five such attributes and then lists the first one: al-qidam (pre-eternity, i.e., existence without beginning). The text references the line of the poem 'And al-qidam' from the Jawharah al-Tawhid, explaining that it means pre-eternity is obligatory (wajib) for God, and it is coordinated with existence as an attribute. This marks the start of the section on negative attributes, which are defined as those that negate anything unbefitting of God. The passage serves as an introduction to the specific negative attributes that will follow in the subsequent lessons.

Negative Attributes and the Threefold Meaning of Qadim
Commentator explains negative attributes and distinguishes three kinds of qadim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the negative attributes are not limited to five and the three distinct meanings of qadim (eternal/preexistent) as applied to God, humans, and relative beings.

Core Idea
God's eternalness is essential, not measured by time.

This passage from the commentary clarifies two foundational points in Islamic theology: first, that the negative attributes of God are not restricted to the five usually listed, because all negations ultimately refer back to them; second, that the term 'qadim' has different meanings depending on context. Understanding these distinctions prevents confusion when reading theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of negative attributes
The passage begins by discussing the negative (salbiyya) attributes, noting that they are not limited to the five enumerated by the author. Realize that any negation (e.g., no partner, no need) reverts to these five by implication.
The five are considered the 'mothers' or principal negations.
2
Learn the definition of essential qadim
When applied to God, 'qadim' means essential qadim: having no beginning of existence. This is absolute preexistence without temporal measure.
Memorize the definition: 'non-initiation of existence'.
3
Distinguish temporal and relative qadim
For creatures, 'qadim' means temporal: a long duration (legally defined as one year). Relative qadim is priority like father over son. Both are impossible for God.
The example of the slave helps clarify temporal qadim in legal contexts.
4
Reflect on the threefold division
The commentator concludes that qadim has three divisions: essential, temporal, and relative. This categorization clarifies theological discussions.
Only essential qadim applies to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات (1) وتعالى، وليست منحصرة (۲) على الصحيح، وعد المصنف منها خمسة لأنَّ ما عداها من نفي الولد والصاحبة والمعين وغير ذلك مما لا نهاية له، راجع إليها ولو بالالتزام، فهي أمهاتها ؛ أي: أصولها المهمات منها . والمراد بالقدم في حقه تعالى : القدمُ الذاتي، وهو عدم افتتاح الوجود، وإن شئت قلت: هو عدمُ الأوَّليَّة للوجود ، وأما القدمُ في حقنا فالمراد به الزماني، وهو طول الزمان، وضُبط بسَنةٍ، حتى إذا قال: كلُّ من كان من عبيدي قديماً فهو حر، عتَقَ مَن الأب له عنده سَنةٌ، وهذا مستحيل في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ وكذلك القدم الإضافي؛ كقدم الأ[؟] بالنسبة للابن(۳)، فتحصل من هذا أن القدم ثلاثة أقسام: ذاتي وزماني، وإضافي.
The Divine Attributes. (1) And He is exalted, and they are not limited (2) according to the correct view. The author counted five of them because whatever else there is of negation of offspring, consort, helper, and other things without end, goes back to them even by implication; so they are their mothers, i.e., their important principles. What is meant by qadim (eternity/preexistence) with respect to Him, exalted, is essential qadim, which is non-initiation of existence; if you wish you say: it is non-precedence of existence. As for qadim with respect to us, what is meant is temporal, which is length of time, and it is defined by a year, such that if someone says: every one of my slaves who is qadim is free, it frees one who has served him for a year. And this is impossible with respect to Him, exalted. Likewise relative qadim; like the qadim of the father relative to the son (3). So from this it results that qadim is three divisions: essential, temporal, and relative.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage begins by discussing the negative (salbiyya) attributes of God, noting that they are not limited to the five usually enumerated, because any negation (e.g., no partner, no need) reverts to them by implication. The author's counting of five is for the most important ones. Then the commentator turns to the term 'qadim' (eternal/preexistent). He clarifies that when applied to God, it means essential qadim: having no beginning of existence. In contrast, for us, qadim means temporal: a long duration in time, legally defined as one year. He gives the example of a manumission conditional on a slave being 'qadim' – it applies to one who served a year. This temporal qadim is impossible for God. A third type, relative qadim, is explained as something like the father's priority over the son (even if both are created). Thus three kinds: essential, temporal, relative.

Key Terms
الصفات السلبية
القدم الذاتي
القدم الزماني
القدم الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think qadim simply means 'ancient' in time, applicable to God in a similar way as to old creatures.
Correction: For God, qadim means essential beginninglessness, not temporal duration. Temporal and relative qadim involve time or priority, which are impossible for the Eternal.

Checkpoint

B) Temporal qadim (زماني)
C) Relative qadim (إضافي)

Key Takeaway
The term 'qadim' is not univocal; for God it means essential beginninglessness, while for creatures it refers to temporal duration or relative priority.

Proof of Allah's Pre-Eternity (Qidam)
Proof of Allah's pre-eternity by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that Allah is pre-eternal, using the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
If Allah were not pre-eternal, an endless chain of creators would be needed, which is impossible.

This lesson presents a rational proof for Allah's attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). Using a reductio ad absurdum argument, Sanusi shows that denying pre-eternity leads to impossible consequences: either circularity or infinite regress. By understanding this proof, you will see why the intellect affirms that Allah must be eternal without beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how each step of the argument builds on the previous one.
2
Identify the logical structure
The proof follows a chain: if not pre-eternal → originated → needs originator → similarity → need for another → circularity or regress → impossible. Trace each link.
3
Understand key terms
Focus on the Arabic terms: hadith (originated), muhdith (originator), dawr (circularity), tasalsul (infinite regress). These are essential for the argument.
The commentary explains these terms in context.
4
Ask why the chain is impossible
Reflect on why circularity and infinite regress are deemed impossible in rational theology. This is a key principle in kalam.
This connects to the broader method of proving divine attributes.
5
Affirm the conclusion
The proof concludes that not being pre-eternal is impossible, so pre-eternity is established. Accept this as a rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليلُ القدم: أنه لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً ؛ إذ لا واسطة، ولو كان حادثاً لافتقر لمحدث، ولو افتقر لمحدث لافتقر محدِثُهُ إلى محدث؛ لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، فيلزم الدورُ أو التسلسل، وكلٌّ منهما محال، فما أدى إليه ـ وهو افتقاره لمحدث - محال ، فما أدَّى إليه - وهو كونُهُ حادثاً - محال، فما أدى إليه ـ وهو عدم كونِهِ قديماً - محالٌ ، وإذا استحال عدم كونه قديماً ، ثبت كونه قديماً، وهو المطلوب.
The proof of pre-eternity: that if He were not pre-eternal, He would be originated, for there is no intermediate; and if He were originated, He would need an originator; and if He needed an originator, then His originator would need an originator due to the similarity between them; so circularity or infinite regress would follow, and each of them is impossible. Thus, that which leads to it—i.e., His need of an originator—is impossible; and that which leads to it—i.e., His being originated—is impossible; and that which leads to it—i.e., His not being pre-eternal—is impossible. And if the negation of His pre-eternity is impossible, then His pre-eternity is established, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage contains a rational proof for the attribute of qidam (pre-eternity) for Allah. The proof proceeds by reductio ad absurdum: Assume He is not pre-eternal; then He must be originated (hadith), since there is no intermediate state. An originated being requires an originator (muhdith). If the originator were also originated, then due to similarity between them, that originator would need another originator, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both impossible. Therefore, the initial assumption that He is not pre-eternal is false, and His pre-eternity is established.

Key Terms
قدم
حادث
محدث
دور
تسلسل
مماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if Allah is pre-eternal, He could still have a beginning from nothing; but the proof shows that having a beginning implies need for an originator, which leads to impossibility.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that the concept of 'beginning' (origination) necessarily entails dependence on a cause. Since Allah is independent, He cannot have a beginning.

Checkpoint
What does the proof conclude if Allah were not pre-eternal?
A) He would be eternal.
B) He would be originated and need an originator.
C) He would be self-sufficient.
D) He would be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Allah's pre-eternity is rationally necessary because denying it leads to an impossible infinite chain of creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah were not pre-eternal, He would need a creator. Do you live as though Allah is the eternal, self-sufficient One, or do you sometimes act as if He is dependent on your worship? What would change in your life today if you truly internalized that He has no beginning and needs nothing?
?
The proof shows that an infinite regress of creators is impossible. Yet we often act as if our own lineage or achievements are the source of our identity. How does acknowledging that only Allah is uncreated and pre-eternal affect how you view your own worth?
?
The argument relies on the principle that like things have like properties. If you believe Allah is incomparable, why do you sometimes judge His actions by human standards? What is one area where you need to accept that His pre-eternity means His ways are beyond your measure?

Definition and Proof of Permanence (Baqa')
Definition of baqa' as absence of posteriority and proof via qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of baqa' as the absence of posteriority of existence and its rational proof derived from Allah's pre-eternity (qidam).

Core Idea
Baqa' means existence without end, proven by Allah's pre-eternity.

This lesson introduces the divine attribute of permanence (baqa'). Before we explore how Allah's existence continues forever, we first need a precise definition. The author defines baqa' not as mere endless duration, but as the absence of posteriority—that is, existence that does not come to an end. Then, he provides a crisp rational proof: because Allah is pre-eternal (qadim), non-existence cannot be possible for Him, so His existence is permanent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of baqa'
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Baqa' is defined as 'absence of posteriority of existence' or 'absence of termination of existence.' This is a precise definition that distinguishes it from mere continuation.
The tanwin in the phrase 'kadha baqa'' indicates that it is a great type of permanence, like existence and pre-eternity.
2
Examine the proof from qidam
The proof is a conditional: if non-existence were possible for Allah, then pre-eternity (qidam) would be impossible for Him. Since qidam is already established, non-existence is impossible, proving baqa'.
The author quotes an earlier statement from the poem: 'And everything upon which non-existence is possible, upon it pre-eternity is certainly impossible.'
3
Recognize the rational consensus
The passage concludes that rational people agree on this proposition. This highlights that the proof is based on universal reason, not solely on scripture.
The reference to 'al-'Ukari' is a commentary that elaborates on the major premise of this syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كذا بقاء) التنوين للتنويع والتعظيم؛ أي نوع من أنواع البقاء عظيم مثل المذكور من الوجود والقدم في الوجوب له تعالى، فاسمُ الإشارة عائد على المذكور من الوجودِ والقدم والجامع هو الوجوب له تعالى، والمراد به في حقه تعالى: عدمُ الآخرية للوجودِ، وإن شئتَ قلت : عدم اختتام الوجود. ودليلُ البقاء له تعالى: أنه لو جاز عليه العدم لاستحال عليه القدمُ، لما تقدَّمَ في كلام المصنف من قوله : وَكُلُّ مَا جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ عَلَيْهِ قَطْعاً يَسْتَجِيلُ القِدَمُ كيف وقد سبق قريباً وجوبُ القدم له تعالى؟! وكلُّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه، وقد اتفقت العقلاء على هذه القضية كما في العكاري على الكبرى.
His saying: (kadha baqa') the tanwin is for diversification and magnification; i.e., a type of permanence, great like the mentioned existence and pre-eternity in being necessary for Him, may He be exalted. So the demonstrative pronoun refers back to the mentioned existence and pre-eternity, and the common factor is necessity for Him, may He be exalted. What is meant by it with respect to Him, may He be exalted, is the absence of posteriority of existence; and if you wish, you may say: the absence of the termination of existence. And the proof of permanence for Him, may He be exalted, is that if non-existence were possible for Him, then pre-eternity would be impossible for Him, according to what preceded in the author's saying: 'And everything upon which non-existence is possible, upon it pre-eternity is certainly impossible.' How, while the necessity of pre-eternity for Him, may He be exalted, has just preceded?! And everything whose pre-eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible. And the rational people have agreed on this proposition, as in al-'Ukari on the major premise.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of baqa' (permanence) and provides its proof. The definition explains that baqa' means 'absence of posteriority' of existence, i.e., existence without end. The proof links baqa' to qidam: the author quotes a previous statement that if non-existence were possible for something, then pre-eternity is impossible for it. Since Allah's pre-eternity is already established, non-existence cannot be possible; hence, His existence is permanent. This is a rational proof agreed upon by rational thinkers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' is merely indefinite continuation that could end at some point.
Correction: Baqa' is defined as the 'absence of posteriority,' meaning existence without any possible end, not just indefinite continuation.

Checkpoint
How is baqa' defined in this passage?
A) Continual existence forever.
B) The absence of posteriority of existence.
C) The impossibility of non-existence.
D) Existence without beginning.

Key Takeaway
Baqa' is the attribute of permanent existence without end, rationally proven by Allah's pre-eternity (qidam).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage proves that non-existence is impossible for Allah. Does your daily life reflect a belief that Allah is always present and aware, or do you sometimes act as if He is absent or will not hold you accountable? Which specific habit would you change to live with more awareness of His permanence?
?
You have just read a logical argument for Allah's permanence. Are you convinced by it? If not, what doubt remains? If yes, how will this certainty change the way you trust in Allah's promises regarding the afterlife?

Objection on Permanence and Its Answer
Objection and two answers on pre-eternal non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians defend the principle that what is pre-eternal cannot cease against the objection based on our created existence.

Core Idea
The eternity rule applies to existents, not non-existents, and non-existence ends in time, not in eternity.

This passage presents an important objection to the proof that pre-eternal existence implies impossibility of cessation. The objector points to our own non-existence before creation, which was pre-eternal yet ended. The answers clarify that the rule applies only to pre-eternal existents, and that termination occurred in the future, not in pre-eternity itself. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the precise scope of theological arguments about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and locate the objection. Why does the objector think our pre-eternal non-existence challenges the rule that what is pre-eternal cannot cease?
The rule is roughly: 'If something has no beginning, it cannot cease.'
2
Examine the first answer (Ibn Dhakri)
Study Ibn Dhakri's answer: the rule applies only to pre-eternal existents, not to non-existents. Why does this distinction matter?
The proof for permanence (baqa') was established for existent beings, not for non-existents.
3
Examine the second answer (al-Fahri)
Analyze al-Fahri's refutation: our existence terminated our non-existence in the future (ma la yazal), not in pre-eternity itself. What does this imply?
If it had terminated in pre-eternity, we would have existed in pre-eternity, which is impossible.
4
Synthesize the main insight
Consider how both answers protect the original rule while accounting for creation. What is the key takeaway about theological reasoning?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأُوردَ عليه عدمُنا في الأزل؛ فإنَّه قديم، بناءً على القول بترادف القديم والأزلي، فهو كعدم المستحيل، فلمَ جازَ انقطاعه بوجودنا فيما لا يزالُ؟ أجيب بأن هذه القاعدة إنَّما هي في القديم الوجودي؛ إذ الدليلُ إنَّما قام فيه كما ذكره الإمام ابن ذكري، وقال الفهري : إنَّ الإيراد من أصله مدفوع بأن وجودنا قطع عدمنا فيما لا يزالُ لا في الأزلِ، وإلَّا لوجدنا في الأزل، وهو محال.
And an objection was raised against it: our non-existence in pre-eternity; for it is pre-eternal, based on the opinion that qadim and azali are synonymous, so it is like the non-existence of the impossible. So why is its termination by our existence in the future possible? The answer: that this rule applies only to the existent pre-eternal; for the proof was established only for it, as mentioned by Imam Ibn Dhakri. And al-Fahri said: The objection is refuted from its root because our existence terminated our non-existence in the future, not in pre-eternity; otherwise, we would exist in pre-eternity, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage records an objection to the proof that what is pre-eternal is impossible to cease. The objector points to our own non-existence in pre-eternity (before we were created). If pre-eternity implies impossibility of cessation, then our pre-eternal non-existence should never end, yet it ended when we were created. Two answers are given: First, Ibn Dhakri says the rule that what is pre-eternal is impossible to cease applies only to existent things (qadim wujudi), not to non-existents. The proof for baqa' was for existent beings. Second, al-Fahri says the objection is invalid because our existence did not end our non-existence in pre-eternity itself (which has no beginning), but only in the future (fi ma la yazal), which has a beginning. If it had ended in pre-eternity, we would have existed in pre-eternity, which is impossible.

Key Terms
عدم
أزل
قديم وجودي
ما لا يزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rule that what is pre-eternal cannot cease applies to everything pre-eternal, including non-existents.
Correction: The rule applies only to pre-eternal existents (qadim wujudi). Non-existence, though described as pre-eternal, is not an existent and therefore not subject to the same rule.

Checkpoint
According to al-Fahri, why is the objection invalid?
A) Because our non-existence was not truly pre-eternal.
B) Because our existence cut off our non-existence in the future, not in pre-eternity.
C) Because the rule applies only to Allah.
D) Because non-existence cannot be described as pre-eternal.

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternal existence implies impossibility of cessation only for existents, not for non-existents, and non-existence can end in the future without affecting its pre-eternal status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You existed once only after endless non-existence. Does that reality humble you when you feel entitled to pride or self-sufficiency? How can you make gratitude for your createdness a daily practice, rather than an occasional thought?
?
The objection treated your non-existence as a thing that could be 'pre-eternal'. Do you ever act as though your temporary existence is something you own, rather than a gift given at a specific moment? What would change if you truly lived knowing you are completely dependent on the Creator for every moment of your being?
?
Al-Fahri insisted that your non-existence ended in the future, not in pre-eternity. Are you living as though your existence is secure, forgetting that it could end at any moment? What would it mean to prepare for your own end, acknowledging that your existence is a loan, not a possession?

Objection to Baqa' Proof and Its Answers
Al-Bajuri presents and answers the objection that our pre-eternal non-existence is eternal yet ceases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the proof of baqa' and the key answers that protect the doctrine of eternal divine attributes by distinguishing existential from non-existential eternality.

Core Idea
Eternal non-existence is not permanent; only existential eternality implies permanence.

In this passage, al-Bajuri addresses a subtle objection to the proof of baqa': if our pre-eternal non-existence is eternal, why was it interrupted by our existence? The discussion clarifies that the rule 'every eternal is permanent' applies only to existential realities, not to non-existence. Al-Bajuri presents two answers and a further distinction to resolve the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection equates our pre-eternal non-existence with the non-existence of the impossible, arguing that both are eternal and should therefore be permanent. Notice how the objector uses the equivalence of 'qadim' and 'azali'.
The rule being attacked is: 'كل قديم فهو باق' (every eternal is permanent).
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer restricts the rule to existential eternality (al-qadim al-wujudi). Non-existence does not fall under the proof of baqa'. Identify the authority cited (Ibn Dhikri).
This answer preserves the original proof for divine attributes.
3
Examine the second answer and its critique
Al-Fahri argues the objection is invalid because our existence cuts off non-existence in the future, not in pre-eternity. Then note al-Yusi's agreement and al-Amir's counterpoint: interruption in the future still violates the rule if the non-existence is eternal.
Al-Bajuri sides with the first answer as the apparent one.
4
Study the final distinction
A further objection asks: what distinguishes our non-existence from that of the impossible? The answer: our non-existence's necessity is restricted to pre-eternity, while the impossible's non-existence is absolutely necessary. Thus our non-existence can be interrupted.
This distinction relies on the modal concepts of restricted vs. absolute necessity.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Trace the argument: objection → two answers → scholarly discussion → further objection → resolving distinction. This shows how kalam reasoning uses precise modal terms to defend doctrine.
The lesson reinforces that divine attributes are existential and their eternity is unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأُوردَ عليه عدمُنا في الأزل؛ فإنَّه قديم، بناءً على القول بترادف القديم والأزلي، فهو كعدم المستحيل، فلمَ جازَ انقطاعه بوجودنا فيما لا يزالُ؟ أجيب بأن هذه القاعدة إنَّما هي في القديم الوجودي؛ إذ الدليلُ إنَّما قام فيه كما ذكره الإمام ابن ذكري، وقال الفهري : إنَّ الإيراد من أصله مدفوع بأن وجودنا قطع عدمنا فيما لا يزالُ لا في الأزلِ، وإلَّا لوجدنا في الأزل، وهو محال. قال العلامة اليوسي : (وهو ظاهر)، لكن قال العلامة الأمير: (ولك أن تقول : لم يظهر ، لقولهم : كلُّ قديم فهو باق فانقطاع الاستمرار فيما لا يزال مضرٌّ ) فالظاهرُ الجوابُ الأوَّل (انتهى). لا يُقالُ: أيُّ فرق بين عدينا وعدم المستحيل كالشريك؟ فإن كلا منهما واجب في الأزلِ؛ لأنا نقول: وجوب عدمنا مقيَّد بالأزلِ، فهو ممكن فيما لا يزال، وأما عدمُ المستحيل فواجب على الإطلاق.
And it was objected against it that our non-existence in pre-eternity is eternal, based on the view that 'ancient' and 'pre-eternal' are synonymous. So it is like the non-existence of the impossible. Why then did its cessation become permissible by our existence in the future? It was answered that this rule is only concerning existential eternality, for the proof was established upon it, as mentioned by Imam Ibn Dhikri. And al-Fahri said: the objection is fundamentally repelled because our existence cuts off our non-existence in the future, not in pre-eternity; otherwise we would have existed in pre-eternity, which is impossible. Al-‘Allama al-Yusi said: 'That is apparent.' But al-‘Allama al-Amir said: 'But you may say: it is not apparent, because of their statement: every eternal is permanent, so the interruption of continuity in the future is harmful.' So the apparent [view] is the first answer. It is not said: What is the difference between our non-existence and the non-existence of the impossible like a partner? For each of them is necessary in pre-eternity. Because we say: the necessity of our non-existence is restricted to pre-eternity, so it is possible in the future. As for the non-existence of the impossible, it is absolutely necessary.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof of baqa' and supplies two answers. The objection is: Our non-existence in pre-eternity (al-azal) is also eternal (qadim) according to those who equate 'qadim' and 'azali'. So it should be permanent like the non-existence of an impossible thing. But it was interrupted by our existence in the future. Why? The first answer restricts the rule 'everything eternal is permanent' to existential eternality (al-qadim al-wujudi) only, because the original proof concerned existent attributes. The second answer, by al-Fahri, argues that the objection is invalid because our existence ends our non-existence in the future, not in pre-eternity, otherwise we would have existed in pre-eternity. Al-Yusi considers this answer clear, but al-Amir disagrees: he thinks the objection still stands because if every eternal is permanent then even pre-eternal non-existence should be permanent. However, al-Bajuri concludes that the first answer is the apparent one. Then a further objection arises: what distinguishes our pre-eternal non-existence from that of an impossible thing? Both are necessary in pre-eternity. The answer: our non-existence's necessity is restricted to pre-eternity, so it is possible in the future, whereas the non-existence of the impossible is absolutely necessary.

Key Terms
عدم أزلي
قديم
أزلي
مستحيل
واجب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the rule 'every eternal is permanent' applies to non-existential entities as well as existential ones.
Correction: The author clarifies that the rule applies only to existential realities (al-qadim al-wujudi). Non-existence is subject to a different logical modality.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer to the objection, why does the rule 'everything eternal is permanent' not apply to our pre-eternal non-existence?
A) Because it is not truly eternal
B) Because it is not an existential reality
C) Because it is not possible
D) Because it has a beginning

Key Takeaway
The eternity of non-existence does not entail permanence; only existential eternality is permanent, and the necessity of our non-existence is restricted to pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your non-existence before birth was only temporary, but you now exist. How often do you truly reflect on the fact that your existence is a gift from the Eternal? What will you do today to thank God for bringing you into existence and sustaining you?
?
The scholars debated whether the rule 'every eternal is permanent' applies to non-existence. When you face theological questions, do you humbly admit that your understanding is limited, or do you insist on your own opinion? How can you cultivate humility in seeking knowledge about God?
?
The impossibility of a partner with God is absolute and never changes. Are there any 'partners' in your life—like wealth, status, or desires—that you treat as if they are permanently necessary? How does this lesson help you recognize that only God's existence is absolutely necessary and permanent, and what will you do to realign your priorities?

Four Categories of Things Regarding Beginning and End
Al-Bajuri classifies realities into four categories based on having a beginning and/or an end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four categories of things based on their beginning and end as outlined by Al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Everything can be classified by whether it has a beginning and an end into four distinct categories.

Al-Bajuri presents a concise classification that builds on the earlier discussion of God's attribute of permanence (baqa'). This classification helps us distinguish between God, our pre-eternal non-existence, created things, and the eternal abodes of Paradise and Hell. Understanding these categories clarifies key Islamic theological concepts about eternity and temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how Al-Bajuri introduces the four cases.
Notice the use of 'la awwala lahu' and 'la akhira lahu'.
2
Identify each category
List the four categories mentioned. For each, note whether it has a beginning and/or an end as described in the text.
The categories are: God, our pre-eternal non-existence, created beings, and Paradise/Hell.
3
Understand the shar'an vs. 'aqlan distinction
Reflect on why the permanence of Paradise and Hell is by revelation (shar'an) and not by reason ('aqlan). Consider what reason alone would permit.
Reason allows for the possibility of their non-existence; revelation confirms their permanence.
4
Apply the classification
Think of other examples that fit each category. For instance, angels or jinn? Are they created beings? Why?
This reinforces the logic of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : عُلمَ ممَّا تقدَّم أن الله تعالى لا أوّل له ولا آخر وأن عدمنا في الأزل لا أوَّل له وله آخر وأمَّا المخلوقات فلها أول وآخر، ونعيم الجنة وعذاب النار له أول ولا آخر له، فكل منهما باق لكن شرعاً لا عقلاً لأنَّ العقل يجوزُ عدمَهُما ، فالأقسام أربعة .
A note: It is known from what preceded that God, may He be exalted, has no beginning and no end. And that our non-existence in pre-eternity has no beginning and has an end. As for created things, they have a beginning and an end. And the bliss of paradise and the torment of hell have a beginning and no end. So each of them is permanent, but by revelation, not by reason, because reason permits their non-existence. So the divisions are four.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a classification note (tanbih) derived from the preceding discussion of baqa'. The author identifies four categories of things with respect to having a beginning (awwal) and/or an end (akhir): (1) God has no beginning and no end; (2) our pre-eternal non-existence has no beginning but has an end (when we came to exist); (3) created beings have both a beginning and an end; (4) the bliss of Paradise and the torment of Hell have a beginning but no end. The author notes that the last two are permanent only by divine decree (shar'an), not by reason, because reason alone does not forbid their annihilation. Thus the complete set of divisions is four.

Key Terms
أول
آخر
أزلي
أبدي
شرعاً
عقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the permanence of Paradise and Hell is rationally self-evident and does not depend on revelation.
Correction: The text clarifies that while reason does not forbid their permanence, it also does not necessitate it; their everlastingness is established by revelation (shar'an) alone.

Checkpoint
Which of the four categories applies to the torment of Hell?
A) Has no beginning and no end
B) Has no beginning but has an end
C) Has a beginning and an end
D) Has a beginning but no end

Key Takeaway
Al-Bajuri teaches that everything can be categorized by whether it has a beginning and/or an end, and that the permanence of Paradise and Hell is by revelation, not reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your non-existence in pre-eternity had no beginning but ended when you were created. How does this truth affect your gratitude for the gift of existence right now?
?
The permanence of Paradise and Hell is by revelation, not reason. Is your life built on the certainty of God's promise, or are you letting your own reasoning waver about the afterlife?
?
God alone has no beginning and no end. Does your heart truly recognize His absolute uniqueness in this, or do you sometimes act as if something else is eternal alongside Him?

God's Existence Beyond Time
Definition and explanation of a divine attribute and time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means that God's existence is not mixed with non-existence and how time is defined as a created measure in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
God's existence is timeless; time is a created measure.

This lesson unpacks a key phrase from al-Jawharah: 'لا يشاب بالعدم' (not mixed with non-existence). The commentator clarifies that God's everlastingness is not temporal duration. He defines time as either the movement of the celestial sphere or the comparison of events—both created phenomena. Understanding this distinction guards against anthropomorphic conceptions of God's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the careful theological language.
2
Understand the meaning of 'لا يشاب بالعدم'
Focus on the first part: the phrase means non-existence does not mix with God. The author explains that mixing would require coexistence, which is impossible for God.
He cites Labid's poem to illustrate that everything except God is subject to nullity.
3
Distinguish God's existence from ours
The author says our existence is 'mixed with non-existence' because it is temporal—defined by duration over times. This is impossible for God.
4
Examine the definition of time
Time is defined in two ways: as the movement of the sphere, or as the comparison of an imagined event to a known event (e.g., 'I will come at sunrise'). Both definitions show time is created.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the implication is clear: time does not apply to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَا يُشَابُ بِالعَدَم أي : لا يخلط بالعدم، والمراد من ذلك : أنه لا يلحقه عدم؛ لأن حقيقة المخالطة تقتضي الاجتماع، والبقاء لا يجتمع مع العدم، إلَّا أن يقدر مضاف؛ أي: لا يُشابُ بجواز العدم، وهو معنى البطلان في قول لبيد : [من الطويل] أَلَا كُلُّ شَيْءٍ مَا خَلَا اللَّهَ بَاطِلُ وَكُلُّ نَعِيمِ لَا مَحَالَةَ زَائِلُ (٦) أي : من نعيم الدنيا كما يدلُّ عليه بقية القصيدة، فلا يرد عليه نعيم الجنان. واحترز المصنف بذلك من بقائنا؛ فإنه يُشابُ بالعدم ويخلط به؛ لأنه مقارنة استمرار الوجود زمانين فصاعداً، وهذا مستحيل في حقه تعالى؛ لأن الزمان حركة الفلك، أو مقارنة متجدّد (۳) موهوم لمتجدّد معلوم إزالة للإبهام) ، كما في قولك : (آتيك طلوع الشمس)، فالزمانُ هو مقارنة الإتيان المتجدّد الموهوم لطلوع الشمس المتجدّدِ المعلوم، وكلٌّ من حركة الفلك والمقارنة المذكورة حادث، ولا يقترنُ (٤)
His saying: 'is not mixed with non-existence' meaning: it is not intermixed with non-existence. The intent is that non-existence does not befall Him. Because the reality of mixing requires coexistence, and subsistence (baqa') does not coexist with non-existence. Unless it is considered with an added phrase, i.e., 'not mixed with the possibility of non-existence'. And this is the meaning of nullity in the verse of Labid: 'Indeed everything except Allah is false, and every bliss will inevitably vanish.' Meaning: from worldly bliss, as the rest of the poem indicates, so the bliss of Paradise is not included. The author (of the Jawhara) guarded thereby from our existence, for it is mixed and intermixed with non-existence, because it is the comparison of the continuation of existence for two times or more, and this is impossible with respect to Him, exalted, because time is the movement of the sphere, or the comparison of an imagined renewed event to a known renewed event to remove ambiguity, as in your statement: 'I will come to you at sunrise.' So time is the comparison of the imagined renewed coming to the known renewed sunrise. And each of the movement of the sphere and the mentioned comparison is originated (hadith), and it is not associated...
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation. The author interprets the phrase 'لا يشاب بالعدم' (not mixed with non-existence) as meaning that non-existence never overtakes God. He clarifies that mixing would require coexistence, impossible for God. The phrase is then understood as 'not mixed with the possibility of non-existence'. He cites Labid's poem to illustrate that everything except God is subject to nullity, but the poet meant worldly bliss, not the bliss of Paradise. Then he distinguishes our existence: it is temporal, defined by duration (comparison of continuation over two times). This temporal nature is impossible for God. He defines time in two ways: as the movement of the sphere (Aristotelian definition) or as the comparison of an imagined event (like 'coming') to a known event (like 'sunrise') to remove ambiguity. Both definitions show that time is created (حادث). The passage ends mid-sentence; the author was likely continuing that time does not apply to God.

Key Terms
يشاب
عدم
بقاء
زمان
حركة الفلك
مقارنة
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's everlastingness is a kind of temporal duration.
Correction: God's existence is timeless; time is a created measure of change, whereas God is unchanging.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is our existence said to be 'mixed with non-existence' while God's is not?
A) Because our existence is temporal and duration-based.
B) Because we are created from non-existence.
C) Because we can die.
D) Because we are contingent.

Key Takeaway
God's existence is absolutely timeless, while time itself is a created measure of change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is a created measure that does not apply to Allah, what does that mean for your anxieties about the future? How can you truly trust in the One who is beyond time, starting today with one specific worry you will hand over to Him?
?
Your existence is 'mixed with non-existence' because it is temporal. How does this awareness of your mortality change what you prioritize in your next 24 hours? What will you do today that has eternal value?
?
The author insists that God's subsistence is not even mixed with the possibility of non-existence. When you say 'Allahu Akbar', do you grasp that He is beyond all temporal limitations? What thought pattern do you need to break to deepen that conviction, and what action will you take to break it?

God's Dissimilarity to Contingent Things: Proof and Grammatical Defense
The commentator proves God's dissimilarity to contingent things from the poem line and explains its grammatical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator proves God's dissimilarity to contingent things from the poem line, and how he refutes a grammatical criticism of the author's wording.

Core Idea
God's eternity is the proof that He is unlike all that perishes.

The poem line from Jawharat al-Tawḥīd states that God is dissimilar to things that undergo non-existence, with eternity as the proof. The commentator unpacks the grammar of the line to defend its precision, addressing a critique that the wording is loose. This lesson walks through the proof and the grammatical rebuttal, showing how careful expression safeguards divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line and translation
Read the Arabic line and its literal translation to grasp the claim: God is unlike anything that can cease to exist, and the evidence is His eternity.
Notice that the proof is built directly into the line—eternity implies no beginning or end, so God cannot resemble temporal things.
2
Examine the grammatical analysis of 'anna'
The commentator explains that 'anna' (with fatha) has a pronoun referring to God and a predicate 'mukhālif'. He clarifies why the phrase 'limā yanālu al-‘adam' is fronted and how the whole clause functions syntactically.
This step shows how grammar supports theology: even the structure of the sentence emphasizes that dissimilarity is an essential attribute.
3
Understand the refutation of al-‘Adawī's criticism
Al-‘Adawī claimed the author's word choice was loose because the attribute should be the abstract noun 'mukhālafa' not the adjective 'mukhālif'. The commentator refutes this by citing the rule that 'anna' can be transformed into a verbal noun from its predicate, a standard usage in Arabic.
This defends the author's precision and shows that classical Arabic allows the adjective to carry the sense of the abstract.
4
Grasp the extension to eternal non-existences
The commentator adds that God is also dissimilar to eternal non-existences (al-a‘dām al-azaliyya), because non-existence is not something that can be ascribed to God. This follows from the attribute of existence.
This deepens the concept: God is not only unlike temporal things, but also unlike the state of non-existence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٤ - وَأَنَّهُ لِمَا يَنَالُ العَدَمُ مُخَالِفٌ بُرْهَانُ هَذَا القِدَمُ
قوله : (وَأَنَّهُ لِمَا يَنَالُ العَدَمُ، مُخَالفٌ ... إلخ)؛ أي: وواجب له أنه تعالى مخالف للحوادث التي يلحقها العدم، فهو بفتح الهمزة من (أنَّ) واسمها الضمير العائد عليه تعالي وخبرها (مخالف)، ويتعلق به الجار والمجرور قبله، وإنما قدمه لضرورة النظم، و(ما) واقعةً على الحوادث، وعائدها محذوف، و(أن) وما دخلت عليه في تأويل مصدر معطوف على الوجود، والتقديرُ (وواجب له تعالى مخالفته للحوادث التي يلحقها العدمُ)، وبذلك يندفع ما في حاشية الشيخ العدوي من أن في كلام المصنف تسمحاً ؛ لأن الصفة مخالفته، لا أنَّه ،مخالف، ووجه اندفاع ذلك أن القاعدة سبْكُ (أن) المفتوحة بمصدرٍ من خبرها، وهو شائع في العربية، فلا يُقال: فيه تسمح، وجَعْلُنا لذلك معطوفاً على الوجود أولى من جعله خبراً لمبتدأ محذوف، والتقدير (والصفة الثالثة من الصفات السلبية أنه ... إلى آخره وكلام الشيخ عبد السلام في هذا المقام حلُّ معنى لا حلُّ إعراب، وإن أوهمت عبارته خلاف ذلك . وإنما أسند المخالفة له تعالى لأنها تنزيه والموصوف به الله لا الحوادث، وكما أنه تعالى مخالف للحوادث مخالف للأعدام الأزليَّة كما عُلمَ من وصفه بالوجود ؛ إذ هي ليست موجودةً ؛ وقد ذكر الشيخ عبد السلام في هذا المقام أن الأعدام الأزلية
24 - And that He is dissimilar to that which non-existence reaches; the proof of this is eternity. His saying: 'And that He is dissimilar to that which non-existence reaches...' i.e., it is necessary for Him, the Exalted, that He is dissimilar to the contingent things that non-existence overtakes. So 'anna' is with fatha on the hamza, its pronoun refers back to Him, and its predicate is 'dissimilar'. The genitive phrase before it is attached to it, and it is only brought forward due to poetic necessity. 'Mā' refers to contingent things, and its pronoun is omitted. 'Anna' and what is included in it are in the form of a verbal noun coordinated with 'existence'. The paraphrase is: 'It is necessary for Him, the Exalted, His dissimilarity to the contingent things that non-existence overtakes.' By this, what is in the margin of al-Shaykh al-‘Adawī is refuted, namely that there is looseness in the author's speech because the attribute is 'His dissimilarity', not 'He is dissimilar'. The refutation is that the rule is to cast 'anna' with fatha into a verbal noun from its predicate, which is common in Arabic, so one should not say there is looseness. And making that coordinate with 'existence' is better than making it a predicate of a deleted subject, with the paraphrase: 'The third of the negative attributes is that...' etc. And the speech of al-Shaykh ‘Abd al-Salām on this point is a semantic explanation, not a syntactic one, even if his wording suggests otherwise. And the dissimilarity is only attributed to Him, the Exalted, because it is a glorification, and the described is God, not the contingent things. And just as He, the Exalted, is dissimilar to contingent things, He is dissimilar to eternal non-existences, as is known from His attribute of existence, since they are not existent. And al-Shaykh ‘Abd al-Salām mentioned on this point that eternal non-existences...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of poetry from Jawharat al-Tawḥīd: 'And that He is dissimilar to that which non-existence reaches; the proof of this is eternity.' The commentator explains that the line establishes a necessary attribute of God: His dissimilarity (mukhālafa) to contingent things (al-ḥawādith) which are subject to non-existence. He then provides a detailed grammatical analysis of the line. The word 'anna' is read with fatha on the hamza, and its pronoun refers to God, with the predicate 'mukhālif' (dissimilar). The phrase 'limā yanālu al-‘adam' (to that which non-existence reaches) is an attached genitive phrase. The commentator then addresses a criticism from al-Shaykh al-‘Adawī, who claimed that the author's phrasing was loose because the attribute should be 'mukhālafa' (dissimilarity) not 'mukhālif' (dissimilar). The commentator refutes this by citing the grammatical rule that the subordinating particle 'anna' with fatha is cast into a verbal noun derived from its predicate, which is standard in Arabic. Therefore, there is no looseness. The commentator prefers to treat the clause introduced by 'anna' as a grammatical subject coordinated with 'al-wujūd' (existence) rather than as a predicate of a deleted subject. The objection is thus refuted. Next, the commentator clarifies that the attribute of dissimilarity is attributed to God because it is a statement of His transcendence (tanzīh), and the described subject is God, not the contingent things. Finally, the commentator notes that just as God is dissimilar to contingent things, He is also dissimilar to eternal non-existences (al-a‘dām al-azaliyya), because non-existences are not existent. This is known from the attribute of existence. The passage ends by referencing al-Shaykh ‘Abd al-Salām's discussion on eternal non-existences.

Key Terms
مخالفة
الحوادث
العدم
القِدَم
تنزيه
الأعدام الأزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's phrasing 'He is dissimilar' is grammatically imprecise; the correct attribute is the abstract noun 'dissimilarity'.
Correction: Classical Arabic grammar allows the transformation of 'anna' plus its noun and predicate into a verbal noun, so 'He is dissimilar' thereby implies 'His dissimilarity'. There is no looseness.

Checkpoint
What is the proof of God's dissimilarity to contingent things according to the poem line?
A) God's mercy.
B) God's eternity (al-qidam).
C) God's power.
D) God's will.

Key Takeaway
God's eternity proves His absolute dissimilarity to all contingent things and even to the state of non-existence, and careful grammatical analysis defends the precision of theological expression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is utterly dissimilar to everything that perishes, what does that mean for how you view your own mortality? How does this truth reshape your attachment to worldly things? Will you let it change your priorities today?
?
This lesson refines our language about God. Are there any phrases you use to describe God that might be careless or imply comparison with created things? Will you examine your speech and correct it starting now?
?
Knowing that God is even dissimilar to non-existence itself, how does that expand your awe of His transcendence? How will you cultivate that awe—through silence, reflection, or worship—before you sleep tonight?
