The Attribute of Self-Subsistence and the Preposition 'Ba'
Commentator analyzes the grammar and preposition of the attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of God's self-subsistence (qiyamuhu bi nafsihi) and the correct interpretation of the preposition 'ba' that preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God subsists by Himself, not by any instrument or cause.

The commentator begins by clarifying the grammatical structure of 'qiyamuhu bi al-nafs' and then addresses the critical issue: what does the preposition 'ba' mean here? Several options exist—instrumental, transitive, causal, or spatial—but only some are theologically sound. This lesson walks through those interpretations and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the grammar and 'ba'
Read the first part of the passage (from 'His saying: (His self-subsistence)...' to '...something else with him'). Focus on the grammatical notes and the different interpretations of 'ba'.
Notice how the commentator first resolves the syntax, then moves to the preposition's meaning.
2
Identify the four interpretations of 'ba'
List the four interpretations mentioned: instrument (al-Shuktanī), transitivity (rejected), causality (preferred), and metaphorical spatiality (al-Mallawī). Understand why each is proposed or rejected.
The instrumental interpretation is considered disrespectful; the causal or spatial interpretations are safer.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider why this precision matters: God does not need an instrument or any external means. The attribute 'self-subsistence' affirms His independence from all creation.
This connects to the broader Islamic understanding of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤. صفة قيامه تعالى بالنفس ودليلها
٢٥ - قِيَامُهُ بِالنَّفْسِ وَحدَانية مُنَزَّهاً أَوْصَافُهُ سَنِيَّة قوله : (قِيَامُهُ بِالنَّفْسِ) معطوف على الوجود بحذف حرف العطف، والتقدير : وواجب قيامه بنفسه، فـ (أل) في النفس عوض عن المضاف إليه، وقول الشارح: (والصفة الرابعة من الصفات السلبية الواجبة له تعالى قيامه بالنفس) حل معنى لا حل
(1) =
إعراب كما تقدّم وقد جعل بعضهم الباء في قوله (بالنفس) باءَ الآلة، وأصله للشكتاني، وفيه إساءة أدب ؛ وقد تخلّص الشيخ يحيى الشاوي من إساءة الأدب بأن فائدة ذلك تظهرُ في المقابل؛ أي لا بغيره فالمعنى: أن الغير ليس آلةٌ في قيامه تعالى، فهو نظير ما سبق في وجوده لذاته لا لعلَّةٍ، ولكن الأولى أن الباء للسببيَّة؛ لأن الآلة واسطة الفعل، ولا تناسب هنا، كما لا يناسب جعلها للتعدية؛ لأن مجرور الباء التي للتعدية يكون مفعولاً به معنى؛ كـ ذَهَبَ اللهُ بِنُورِهِمْ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧] ، ولا كذلك ما هنا، وجعلها الشيخ الملوي بمعنى (في)، فهي للظرفية المجازية، فالمعنى: قيامه في نفسه ليس باعتبار شيءٍ آخر ؛ كما يقال : هذا العبد في نفسه يساوي كذا)؛ أي: لا باعتبار شيء
آخر معه .
والمراد من النفس هنا الذاتُ ؛ فإنها تطلق على الذات كما هنا، وتطلقُ على الدم كما في قولهم : ( ما لا نفسَ له سائلةٌ لا يُنجس الماء)، وعلى الأنفة كما في قولهم : (فلان) ذو نَفْسٍ)، وعلى العقوبة، قيل: منه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيُعَذِرُكُمُ اللهُ نَفْسَةُ) [آل عمران: ۲۸]؛ أي: عقوبته. والحقُّ أنه يجوز إطلاقُ النَّفْس على ذاته تعالى من غير مشاكلة؛ كما يدلُّ عليه قوله :تعالى: ﴿كَتَبَ رَبُّكُمْ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ الرَّحْمَةُ [الانعام: ٥٤، خلافاً لمن زعم أنها لا تطلقُ عليه تعالى إلا مشاكلةٌ ؛ كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِي وَلَا أَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِكَ
المائدة : ١١٦].
(۱) انظر حاشية العلامة الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) (٦٨) .
[4. The attribute of His, Exalted, self-subsistence (qiyāmuhu bi nafsihi) and its proof. 25 - His self-subsistence, oneness, free (of imperfections), His attributes sublime. His saying: (His self-subsistence) is coordinated to existence with the deletion of the conjunction, and the estimation: and His self-subsistence is necessary, so the definite article in 'al-nafs' stands in for the genitive complement, and the statement of the commentator: (And the fourth attribute among the necessary negative attributes for Him, Exalted, is His self-subsistence) is a resolution of meaning, not a resolution of parsing as previously mentioned. And some have made the 'ba' in His saying (bi al-nafs) as the 'ba' of instrument, and its origin is from al-Shuktanī, and in it there is disrespect; and al-Shaykh Yaḥyā al-Shāwī extricated from the disrespect by [saying] that the benefit of that appears in the opposite; i.e., not by another, so the meaning: that the other is not an instrument in His self-subsistence, and it is analogous to what preceded in His existence by Himself and not by a cause. But the better [view] is that the 'ba' is for causality; because the instrument is an intermediary for the action, and it is not suitable here, as it is not suitable to make it for transitivity; because the object of the 'ba' of transitivity is a direct object in meaning, like 'Allāh took away their light' (Quran 2:17), and that is not the case here. And al-Shaykh al-Mallawī made it with the meaning of 'in' (fī), so it is for metaphorical spatiality, so the meaning: His self-subsistence in Himself is not by consideration of something else, as it is said: 'This slave in himself is worth such-and-such'; i.e., not by consideration of something else with him. And what is meant by 'nafs' here is the self (dhāt); for it is used for the self as here, and it is used for blood as in their statement: 'That which has no flowing blood does not make water impure', and for pride as in their statement: 'So-and-so is a man of pride,' and for punishment: it is said that from it is His statement, Exalted: 'And Allah warns you of Himself (nafsahu)' (Quran 3:28); i.e., His punishment. And the truth is that it is permissible to apply 'nafs' to His self, Exalted, without figurative interpretation (mushākala), as is indicated by His statement, Exalted: 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself (nafsihi)' (Quran 6:54), contrary to the one who claims that it is not applied to Him except by figurative interpretation, as in His statement, Exalted: 'You know what is in my soul (nafsī) and I do not know what is in Your soul (nafsika)' (Quran 5:116). (1) See the footnote of al-'Allāma al-Amīr on 'Abd al-Salām (p. 68).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the fourth negative attribute of God: self-subsistence (qiyāmuhu bi nafsihi). The commentator first clarifies the grammatical structure: the phrase 'qiyāmuhu bi al-nafs' is conjoined to 'al-wujūd' (existence) from a previous line, with an omitted conjunction, so the meaning is 'and His self-subsistence is necessary.' The definite article in 'al-nafs' substitutes for the possessive pronoun. He then addresses the interpretation of the preposition 'ba' in 'bi al-nafs'. Some scholars, like al-Shuktanī, took it as the 'ba' of instrument, implying God uses His self as an instrument to subsist – but this is deemed disrespectful. Al-Shāwī mitigated this by saying the point is the opposite: not by another, so no instrumentality. The commentator prefers the 'ba' of causality, because an instrument is an intermediary for an action, inappropriate for God. He also rejects the 'ba' of transitivity. Al-Mallawī took it as metaphorical spatiality ('in'), meaning 'His subsistence in Himself,' not dependent on anything else. The commentator then explains the word 'nafs'. Here it means 'dhāt' (self). He lists other usages: blood, pride, punishment. He argues that applying 'nafs' to God is permissible literally, without figurative interpretation (mushākala), citing Quran 6:54 ('Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself') as evidence, and refutes those who claim it is only figurative based on Quran 5:116. The passage thus clarifies the meaning of this attribute through grammar, semantics, and theological precision.

Key Terms
قيام بالنفس
باء
الآلة
السببية
الظرفية المجازية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preposition 'ba' in 'bi nafsihi' is instrumental, meaning God uses His self as an instrument.
Correction: The instrumental interpretation is considered disrespectful. The 'ba' is better understood as causal or metaphorical spatial, affirming that God subsists by His essence, not by any instrument.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the interpretation of 'ba' as instrumental?
A) It would imply God is composed of parts
B) It implies God uses an instrument, which is disrespectful
C) It contradicts the Arabic grammar
D) It makes God dependent on creation

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence means He subsists by His own essence, not by any instrument or cause external to Him; the preposition 'ba' is best understood as causal or spatial, not instrumental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God's self-subsistence is not by any instrument. Is there any 'instrument' in your life—wealth, status, relationships, or even your own abilities—that you unconsciously rely on instead of relying on God's self-sufficiency? What would it mean for you to truly depend on God alone, without any intermediary?
?
The commentator rejects the instrumental interpretation because it implies disrespect. Do you ever think of God's attributes in a way that reduces His transcendence? Reflect on one attribute you often misunderstand or underestimate. What concrete step will you take to seek more accurate knowledge of that attribute?

The Term 'Nafs' in Reference to God
Commentator explains the term 'nafs' and defends its literal application to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the word 'nafs' when applied to God and why it can be used literally without figurative interpretation.

Core Idea
'Nafs' when used for God means His essence, and it is used literally in the Qur'an.

After clarifying the preposition, the commentator turns to the word 'nafs' itself. This word has several meanings in Arabic, but in the divine context it refers to God's essence. Some scholars claimed it is only used figuratively for God, but the commentator argues that it can be literal, citing Qur'anic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'nafs'
Read the second part of the passage (from 'And what is meant by 'nafs' here...' to the end). Identify the different meanings of 'nafs' and the commentator's main argument.
Focus on the distinction between literal and figurative (mushakala).
2
List the meanings of 'nafs'
The commentator gives four meanings: self (essence), blood, pride, and punishment. Understand each and note that only 'self' applies to God.
The Quranic verse about 'mercy upon Himself' uses 'nafs' literally for God's essence.
3
Evaluate the literal vs figurative debate
Some argue that 'nafs' for God is always figurative (mushakala), based on Quran 5:116. The commentator counters with Quran 6:54, showing literal usage is permissible.
The commentator sides with the literal interpretation, citing the verse as evidence.

(۱) انظر حاشية العلامة الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) (٦٨) .
[4. The attribute of His, Exalted, self-subsistence (qiyāmuhu bi nafsihi) and its proof. 25 - His self-subsistence, oneness, free (of imperfections), His attributes sublime. His saying: (His self-subsistence) is coordinated to existence with the deletion of the conjunction, and the estimation: and His self-subsistence is necessary, so the definite article in 'al-nafs' stands in for the genitive complement, and the statement of the commentator: (And the fourth attribute among the necessary negative attributes for Him, Exalted, is His self-subsistence) is a resolution of meaning, not a resolution of parsing as previously mentioned. And some have made the 'ba' in His saying (bi al-nafs) as the 'ba' of instrument, and its origin is from al-Shuktanī, and in it there is disrespect; and al-Shaykh Yaḥyā al-Shāwī extricated from the disrespect by [saying] that the benefit of that appears in the opposite; i.e., not by another, so the meaning: that the other is not an instrument in His self-subsistence, and it is analogous to what preceded in His existence by Himself and not by a cause. But the better [view] is that the 'ba' is for causality; because the instrument is an intermediary for the action, and it is not suitable here, as it is not suitable to make it for transitivity; because the object of the 'ba' of transitivity is a direct object in meaning, like 'Allāh took away their light' (Quran 2:17), and that is not the case here. And al-Shaykh al-Mallawī made it with the meaning of 'in' (fī), so it is for metaphorical spatiality, so the meaning: His self-subsistence in Himself is not by consideration of something else, as it is said: 'This slave in himself is worth such-and-such'; i.e., not by consideration of something else with him. And what is meant by 'nafs' here is the self (dhāt); for it is used for the self as here, and it is used for blood as in their statement: 'That which has no flowing blood does not make water impure', and for pride as in their statement: 'So-and-so is a man of pride,' and for punishment: it is said that from it is His statement, Exalted: 'And Allah warns you of Himself (nafsahu)' (Quran 3:28); i.e., His punishment. And the truth is that it is permissible to apply 'nafs' to His self, Exalted, without figurative interpretation (mushākala), as is indicated by His statement, Exalted: 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself (nafsihi)' (Quran 6:54), contrary to the one who claims that it is not applied to Him except by figurative interpretation, as in His statement, Exalted: 'You know what is in my soul (nafsī) and I do not know what is in Your soul (nafsika)' (Quran 5:116). (1) See the footnote of al-'Allāma al-Amīr on 'Abd al-Salām (p. 68).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the fourth negative attribute of God: self-subsistence (qiyāmuhu bi nafsihi). The commentator first clarifies the grammatical structure: the phrase 'qiyāmuhu bi al-nafs' is conjoined to 'al-wujūd' (existence) from a previous line, with an omitted conjunction, so the meaning is 'and His self-subsistence is necessary.' The definite article in 'al-nafs' substitutes for the possessive pronoun. He then addresses the interpretation of the preposition 'ba' in 'bi al-nafs'. Some scholars, like al-Shuktanī, took it as the 'ba' of instrument, implying God uses His self as an instrument to subsist – but this is deemed disrespectful. Al-Shāwī mitigated this by saying the point is the opposite: not by another, so no instrumentality. The commentator prefers the 'ba' of causality, because an instrument is an intermediary for an action, inappropriate for God. He also rejects the 'ba' of transitivity. Al-Mallawī took it as metaphorical spatiality ('in'), meaning 'His subsistence in Himself,' not dependent on anything else. The commentator then explains the word 'nafs'. Here it means 'dhāt' (self). He lists other usages: blood, pride, punishment. He argues that applying 'nafs' to God is permissible literally, without figurative interpretation (mushākala), citing Quran 6:54 ('Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself') as evidence, and refutes those who claim it is only figurative based on Quran 5:116. The passage thus clarifies the meaning of this attribute through grammar, semantics, and theological precision.

Key Terms
نفس
ذات
مشاكلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nafs' is never applied to God literally; it is always figurative (mushakala).
Correction: Quran 6:54 uses 'nafs' literally for God's essence ('upon Himself'). The commentator argues that literal usage is permissible and indicated by the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does the commentator use to argue that 'nafs' can be applied to God literally?
A) Quran 5:116
B) Quran 3:28
C) Quran 6:54
D) Quran 2:17

Key Takeaway
When applied to God, 'nafs' means His essence, and it is used literally in the Qur'an, not merely as a figure of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the words of the Qur'an about Allah's 'nafs', do you find yourself tempted to immediately reinterpret it as a figure of speech to avoid discomfort? What does that reveal about your relationship with the unseeable reality of your Lord? This week, when you encounter such verses, pause and ask: 'Do I trust that Allah means what He says, even if my mind cannot fully grasp it?'
?
The verse 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself' (Quran 6:54) declares that mercy is essential to who Allah is. Have you ever truly felt that mercy as a personal reality in your life, or do you sometimes doubt that it is meant for you? What would change in your daily struggles if you firmly believed that His mercy is not a mere concept but a binding promise upon His own self?

The Two Components of Self-Subsistence
Al-Bajuri defines self-subsistence and explains its two components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two components of the divine attribute of self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs) and why both are necessary for a complete definition.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence means Allah depends on neither a locus nor a creator.

Al-Bajuri gives a precise definition of qiyam bi al-nafs, breaking it down into two essential meanings. Before we can properly understand what it means for Allah to be self-subsistent, we must clear up potential confusions about the term 'locus' and why independence from a specifier is explicitly stated. This passage establishes that foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Bajuri uses a very condensed style here.
2
Identify the two components
The passage explicitly gives two components of qiyam bi al-nafs. Find each one and note how al-Bajuri defines it.
The first component involves the term 'locus' (al-mahall), which requires clarification.
3
Reflect on the necessity of the second component
Al-Bajuri notes that the second component (independence from a specifier) could be inferred from pre-eternity, but scholars still include it. Ask yourself why this is important.
Think about the danger of relying on implied meanings in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى قيامه بنفسه عدم افتقارِهِ تعالى إلى المحلِّ؛ أي: الذاتِ التي يقوم بها، لا بمعنى المكان؛ لأن ذلك عُلِمَ من المخالفة للحوادث، وقال الغنيمي: (ولا مانع من حمل المحلّ على معنييه هنا ) (۱) ، وعدم افتقاره تعالى إلى المخصص؛ أي: الموجد، وهذا الثاني وإن كان يُستغنى عنه بالقدم، لكن تقدَّمَ أن العلماء لا يكتفون في هذا الفنّ بدلالةِ الالتزام؛ لشدَّة خطر الجهل بالعقائد (۲) . فمعنى القيام بالنفس شيئان عدم افتقاره إلى المحل، وعدم افتقاره إلى المخصص.
And the meaning of His self-subsistence is His lack of need, exalted is He, for a locus; that is, the essence in which He subsists, not in the sense of place, because that is known from His being contrary to contingents. And al-Ghanimi said: 'There is no impediment to taking the locus in both of its meanings here.' And His lack of need, exalted is He, for a specifier; that is, the bringer into existence. And this second [meaning], even though it is dispensed with by [the attribute of] pre-eternity, yet it was mentioned earlier that the scholars do not content themselves in this art with the implication of necessity, because of the severity of the danger of ignorance about beliefs. So the meaning of self-subsistence is two things: lack of need for a locus, and lack of need for a specifier.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a definition. Al-Bajuri defines the divine attribute of qiyam bi al-nafs (self-subsistence). He first explains that 'qiyam bi al-nafs' means that Allah does not need a mahall (locus) in which to subsist. He clarifies that 'locus' here means the essence in which something subsists, not a physical location, because Allah's transcendence above spatiality is already known from His being contrary to contingents. He then notes a statement by al-Ghanimi allowing both meanings (essence and place) for 'locus' in this context. Second, qiyam bi al-nafs also means independence from a mukhasṣiṣ (specifier), i.e., a creator who brings into existence. Although this independence could be inferred from Allah's pre-eternity, scholars still mention it explicitly because they do not rely solely on implied meanings in theology due to the danger of ignorance. Thus, the definition resolves into two components: no need for a locus and no need for a specifier.

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
المحل
المخصص
الافتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'locus' here refers to a physical place or location.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'locus' in this context means the essence in which something subsists, not a spatial location. Allah's transcendence above spatiality is already established by His being unlike contingent things.

Checkpoint
What are the two components of qiyam bi al-nafs according to al-Bajuri?
A) Independence from locus and from time
B) Independence from locus and from specifier
C) Independence from specifier and from change
D) Independence from space and from matter

Key Takeaway
Self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs) means Allah is independent of both a locus of subsistence and a specifier who brings into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that Allah is self-subsistent, needing neither a locus nor a creator. Reflect: Do you ever act as if your existence depends on people, places, or circumstances rather than on Allah alone? Repent of that dependency and commit today to rely solely on Allah.
?
The scholars explicitly mention even a seemingly redundant point to avoid ignorance about beliefs. Are you thorough in learning your faith, or do you accept shortcuts? What aspect of your aqeedah have you left vague? Resolve to study it properly.
?
If Allah is independent of all loci, then every place is the same to Him. Does your prayer reflect that you are standing before the One who is not confined to any direction? Or do you still feel more spiritual in a masjid than in your own home? Challenge yourself to feel His presence always.

Proof That God Does Not Need a Locus
Al-Bajuri proves God's independence from a locus through reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Bajuri's reductio ad absurdum proof that Allah does not need a locus, and why the assumption leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If Allah needed a locus, He would be an accident; but accidents cannot have necessary attributes — contradiction.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri presents a formal proof by contradiction (burhān al-khalf). He begins by assuming the opposite of what we believe — that Allah needs a locus — and shows that this assumption leads to an impossibility. This method is a cornerstone of Islamic theology. Let's walk through the steps of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'صفة' (accident) and 'خلف' (contradiction).
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: assumption (needs locus) → consequence (would be accident) → further consequence (cannot have necessary attributes) → contradiction (since Allah does have those attributes) → conclusion (assumption false).
Each step is linked by 'لو' (if) and 'لـ' (then).
3
Reflect on the method of proof by contradiction
Understand why this method is persuasive: it shows that denying the truth leads to an impossibility, thus affirming the truth indirectly.
This method is used frequently in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على عدم افتقاره إلى المحلّ : أنه لو افتقر إلى محل لكان صفة، ولو كان صفة لم يتصف بصفات المعاني والمعنوية (۳) ، وهي واجبة القيام به تعالى للأدلة الدالة على ذلك، هذا خَلْفٌ بفتح الخاء؛ أي: يستحقُ أن يُرمى به خلف الظهر، أو بضمها ؛ أي : كذب وباطل، وإذا بطل ذلك بطل ما أدَّى إليه ؛ وهو كونه صفةٌ، فبطل ما أدَّى إليه أيضاً؛ وهو افتقاره إلى محل، وإذا بطل افتقاره إلى محل ثبت عدم افتقاره إلى محل ، وهو المطلوب. (٤) قوله : (وإذا بطل افتقاره إلى محل ثبت عدم افتقاره إلى محل ساقط من (ب) و (ط).
And the proof for His not needing a locus: that if He needed a locus, He would be an accident; and if He were an accident, He would not be qualified with attributes of meanings and moral attributes (3), and they are necessarily subsisting in Him, exalted, due to the proofs indicating that. This is a khalf (with the opening of the kha'), meaning: it is worthy of being thrown behind the back, or with its damma, meaning: false and invalid. And if that is invalidated, then what led to it is invalidated, namely His being an accident; so what led to that is also invalidated, namely His need for a locus. And if His need for a locus is invalidated, then His not needing a locus is established, and that is the required. (4) His statement: 'And if His need for a locus is invalidated, then His not needing a locus is established' is omitted from (B) and (T).
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents a formal proof by contradiction. Al-Bajuri argues that if Allah needed a locus (mahall), He would be an accident (ṣifa), because only accidents subsist in a locus. But accidents cannot possess the 'attributes of meanings and moral attributes' (ṣifāt al-maʿānī wa al-maʿnawiyya)—these are necessary attributes that must be established for Allah based on other proofs. Since it is impossible for an accident to have such attributes, the assumption leads to a contradiction (khalf). Therefore, the original assumption is false: Allah does not need a locus. The note at the end indicates a variant reading where the concluding clause is omitted in some manuscripts.

Key Terms
دليل
افتقار
محل
صفة
خلف
صفات المعاني والمعنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'accident' (ṣifa) in kalam refers to a physical accident like a car crash.
Correction: In kalam, 'accident' (ṣifa) means any entity that subsists in another (like whiteness in a wall), with no spatial connotation. Here it means a dependent attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence if Allah needed a locus?
A) He would be a substance
B) He would be an accident
C) He would be composite
D) He would be dependent on time

Key Takeaway
Allah does not need a locus because the contrary leads to an impossible contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own constant need for a physical body, time, and place, how does the reality that Allah needs none of these confront your tendency to measure God by your own limitations? Have you ever unconsciously imagined Allah as being 'somewhere' or having a body? What must you change in your thoughts of Him?
?
Al-Bajuri argues that if Allah needed a locus, He would be an accident unable to possess necessary attributes. Think of something you deeply depend on—health, wealth, or relationships. Does your dependence on that thing ever affect how you view Allah's perfection, making you doubt that He is completely independent? What would it cost you to fully accept that Allah is beyond all need and limitation?

Proof of Allah's Independence from a Specifier
Al-Bajuri proves Allah's independence from a specifier and discusses His attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot need a specifier (creator) and how His attributes are independent yet subsist in His essence.

Core Idea
If Allah needed a creator, He would be contingent—but He is eternal.

Al-Bajuri begins by proving that Allah does not need a mukhaṣṣiṣ (specifier). He argues that if Allah needed a creator, He would be originated (ḥādith), but it is already established that He is necessarily existent, pre-eternal, and everlasting. This lesson walks through that proof and the subsequent clarification about Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, contradiction, conclusion.
The word 'kayfa' expresses astonishment at the impossibility.
2
Understand the tanbih (reminder)
Al-Bajuri reminds us that Allah is independent of both a locus and a specifier. His attributes are also independent of a specifier but subsist in His essence.
Note the careful language: we do not say attributes 'need' the essence to avoid misapprehension.
3
Reflect on the criticism of al-Fakhr
Al-Bajuri criticizes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for using the words 'indigence' and 'need' for Allah's attributes. Think about why such language is inappropriate.
This shows the importance of precise theological language.
4
Ask: why does this matter?
This proof establishes tawḥīd al-ṣifāt (unity of attributes) and refutes any idea that Allah's attributes are separate entities needing a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على عدم افتقاره إلى المخصص: أنه لو افتقر إلى مخصص لكان حادثاً، كيف وقد سبق وجوب وجوده وقدمه وبقائه ذاتاً وصفات؟! تنبيه : عُلمَ من ذلك أنه تعالى مُستغن عن المحل والمخصص معاً، وأما صفاته مستغنيةٌ عن المخصص وقائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى ولا يعبر فيها بالافتقار إلى الذات؛ لما فيه من الإيهام، وقد أساء الفخرُ الأدب حيث أطلق لفظ الافتقار والاحتياج فيها، وذوات الحوادث مفتقرة إلى المخصص مستغنيةٌ عن الذات التي تقوم بها، وصفاتُ الحوادث مفتقرةٌ إليهما معاً، فالأقسام أربعة، فتدبَّر.
And the proof for His not needing a specifier: that if He needed a specifier, He would be originated, despite already [knowing] the necessity of His existence, pre-eternity, and permanence in essence and attributes?! A note: From that it is known that He, exalted, is free of need for both locus and specifier together. As for His attributes, they are free of need for a specifier and subsist in His essence, and one does not express [for them] 'need for the essence' because of the [resulting] misapprehension. And al-Fakhr behaved impolitely when he used the words 'indigence' and 'need' regarding them. And the essences of contingents are in need of a specifier but free of need for the essence in which they subsist; and the attributes of contingents are in need of both. So the divisions are four, so reflect.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage contains a second proof and a concluding classification. Al-Bajuri proves that Allah does not need a mukhaṣṣiṣ (specifier or creator): if He did, He would be originated (ḥādith), but it is already established that He is necessarily existent, pre-eternal, and everlasting in essence and attributes—so the assumption is false. Then follows a 'tanbīh' (reminder) summarizing the implications: Allah is independent of both locus and specifier. His attributes are independent of a specifier and subsist in His essence, but we avoid saying they 'need' the essence because that might imply incompleteness. Al-Bajuri criticizes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for using such language. Finally, he outlines four divisions of existence regarding need: (1) Allah: independent of both; (2) divine attributes: independent of specifier but subsisting in essence (though not described as 'needing' the essence); (3) essences of contingents: need a specifier but not a locus (they subsist in themselves, but need a creator); (4) attributes of contingents: need both a specifier and a locus. This classification is a key summary for understanding the ontology of kalam.

Key Terms
مخصص
حادث
قدم
بقاء
ذات
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Allah's attributes are separate entities that need a creator or specifier.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that divine attributes are independent of a specifier and subsist in His essence; they are not created or in need.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence if Allah needed a specifier?
A) He would be pre-eternal
B) He would be originated (ḥādith)
C) He would be independent
D) He would be a locus

Key Takeaway
Allah and His attributes are independent of any external specifier or creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you instinctively look for a 'specifier' in creation to solve it, or do you truly believe Allah is independent of all means? How can you shift your reliance today?
?
Al-Bajuri rebukes al-Fakhr for careless speech about Allah. Think of the last time you described God casually. Did your words maintain adab, or did they imply a limitation? What will you do to watch your tongue?
?
If Allah does not need any external cause, why do you often act as if your happiness depends on created things? What would it mean for you to live in light of His absolute independence starting now?

The Fourfold Division of Need and Independence
Al-Bajuri classifies all entities into four divisions of need and independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of entities regarding their need for a specifier and a locus, as outlined by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Everything falls into one of four categories of dependence and independence.

After establishing Allah's independence, al-Bajuri presents a comprehensive classification of all existing things based on their relationship to a specifier (creator) and a locus (substrate). This fourfold division is a foundational taxonomy in Ash'ari ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the four categories are listed concisely.
The word 'fa-tadabbar' (so reflect) invites deep contemplation.
2
Identify each category
List the four: (1) Allah, (2) His attributes, (3) essences of contingents, (4) attributes of contingents. Determine for each whether they need a specifier and/or a locus.
Use the text: 'muftaqira' (needy) and 'mustaghniya' (independent).
3
Compare categories
Notice how the divine realm (Allah and His attributes) differs from the contingent realm (essences and attributes).
This classification reinforces tawḥīd and the dependence of everything on Allah.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Al-Bajuri ends with 'fa-tadabbar' (so reflect). Think about why he encourages reflection on this classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على عدم افتقاره إلى المخصص: أنه لو افتقر إلى مخصص لكان حادثاً، كيف وقد سبق وجوب وجوده وقدمه وبقائه ذاتاً وصفات؟! تنبيه : عُلمَ من ذلك أنه تعالى مُستغن عن المحل والمخصص معاً، وأما صفاته مستغنيةٌ عن المخصص وقائمة بذاتِهِ تعالى ولا يعبر فيها بالافتقار إلى الذات؛ لما فيه من الإيهام، وقد أساء الفخرُ الأدب حيث أطلق لفظ الافتقار والاحتياج فيها، وذوات الحوادث مفتقرة إلى المخصص مستغنيةٌ عن الذات التي تقوم بها، وصفاتُ الحوادث مفتقرةٌ إليهما معاً، فالأقسام أربعة، فتدبَّر.
And the proof for His not needing a specifier: that if He needed a specifier, He would be originated, despite already [knowing] the necessity of His existence, pre-eternity, and permanence in essence and attributes?! A note: From that it is known that He, exalted, is free of need for both locus and specifier together. As for His attributes, they are free of need for a specifier and subsist in His essence, and one does not express [for them] 'need for the essence' because of the [resulting] misapprehension. And al-Fakhr behaved impolitely when he used the words 'indigence' and 'need' regarding them. And the essences of contingents are in need of a specifier but free of need for the essence in which they subsist; and the attributes of contingents are in need of both. So the divisions are four, so reflect.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage contains a second proof and a concluding classification. Al-Bajuri proves that Allah does not need a mukhaṣṣiṣ (specifier or creator): if He did, He would be originated (ḥādith), but it is already established that He is necessarily existent, pre-eternal, and everlasting in essence and attributes—so the assumption is false. Then follows a 'tanbīh' (reminder) summarizing the implications: Allah is independent of both locus and specifier. His attributes are independent of a specifier and subsist in His essence, but we avoid saying they 'need' the essence because that might imply incompleteness. This classification is a key summary for understanding the ontology of kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entire existence is dependent on a specifier, why do you still act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that you are in need of Allah in every moment for your very essence?
?
Contingent attributes need both a specifier and a locus to subsist. Your own qualities—your knowledge, strength, life—are they any different? When did you last acknowledge that your very best traits are lent to you by Allah and vanish without His constant preservation?
?
The passage ends with 'fa-tadabbar'—'so reflect.' What is one specific area of your life where you have been acting as if you are independent of Allah, and what concrete step can you take today to acknowledge your need for Him there?

The Divine Attribute of Oneness: Linguistic Form and Significance
Al-Bajuri analyzes 'wahdaniyya' and extols tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the term 'wahdāniyya' and why the science of tawḥīd is named after it.

Core Idea
Wahdāniyya is the linguistic and theological pinnacle of tawḥīd.

Al-Bajuri begins by unpacking the grammatical structure of 'wahdāniyya', showing how its very form emphasizes unity. He then elevates the discussion by declaring that the science of tawḥīd derives its name from this attribute, indicating its supreme importance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get a feel for the content.
Notice the technical terms used.
2
Understand the grammatical analysis
Focus on how 'wahdāniyya' is derived: yā' for attribution and alif-nūn for hyperbole, as in 'raqabāni' and 'shaʿrāni'.
This shows the form intensifies the meaning.
3
Examine the disagreement
Yaḥyā al-Shāwī objects that the yā' cannot be for attribution because what is affirmed is unity itself, not something attributed to unity. The earlier scholars reply by saying a thing can be attributed to itself for emphasis.
Compare both views.
4
Appreciate the significance of tawḥīd
Al-Bajuri states that the topic of oneness is the noblest in this art, and the science is named after it. Reflect on why this attribute is so central.
Consider the Quranic evidence.
5
Reflect on the Quranic emphasis
Read the quoted verse (2:163) and think about how the Quran repeatedly stresses tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَحْدَانِيَّة) معطوف على (الوجود) بحذف حرف العطف ؛ أي: وواجب له وحدانية، وما ذكره الشارح حل معنى لا حل إعراب كما تقدم، وهي بفتح الواو نسبة إلى الوحدة ) ، فياؤُها للنسب والألف والنون للمبالغة؛ كما في (رقباني) نسبة للرَّقبة، و(شعراني) نسبة للشعر، وقال يحيى الشاوي : (لا يصح كون الياء للنسب؛ إذ المراد ثبوت الوحدة نفسها، لا ثبوت شيء منسوب إليها)، واختار جعلَها للمصدر ؛ كما في الضاربيَّة، وأجاب الأوَّلون بأن الشيء ينسب لنفسه مبالغة (٢) ومبحث الوحدانية أشرفُ مباحث هذا الفن، ولذلك سُمِّي باسم مشتق منها، فقيل : (علم التوحيد)، ولعظم العناية به كثُرَ التنبيه والثناء عليه في الآي القرآنية؛ فقال تعالى: ﴿وَإِلَهُكُمْ إِلَهُ وَبِدٌ لا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الرَّحْمَنُ الرَّحِيمُ ) [البقرة: ١٦٣) ... إلى غير ذلك من الآيات (۳) (۳).
His saying: '(Wahdāniyya)' is conjoined to 'al-wujūd' with the deletion of the conjunction; i.e., 'and necessary for Him is wahdāniyya.' And what the commentator mentioned is a solution of meaning, not a solution of parsing, as previously. It is with the opening of the wāw as a derivation from unity, its yā' is for derivation and the alif and nūn for hyperbole, as in 'raqabāni' derived from 'raqaba', and 'shaʿrāni' derived from 'shaʿr'. And Yaḥyā al-Shāwī said: 'It is not correct for the yā' to be for derivation, because what is intended is the affirmation of unity itself, not the affirmation of something attributed to it,' and he chose to make it for the infinitive, as in al-ḍārbiyya. And the former ones replied that a thing can be attributed to itself hyperbolically. And the topic of oneness is the noblest of the topics of this art, and for that reason it is named with a name derived from it, so it is said: 'the science of tawḥīd.' And because of the great care given to it, admonition and praise of it are abundant in the Quranic verses: He says: 'And your God is one God; there is no god but Him, the Beneficent, the Merciful' [2:163] ... and other such verses.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine attribute of wahdāniyya (oneness). Al-Bajuri first explains the grammatical analysis: the word 'wahdāniyya' is conjoined to 'al-wujūd' in the text of the Jawhara. He clarifies that the commentator (al-Laqani) is explaining meaning, not syntax. The word is formed by adding the relative yā' and the letters alif and nūn to 'waḥda' for hyperbole, like 'raqabāni' and 'shaʿrāni'. He then reports a disagreement: Yaḥyā al-Shāwī objects that the yā' is not for relative attribution because the intended meaning is the existence of unity itself, not something attributed to it; he prefers to treat the form as a verbal noun like 'ḍārbiyya'. The earlier scholars respond that a thing can be attributed to itself for emphasis. Al-Bajuri then extols the topic: it is the noblest subject in kalam, and the science itself is named after it (ʿilm al-tawḥīd). The Quran abounds in verses affirming God's oneness, such as 2:163. This lesson highlights both linguistic nuance and theological importance.

Key Terms
وحدانية
توحيد
نسبة
مبالغة
علم التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The yā' in 'wahdāniyya' indicates attribution to unity, meaning the attribute is something separate from the essence.
Correction: The yā' can be for hyperbole, attributing unity to itself for emphasis, as per the first opinion. The attribute is not a separate entity but a way of emphasizing unity.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bajuri, what do the alif and nūn at the end of 'wahdāniyya' indicate?
A) The feminine gender
B) Hyperbole (mubālagha)
C) The dual number
D) The accusative case

Key Takeaway
The form of the word 'wahdāniyya' itself emphasizes unity through hyperbole, and this attribute gives its name to the entire science of tawḥīd.

The Five Quantities Negated by Divine Oneness
Enumerates five types of quantity negated by divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of quantity that are negated by the comprehensive divine oneness, including how they apply to essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Divine oneness rules out both parts and rivals in essence and attributes.

In this passage, the author clarifies what 'oneness' truly negates. It's not just that God has no partner, but also that His essence is not composed of parts and His attributes are not multiple in kind or shared by others. The five quantities cover both continuous and discrete aspects of essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the overall structure: a claim of five categories followed by explanations.
Pay attention to the objection and answer in the middle.
2
Identify quantities negated by oneness of essence
The first two quantities are continuous quantity (composition from parts) and discrete quantity (multiplicity of gods). Both are negated by oneness of essence.
3
Understand the objection and answer about attributes
The passage raises an objection: continuous quantity usually applies to physical parts, so how can it apply to attributes? The answer is that attributes are treated as analogous to composite because they subsist in one essence.
This shows the subtlety of kalam reasoning.
4
Identify quantities negated by oneness of attributes
The remaining two quantities are continuous quantity (multiple attributes of the same kind) and discrete quantity (another having similar attributes to God). These are negated by oneness of attributes.
Examples include another having power like God's.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these negations matter for tawhid. They guard against both polytheism and anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن الوحدانيَّة الشاملة لوحدانية الذات ووحدانية الصفات ووحدانية الأفعال تنفي كموماً خمسة: الكمُّ المتصل في الذات وهو تركبها من أجزاء. والكم المنفصل فيها : وهو تعدُّدها بحيث يكون هناك إله ثانٍ فأكثر، وهذان الكمان منفيَّان بوحدة ذات. والكمُّ المتصل في الصفات وهو التعدد في صفاته تعالى من جنس واحد؛ كقدرتين فأكثر، ويُحثَ في هذا بأن الكم المتصل مداره على شيء ذي أجزاء، ولا كذلك الصفاتُ ! ويُجاب بأنهم نزَّلوا كونَها قائمة بذاتٍ واحدة منزلة التركب. والكم المنفصل في الصفات : وهو أن يكون لغير الله صفةٌ تشبه صفته تعالى؛ كأن يكون لزيد قدرة يوجد بها ويعدمُ بها كقدرته تعالى أو إرادة تخصص الشيء ببعض الممكنات، أو علم محيط بجميع الأشياء، وهذان الكمَّانِ منفيان بوحدانية الصفات.
The summary is that the comprehensive oneness, which includes oneness of essence, oneness of attributes, and oneness of actions, negates five quantities: (1) Continuous quantity in essence, which is its composition from parts. (2) Discrete quantity in essence, which is its multiplicity such that there is a second or more gods. These two quantities are negated by oneness of essence. (3) Continuous quantity in attributes, which is multiplicity in His attributes of the same kind, such as two or more powers. An objection is raised that continuous quantity pertains to something having parts, but attributes are not like that. The answer is that they treat the fact that attributes subsist in one essence as analogous to composition. (4) Discrete quantity in attributes, which is that someone other than God has an attribute resembling His attribute, such as Zayd having a power by which he brings into existence and annihilates like God's power, or an will that specifies a thing among some possibles, or a knowledge encompassing all things. These two quantities are negated by oneness of attributes.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the five types of 'kamm' (quantity) that are negated by the comprehensive divine oneness. It begins by stating the summary: the all-encompassing oneness—comprising oneness of essence, attributes, and actions—negates five quantities. The first two are related to essence: continuous quantity (composition from parts) and discrete quantity (multiplicity of gods). These are negated by oneness of essence. The next two are related to attributes: continuous quantity (multiplicity within the same kind, e.g., two powers) and discrete quantity (another being having an attribute similar to God's). The passage addresses an objection about how continuous quantity can apply to attributes, since attributes are not composed of parts; the answer is that attributes are treated as analogous to composite because they subsist in one essence. Finally, the discrete quantity in attributes is explained with examples: another possessing power, will, or knowledge like God's. Both attribute quantities are negated by oneness of attributes.

Key Terms
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل
وحدانية الذات
وحدانية الصفات
التركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Continuous quantity in attributes implies attributes have physical parts.
Correction: The passage clarifies that continuous quantity is applied by analogy because attributes subsist in one essence, not because they are composed.

Checkpoint
How many quantities are negated by comprehensive oneness according to this passage?
A) Three
B) Five
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Divine oneness negates all forms of composition, multiplicity, and similarity in essence and attributes.

?

The Grammatical Analysis of 'Munazzahan'
Grammatical analysis of 'munazzahan' as ḥāl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical function of 'munazzahan' as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) and the types of ḥāl it represents.

Core Idea
‘Munazzahan’ is a grammatical key to understanding divine transcendence in the sentence.

In this passage, the author explains the role of 'munazzahan' within the sentence structure. By identifying it as a circumstantial accusative, he shows how the attributes are affirmed while maintaining transcendence. We’ll explore the types of ḥāl mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read up to 'mutadākhila' to grasp the grammatical analysis.
Focus on the classification of ḥāl types.
2
Identify the three types of ḥāl
Find the terms lāzima, mu'akkida, mutarādif, mutadākhila and understand their meanings.
3
Understand the theological significance
Why does the author emphasize that the ḥāl is permanent? How does it affirm transcendence?
Transcendence is not a separate attribute but a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (منزَّهاً) حال من الضمير في قوله : ( فواجب) له ... إلى آخره)، فالمعنى: أنه تعالى وجبَتْ له هذه الصفاتُ حال كونه منزّهاً، فهي حال لازمةٌ ؛ مثل: دعوت الله سميعاً، وهي مؤكّدةً للصفات السابقة، وكذلك جملة قوله: (أوصافه سنيه) فهي حال أيضاً من الضمير المذكور، فهي حال مترادفة، ويجوز أن تكون حالاً من الضمير في (منزّهاً) فهي حال متداخلةٌ . (۲) ومعنى قوله : (سنيَّه) أنها تشبه السَّنا - بالقصر - وهو النور، بجامع الاهتداء، فيُهتدى بها ؛ أي: بأثرها ؛ لأنه المشاهد لنا، كما يهتدى بالسَّنا الذي هو النور، فالنسبة على وجه التشبيه وليس المراد أنه قام بها السنا وهو النورُ ؛ لأن النور عرض يستحيل قيامه بالصفة، أو معناه : رفيعةٌ، فيكون لفظ (سنيه) مأخوذاً من السناء - بالمد يمعنى الرفعة، والمراد الرفعة المعنوية.
His statement: 'munazzahan' (exalted) is a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) from the pronoun in his statement: 'fawājibun lahu... to the end.' So the meaning is: He, exalted, has these attributes necessary for Him while He is transcendent, so it is a permanent circumstantial accusative, like: 'I called upon Allah, hearing.' And it emphasizes the previous attributes. And likewise, the sentence 'awṣāfuhu saniyyah' (his attributes are luminous) is also a circumstantial accusative from the mentioned pronoun, so it is a concurrent circumstantial accusative. And it is permissible that it is a circumstantial accusative from the pronoun in 'munazzahan', so it is an overlapping circumstantial accusative. (2) And the meaning of his statement 'saniyyah' is that they resemble al-sanā (with shortening) which is light, due to the common factor of guidance, so one is guided by them, i.e., by their effect, for it is what we observe, just as one is guided by al-sanā which is light. So the relation is one of comparison; it is not meant that the attribute itself is al-sanā which is light, because light is an accident (ʿaraḍ) and it is impossible for it to subsist in an attribute. Or its meaning is 'lofty' (rafīʿah), so the word 'saniyyah' is derived from al-sanāʾ (with lengthening) meaning loftiness, and what is intended is the lofty station.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical and theological explanation of two terms from the poem: 'munazzahan' (exalted) and 'saniyyah' (luminous). The author describes 'munazzahan' as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) that is permanent (lāzima), emphasizing the attributes. He then explains that 'saniyyah' is also a circumstantial accusative, either concurrent or overlapping. Theologically, he clarifies that 'saniyyah' does not mean the attributes are literally light (an accident), but that they resemble light metaphorically in that they guide us to Allah. Alternatively, it may mean 'lofty' (rafīʿah) from al-sanāʾ (height). The point is to avoid anthropomorphism and uphold transcendence.

Key Terms
منزهاً
حال
لازمة
مؤكدة
مترادفة
متداخلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'munazzahan' is a separate attribute rather than a grammatical condition.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is a circumstantial accusative that emphasizes the attributes, not a separate attribute.

Checkpoint
What grammatical term describes 'munazzahan' in the passage?
A) Subject
B) Object
C) Circumstantial accusative
D) Adjective

Key Takeaway
‘Munazzahan’ serves as a circumstantial accusative that emphasizes the necessary attributes while affirming transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Meaning of 'Saniyyah': Metaphorical Light or Loftiness?
Explanation of the term 'saniyyah' and its two meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible interpretations of 'saniyyah' and why the literal meaning of light is rejected.

Core Idea
‘Saniyyah’ means attributes that guide like light, not that they are made of light.

The poem describes Allah's attributes as 'saniyyah'. The author offers two interpretations: metaphorical guidance from light, or loftiness. He argues that light cannot be literal because it’s an accident. We’ll see how this protects divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from '(2) ومعنى قوله' to the end to understand both interpretations.
Note the two possible derivations of 'saniyyah'.
2
Understand the metaphorical comparison
The attributes are compared to light because both guide; one sees their effects.
The common factor is guidance (al-ih tidā').
3
Note the rejection of literal light
Light is an accident (ʿaraḍ) and cannot subsist in an attribute.
This is a theological argument to avoid anthropomorphism.
4
Consider the alternative meaning of lofty
If derived from al-sanāʾ (with lengthening), it means lofty station.
This interpretation also preserves transcendence.

Introduction to the Attributes of Meanings: Power and Will
Introduces attributes of meanings, explains iḍāfa, defines 'maʿānī'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the 'attributes of meanings' are, how they relate to the essential and negative attributes, and why they are foundational for the moral attributes.

Core Idea
The attributes of meanings are the foundation from which all other divine attributes derive.

After establishing the essential attribute and the negative attributes, the text now turns to the seven attributes of meanings. These are not just names; they are real meanings that exist in the Divine Essence. Understanding this classification and the grammatical nuance of 'ṣifāt al-maʿānī' is key to grasping the later discussion of moral attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's placement of this section after essential and negative attributes.
2
Understand the iḍāfa construction
Identify the phrase 'ṣifāt al-maʿānī' and its two possible interpretations: explanatory (li-l-bayān) or partitive (min). Note the scholars who support each view.
The passage explicitly states that the partitive interpretation is valid.
3
Note the disagreement and resolution
Some expressions deny the partitive meaning. Al-Amīr dismisses this as a corruption. Recognize that the authoritative view permits both interpretations.
4
Define 'maʿānī'
The passage defines 'maʿānī' as what is opposite to the essence (dhāt), thus including both the essential and negative attributes. This shows the breadth of the term.
This prevents confusion that attributes of meanings are separate from the earlier categories.

ج. صفات المعاني سبع ١. صفة القدرة ودليلها] ٢٧ - وَقُدْرَةٌ إِرَادَةٌ وَغَايَرَتْ أَمْراً وَعِلْماً وَالرِّضَا كما ثَبَتْ  قوله : (وَقُدْرَةٌ) لما تكلم على الصفة النفسية وعلى الصفات السلبية، شرع يتكلم على صفات صفات المعاني مقدماً لها على الصفات المعنوية لكونها كالأصل لها، والإضافة في صفات المعاني للبيان، فالمراد الصفات التي هي المعاني، ويصح أن تكون على معنى (من)، كما نص عليه السكتاني وسيدي يحيى الشاوي، وقد نص عليه أيضاً في شرح الوسطى ، فالمعنى: صفات من المعاني باعتبار المعاني من حيث هي الشاملة لكل موجود من صفات القديم والحادث كالبياض ونحوه، ووقع في بعض العبارات ولا يصح أن تكون على معنى (من)، قال العلامة الأمير: (ولا وجه له، فلعله (تحريف انتهى . (٣) والمعاني : جمع معنى، وهو لغةٌ ما قابل الذات، فيشمل النفسية والسلبية،
[Section] C. The attributes of meanings are seven. 1. The attribute of power and its proof] 27 - 'And power, will, and they differ from command, knowledge, and contentment as established.' His saying: 'And power' – when he spoke about the essential attribute (al-ṣifa al-nafsiyya) and the negative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-salbiyya), he began to speak about the attributes of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), presenting them before the moral attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) because they are like the foundation for them. The additive construction (iḍāfa) in 'attributes of meanings' is for explanation (al-bayān), so what is meant is the attributes that are the meanings. And it is permissible that it be on the meaning of 'from' (min), as stated by al-Saktānī and my master Yaḥyā al-Shāwī, and it is also stated in Sharḥ al-Wusṭā. So the meaning: attributes from among the meanings, considering the meanings insofar as they encompass every existent, from the attributes of the Eternal and the originated, like whiteness and the like. And it occurs in some expressions that it is not permissible to be on the meaning of 'from'. Al-ʿAllāma al-Amīr said: 'There is no basis for this; perhaps it is a corruption.' (3) And 'meanings' (al-maʿānī) is the plural of 'meaning' (maʿnā), and it is linguistically what is opposite to the essence (al-dhāt), so it includes the essential and negative attributes.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage introduces the section on the seven attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), beginning with power and will. The author explains that after discussing the essential attribute (al-ṣifa al-nafsiyya) and the negative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-salbiyya), the text now proceeds to the attributes of meanings, placing them before the moral attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) because the latter are derived from them. The term 'attributes of meanings' (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) uses an additive construction (iḍāfa) that is explanatory (li-l-bayān), meaning these attributes are themselves meanings. Alternatively, it can be understood as 'attributes from among the meanings' (iḍāfa with min), as supported by scholars like al-Saktānī and Yaḥyā al-Shāwī. The passage also notes a disagreement: some say it cannot be 'from', but al-Amīr dismisses this as a corruption. Finally, it defines 'meanings' (maʿānī) as what is opposite to the essence (dhāt), including both the essential and negative attributes. This sets the stage for detailed discussion of each of the seven attributes.

The Attributes of Meaning and Defining Divine Power
The commentator introduces the seven attributes of meaning and defines the attribute of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the seven attributes of meaning are, how the term 'attributes of meaning' is interpreted, and why the definition of divine power is a descriptive definition (rasm) rather than a true definition (hadd haqiqi).

Core Idea
God's attributes of meaning are the foundations for understanding His actions, but their true essence is known only to Him.

In this passage, the commentator begins explaining the seven attributes of meaning (sifaat al-ma'aani), starting with the attribute of power (qudra). You'll explore the linguistic and technical meanings of key terms, the structure of the definition, and the crucial distinction between a descriptive definition and a true essential definition—a distinction rooted in the unknowability of God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the commentator moves from discussing the essential and negative attributes to the attributes of meaning.
Pay attention to the explanation of the idaafah construction: is it for clarification or does it mean 'min'?
2
Define key terminology
Identify the terms 'sifaat al-ma'aani', 'ma'na', 'qudra', 'rasm', and 'hadd haqiqi'. Understand their linguistic and technical definitions as given in the passage.
3
Analyze the definition of power
Look at the technical definition of qudra: an eternal attribute by which God creates and annihilates according to will. Ask yourself: is this a complete definition?
The commentator explicitly says it is a rasm, not a hadd haqiqi.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the commentator insists this is a descriptive definition. What does it imply about our knowledge of God? Connect this to the common misunderstanding that we can fully define divine attributes.
The passage ends with a powerful reminder: 'no one knows the reality of His essence and attributes except Him.'

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج. صفات المعاني سبع ١. صفة القدرة ودليلها] ٢٧ - وَقُدْرَةٌ إِرَادَةٌ وَغَايَرَتْ أَمْراً وَعِلْماً وَالرِّضَا كَمَا ثَبَتْ قوله : (وَقُدْرَةٌ) لما تكلم على الصفة النفسية وعلى الصفات السلبية، شرع يتكلم على صفات صفات المعاني مقدماً لها على الصفات المعنوية لكونها كالأصل لها، والإضافة في صفات المعاني للبيان، فالمراد الصفات التي هي المعاني، ويصح أن تكون على معنى (من)، كما نص عليه السكتاني وسيدي يحيى الشاوي، وقد نص عليه أيضاً في شرح الوسطى ، فالمعنى: صفات من المعاني باعتبار المعاني من حيث هي الشاملة لكل موجود من صفات القديم والحادث كالبياض ونحوه، ووقع في بعض العبارات ولا يصح أن تكون على معنى (من)، قال العلامة الأمير: (ولا وجه له، فلعله (تحريف انتهى . (۳) والمعاني : جمع معنى، وهو لغةً ما قابل الذات، فيشمل النفسية والسلبية، واصطلاحاً كل صفة قائمة بموصوف موجبة له حكماً، ككونه قادراً ؛ فإنه لازم للقدرة)، وفي الحقيقة المعاني والمعنوية متلازمان لكنهم لاحظوا الوجودي أصلاً لغيره. وبدأ المصنف من صفات المعاني بالقدرة لظهور تأثيرها فقال: (وقدرة)؛ أي: وواجب له قدرةٌ، فهو معطوف على الوجود وهي لغة القوة والاستطاعة كما قاله المؤلف في «كبيره»، وعرفاً : صفةٌ أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى يتأتى بها إيجادُ كلِّ ممكن وإعدامه على وفق الإرادة وهذا رسم لا حد حقيقي، وهكذا سائر التعاريف المذكورة للصفات ؛ لأنَّه لا يعلم كُنْهَ ذاته وصفاته - أي : حقيقة ذلك - إلا هو.
J. The attributes of meaning are seven. 1. The attribute of power and its evidence. 27. 'And power, will, and that which differs from command, and knowledge, and pleasure, as established.' His saying: (And power) – When he spoke about the essential attribute and the negative attributes, he began to speak about the attributes of the attributes of meaning, giving them precedence over the moral attributes because they are like the foundation for them. The idaafah in 'attributes of meaning' is for clarification, so the intended meaning is the attributes that are the meanings. It is valid that it be in the sense of 'min' (of), as stated by al-Saktaani and Sayyidi Yahya al-Shawi, and also stated in Sharh al-Wustaa. So the meaning is: attributes from the meanings, considering the meanings as they are inclusive of every existent from the attributes of the Eternal and the contingent, like whiteness and the like. In some expressions it occurs that it is not valid to be in the sense of 'min'; al-Ameer said: 'There is no basis for it, perhaps it is a corruption.' (3). And 'al-ma'aani' is the plural of 'ma'naa', which linguistically is what is opposite to the essence, so it includes the essential and the negative. Technically, it is every attribute subsisting in a described thing necessitating a judgment for it, such as being able, for it is necessary for power. In reality, the meanings and the moral attributes are concomitant, but they considered the existential as the origin for other than itself. And the author began from the attributes of meaning with power due to the apparentness of its effect, so he said: (And power) i.e., the Necessary has power, so it is coordinated with existence. Linguistically, it is strength and capability, as the author said in his 'Kabeer'. Technically, it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence—exalted is He—by which the bringing into existence of every possible thing and its annihilation is achieved in accordance with will. This is a description (rasm), not a true definition (hadd haqiqi). Likewise all the definitions mentioned for the attributes, because no one knows the reality of His essence and attributes—i.e., their true nature—except Him.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage introduces the seven attributes of meaning (sifaat al-ma'aani) and provides a detailed definition of the first, power (qudra). The commentator begins by noting that the author, after discussing the essential attribute and the negative attributes, now turns to the attributes of meaning, giving them precedence over the moral attributes (al-sifaat al-ma'nawiyya) because they are like the foundation. The phrase 'attributes of meaning' is explained: the idaafah (genitive construction) is for clarification, meaning the attributes that are the meanings themselves, or it can be understood as 'min' (i.e., attributes from among the meanings). The commentator cites authorities like al-Saktaani and Yahya al-Shawi, and notes a disagreement where some deny this interpretation. The term 'ma'aani' (meanings) is defined linguistically as what is opposite to the essence, including essential and negative attributes; technically, it is every attribute inherent in a subject that necessitates a judgment, like being powerful necessitates power. The author starts with power due to its evident effect. The word 'qudra' is defined linguistically as strength and capability, and technically as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence by which He brings into existence and annihilates every possible thing according to His will. This definition is a 'rasm' (descriptive definition), not a 'hadd haqiqi' (true definition), because the true nature of God's essence and attributes is known only to Him.

Correction: The commentator explicitly states it is a descriptive definition (rasm) because the true nature of God's essence and attributes is known only to Him. All definitions of divine attributes are merely explanatory.

The Seven Modalities of Power and the Ash'ari View
Enumeration of seven power relationships and Ash'ari view on annihilation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven modalities through which divine power relates to possible things, and the Ash'ari position that annihilation occurs indirectly.

Core Idea
Divine power relates to creation and annihilation through seven distinct modalities.

This passage from the commentary on al-Jawhara provides a detailed classification of how God's power (qudra) engages with possible beings. The author distinguishes three types of relationships: eternal suitability, grasp, and temporal actuality. He also notes the unique Ash'ari view on annihilation, which sets it apart from direct causation. Let's work through the text to master these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once through, then follow the translation. Notice the technical terms for each type of relationship.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three categories
The author divides the relationships into three kinds: one eternal potential, three 'grasp', and three 'temporal actual'. Find the descriptions for each.
The 'grasp' and 'temporal actual' each have three sub-items.
3
Understand the Ash'ari view
Pay attention to the last part where al-Ash'ari's opinion is cited: power does not directly annihilate but removes sustenance. Compare this to the earlier classification.
This is a key theological nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قولنا : يتأتى بها إيجاد كلِّ ممكن وإعدامُهُ إشارة إلى تعلقها الصلوحي القديم، ويُقال له الصلاحي القديم، وهو صلاحيتها في الأزل للإيجاد والإعدام فيما لا يزال، وتتعلَّق بعدمنا فيما لا يزال قبل وجودنا وباستمرار الوجود بعد العدم، وباستمرار العدم بعد الوجود تعلق قبضة في هذه الثلاثة، بمعنى أن الممكن في قبضة القدرة، فإن شاء الله أبقاه على عدمه أو على وجوده، وإن شاء أوجده أو أعدمه، وتتعلق بإيجادنا بالفعل بعد العدم السابق، وبإعدامنا بالفعل بعد الوجود، وبإيجادنا بالفعل حين البعث تعلقاً تنجيزيا حادثاً في هذه الثلاثة، فأقسام تعلقات القدرة سبعة تفصيلاً : صلوحي قديم، وتعلقات القبضة ثلاثة والتعلقات التنجيزية ثلاثة؛ فالجملة ما ذكر كما وضحه شيخنا (۲) في رسالته، وأما العدم الأزلي فلا تتعلق به القدرة لأنه واجب. وذهب الأشعري إلى أنها لا تتعلق بإعدامنا بعد وجودنا، بل إذا أراد الله عدم الممكن قطع عنه الإمدادات فينعدم بنفسه، كالفتيلة إذا قطع عنها الزيت، انطفأت
And in our saying: 'by which the bringing into existence of every possible thing and its annihilation is achieved' is an indication of its eternal potential relationship (al-talluq al-saluhi al-qadeem), also called the eternal suitability (al-salahi al-qadeem), which is its suitability in pre-eternity for bringing into existence and annihilating in what will be. And it relates to our non-existence in what will be before our existence, and to the continuation of existence after non-existence, and to the continuation of non-existence after existence – a 'grasp' relationship in these three, meaning that the possible is in the grasp of power: if God wills, He keeps it in its non-existence or in its existence, and if He wills, He brings it into existence or annihilates it. And it relates to our actual bringing into existence after prior non-existence, and to our actual annihilation after existence, and to our actual bringing into existence at the time of resurrection – a temporal actual relationship (talluq tanjizi hadith) in these three. So the divisions of the relationships of power are seven in detail: an eternal potential relationship, three 'grasp' relationships, and three temporal actual relationships. The total is what was mentioned, as our teacher (2) clarified in his epistle. As for eternal non-existence, power does not relate to it because it is necessary. And al-Ash'ari held that it does not relate to our annihilation after our existence, rather if God wills the non-existence of a possible, He cuts off its supplies and it becomes non-existent by itself, like the candle when fuel is cut off from it – it goes out.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the ways in which the divine power (qudra) relates to possible things. The commentator starts by clarifying that the phrase 'by which every possible thing is brought into existence and annihilated' refers to the eternal potential relationship (al-talluq al-saluhi al-qadeem), also called the eternal suitability. This means that power, from eternity, is suitable for creating and annihilating things at the appropriate times. Then he distinguishes three types of 'grasp' relationships (talluq al-qabdah): power relates to our non-existence before existence (keeping us in nonexistence), to the continuation of existence after it is given, and to the continuation of nonexistence after annihilation. In these three, the possible is held in the grasp of God's power, subject to His will. Next, three 'actual temporal' relationships (talluq tanjizi) are listed: power relates to our actual creation after prior nonexistence, to our actual annihilation after existence, and to our actual recreation at the resurrection. Each of these is a temporal actuality. Thus the total is seven relationships: one eternal potential, three grasp, and three actual. The commentator notes that eternal nonexistence is not subject to power because it is necessary (i.e., God's own nonexistence is impossible). Finally, he reports that al-Ash'ari held a different view: power does not directly cause annihilation; rather, when God wills a thing to cease, He removes the 'supplies' that sustain it, and it ceases by itself, like a lamp that goes out when oil is cut.

Key Terms
تعلق صلوحي
تعلق قبضة
تعلق تنجيزي
قدرة
إعدام
أشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine power directly causes annihilation in the same way it causes creation.
Correction: According to al-Ash'ari, annihilation is indirect: God cuts off the supplies sustaining existence, and the thing ceases by itself.

Checkpoint
How many 'grasp' relationships of power are mentioned?
A) One
B) Three
C) Seven
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The divine power relates to possible things in seven modalities: one eternal potential, three grasp, and three actual, with the Ash'ari view that annihilation occurs indirectly by cutting off sustenance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are reading about the fact that your very existence at this moment is sustained only by God's continuous act of supplying existence to you. If God were to 'cut off the supplies' even for an instant, you would cease to exist. How does that reality make you feel about your independence? In what concrete ways can you live today that reflect total dependence on Him, not just in words but in your actions and decisions?
?
The Ash'ari view says that annihilation happens indirectly—God does not directly 'destroy' but simply stops sustaining. If God's withdrawal of support is what brings about your end, what does that imply about the nature of your relationship with Him? Are you actively seeking His continuous support, or are you living as if you are self-sufficient? What would it mean to 'cling to Him' as the source of your existence, even beyond the afterlife?
?
You learned that divine power relates to you in seven specific ways, from your pre-existence to your resurrection. Which of these relationships do you think about least—your continued existence, your potential annihilation, or your resurrection? Why is it dangerous to ignore any of them? Write down one specific change you will make this week to remind yourself that you are always in God's grasp, whether in life or after death.

Defining Divine Attributes and Power (Qudra)
Defines 'sifa' and gives linguistic and technical definitions of qudra, clarifying it is a rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of a divine attribute (sifa) and the specific definition of qudra, and why it is a descriptive definition (rasm) rather than a true essential definition (hadd).

Core Idea
Divine attributes are described, not defined in essence.

Before discussing specific attributes, the author clarifies what an 'attribute' (sifa) means in kalam terminology. He then zooms in on qudra (power) as the first attribute of meanings, providing its linguistic and technical definitions. Notably, he emphasizes that these definitions are merely descriptive (rasm), not true essential definitions (hadd), because Allah's essence and attributes are ultimately beyond human comprehension.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'sifa'
Read the opening sentence that defines 'sifa' in technical usage. Note that an attribute subsists in a subject and necessitates a judgment.
The example given is power necessitating being powerful.
2
Grasp the distinction between ma'ani and ma'nawiyya
The passage clarifies that these two categories are concomitant, but one is chosen as the existential principle.
This sets up the discussion of attributes of meanings.
3
Learn the linguistic and technical definitions of qudra
First, qudra means strength and ability linguistically. Then, the technical definition: an eternal attribute by which Allah brings into existence and annihilates every possible in accordance with His will.
Notice that the definition includes key elements: eternal, attached to Allah's essence, effect on possibles, and tied to will.
4
Recognize the limitation: rasm not hadd
The author explicitly states that this is a description (rasm), not a true essential definition (hadd), because Allah's true nature is known only to Him.
This humility applies to all attribute definitions.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider why the author emphasizes this limitation and how it affects our understanding of divine attributes.
It protects against anthropomorphism and acknowledges divine transcendence.

الإلهيات واصطلاحاً كل صفة قائمة بموصوف موجبة له حكماً، ككونه قادراً ؛ فإنه لازم للقدرة)، وفي الحقيقة المعاني والمعنوية متلازمان لكنهم لاحظوا الوجودي أصلاً لغيره. وبدأ المصنف من صفات المعاني بالقدرة لظهور تأثيرها فقال: (وقدرة)؛ أي: وواجب له قدرةٌ، فهو معطوف على الوجود وهي لغة القوة والاستطاعة كما قاله المؤلف في «كبيره»، وعرفاً : صفةٌ أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى يتأتى بها إيجادُ كلِّ ممكن وإعدامه على وفق الإرادة وهذا رسم لا حد حقيقي، وهكذا سائر التعاريف المذكورة للصفات ؛ لأنَّه لا يعلم كُنْهَ ذاته وصفاته - أي : حقيقة ذلك - إلا هو.
In terminology, every attribute (sifa) subsisting in a described entity necessitating a judgment for it, such as 'being powerful' for it is a necessary concomitant of power. In reality, the ma'ani and ma'nawiyya are concomitant, but they considered the existential principle for the other. The author began from the attributes of meanings with power because of the obviousness of its effect, and said: 'And power'; i.e., and necessary existent has power; it is coordinated with existence. Linguistically it is strength and ability as the author said in his 'Kabir'. Terminologically: an eternal attribute subsisting in the Essence of the Most High whereby the bringing into existence of every possible (mumkin) and its annihilation takes place in accordance with will. This is a description (rasm), not a true definition (hadd). Similarly for the other mentioned definitions of attributes, because the essence of His Essence and His attributes – i.e., the reality of that – is not known except by Him.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage first defines the term 'sifa' (attribute) in technical kalam usage: an attribute is something that subsists in a subject and necessitates a corresponding judgment (e.g., power necessitates being powerful). It then clarifies that the 'ma'ani' attributes (like power, will) and 'ma'nawiyya' attributes (like being powerful, willing) are inseparable, but the author focuses on the existential aspect. Next, the passage gives the definition of qudra (power) specific to Allah. Linguistically, qudra means strength and ability. Terminologically, it is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence by which He brings into existence and annihilates every possible thing according to His will. Importantly, the author notes that this is only a 'rasm' (descriptive definition), not a 'hadd' (true essential definition), because the true nature of Allah's attributes is known only to Him. This humility underscores the limit of human knowledge.
