Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of qudra is a true essential definition (hadd) that fully captures Allah's attribute.
Correction: The author explicitly says it is a rasm (description) not a hadd (true definition), because we cannot know the true essence of Allah's attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the definition of qudra?
A) It is a true essential definition (hadd).
B) It is a description (rasm) not a true definition.
C) It is unknown.
D) It is linguistic only.

Key Takeaway
The definition of qudra is a descriptive description (rasm) that outlines its function, not a full essential definition (hadd), because Allah's true nature is beyond human knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt a sense of spiritual security from 'knowing' Allah's attributes? The passage says we only have a rasm, a description of effects, not the essence. Does that realization disturb you? How does it change the way you speak to Allah in dua?
?
The author humbly notes that the true nature of His attributes is known only to Him. Are you content with that limit, or do you find yourself wanting to completely 'figure out' Allah? What subtle arrogance might be hidden in your quest for full understanding?
?
Allah's power brings things into existence and annihilates them according to His will. Have you ever questioned why He annihilates something you love? How does knowing that His power is tied to His will (not random) shape your response to loss or hardship?

The Seven Relations of Divine Power
Classification of the seven relations of qudra with doctrinal nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven types of relations (ta'alluqat) of qudra: the primordial aptitude, the three grasp relations, and the three actualized relations, along with the special case of eternal non-existence and al-Ash'ari's view on annihilation.

Core Idea
Divine power relates to creation and annihilation in seven distinct ways.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid examines the different ways in which Allah's power (qudra) relates to possible things. It distinguishes between a primordial suitability that exists prior to any actual creation, a 'grasp' relation that holds possibilities in check, and an actualized relation that brings about existence or non-existence at a specific time. The text also addresses the special case of eternal non-existence and presents al-Ash'ari's unique perspective on how annihilation occurs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the primordial aptitude
Read how the power's suitability in pre-eternity to create or annihilate at any future moment is described. This is the first and most fundamental relation.
This relation is eternal (qadim) and not temporally bound.
2
Identify the three grasp relations
Find the three cases where power relates to the possible as if holding it in a 'grasp': (1) continuation of non-existence before existence, (2) continuation of existence after creation, (3) continuation of non-existence after annihilation. Notice that all these are possibilities held in Allah's grasp.
The term 'grasp' (qabda) emphasizes that the possible is entirely subject to Allah's will.
3
Identify the three actualized relations
Locate the three cases where power actually brings something into being or annihilates it: (1) creation after non-existence, (2) annihilation after existence, (3) resurrection after death. These are temporally new (hadith).
These are the directly observable acts of power in time.
4
Review the total and special cases
Confirm that the total is seven: 1 + 3 + 3. Then note the exception of eternal non-existence (al-`adam al-azali) – power does not relate to it because it is necessary. Finally, consider al-Ash'ari's view that power does not directly annihilate but rather withdraws sustaining existence.
This adds nuance: annihilation is indirect on al-Ash'ari's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قولنا : يتأتى بها إيجاد كلِّ ممكن وإعدامُهُ إشارة إلى تعلقها الصلوحي القديم، ويُقال له الصلاحي القديم، وهو صلاحيتها في الأزل للإيجاد والإعدام فيما لا يزال، وتتعلَّق بعدمنا فيما لا يزال قبل وجودنا وباستمرار الوجود بعد العدم، وباستمرار العدم بعد الوجود تعلق قبضة في هذه الثلاثة، بمعنى أن الممكن في قبضة القدرة، فإن شاء الله أبقاه على عدمه أو على وجوده، وإن شاء أوجده أو أعدمه، وتتعلق بإيجادنا بالفعل بعد العدم السابق، وبإعدامنا بالفعل بعد الوجود، وبإيجادنا بالفعل حين البعث تعلقاً تنجيزيا حادثاً في هذه الثلاثة، فأقسام تعلقات القدرة سبعة تفصيلاً : صلوحي قديم، وتعلقات القبضة ثلاثة والتعلقات التنجيزية ثلاثة؛ فالجملة ما ذكر كما وضحه شيخنا (۲) في رسالته، وأما العدم الأزلي فلا تتعلق به القدرة لأنه واجب. وذهب الأشعري إلى أنها لا تتعلق بإعدامنا بعد وجودنا، بل إذا أراد الله عدم الممكن قطع عنه الإمدادات فينعدم بنفسه، كالفتيلة إذا قطع عنها الزيت، انطفأت بنفسها.
And in our saying 'whereby He brings into existence every possible and annihilates it' is an indication to its primordial aptitude (al-ta'alluq al-saluhi al-qadim), also called the primordial suitability (al-salahi al-qadim), which is its suitability in pre-eternity for bringing into existence and annihilation in the future. And it relates to our non-existence in the future before our existence, and to the continuation of existence after non-existence, and to the continuation of non-existence after existence, as a grasping relation (ta'alluq qabda) in these three, meaning that the possible is in the grasp of power; if Allah wills He keeps it in its non-existence or existence, and if He wills He brings it into existence or annihilates it. And it relates to our actual bringing into existence after prior non-existence, to our actual annihilation after existence, and to our actual bringing into existence at resurrection, as an actualized temporal relation (ta'alluq tanjizi hadith) in these three. So the divisions of the relations of power are seven in detail: one primordial suitability, three grasping relations, and three actualized relations; total as mentioned as our teacher clarified in his treatise. As for eternal non-existence, power does not relate to it because it is necessary. And al-Ash'ari held that it does not relate to our annihilation after our existence; rather, if Allah wills the non-existence of a possible, He cuts off the supplies, and it perishes by itself, like a wick when oil is cut off from it, it extinguishes by itself.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage classifies the relations (ta'alluqat) of qudra into seven types. It begins with the primordial aptitude (saluhi qadim): the power's suitability in eternity to create or annihilate at any future moment. Then there are three 'grasp' relations (qabda): power relates to our non-existence before we exist (continuation of non-existence), to the continuation of our existence after we are created, and to the continuation of non-existence after we are annihilated — all are possibilities held in Allah's grasp. Finally, there are three actualized (tanjizi) relations: actual creation of us after non-existence, actual annihilation after existence, and actual resurrection after death. The total is seven. The passage also notes that eternal non-existence (al-`adam al-azali) is not subject to power because it is necessary (it cannot be changed). Additionally, al-Ash'ari's view is mentioned: power does not directly annihilate a possible; rather, Allah withdraws sustaining existence, and the possible ceases to exist by itself.

Key Terms
التعلق الصلوحي
التعلق التنجيزي
القبضة
العدم الأزلي
الأشعري
الإمدادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that divine power only has one kind of relation: directly causing existence or non-existence in time.
Correction: Power has multiple relations: an eternal suitability, grasp relations that maintain possibilities, and actualized temporal acts. Additionally, al-Ash'ari held that annihilation is indirect.

Checkpoint
How many total divisions of the relations of qudra are mentioned?
A) 3
B) 5
C) 7
D) 9

Key Takeaway
Divine power relates to possibles in seven ways: one eternal aptitude, three holding relations, and three actualizing relations, with the nuance that eternal non-existence is unaffected and al-Ash'ari views annihilation as indirect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a possible thing held in the grasp of Allah's power right now. If He 'cut off the supplies' of your existence, you would cease to be. Does that thought make you feel anxious or liberated? What does it mean for your sense of control over your life? Commit to repeating 'there is no power and no strength except with Allah' with full presence of heart every time you feel self-sufficient today.
?
The lesson distinguishes between primordial suitability and actualized relation. Allah's power is eternally suited to create you, but He brought you into existence at a specific moment. How does knowing that your existence was not haphazard but a deliberate act of divine will affect your gratitude? What can you do today to live as someone who was 'willed into being' rather than a random accident?
?
Al-Ash'ari says annihilation is by withdrawal of sustenance, not by direct destruction. Reflect: every blessing you have is sustained only by Allah's ongoing supply. What 'supplies' (health, wealth, relationships, faith) are you taking for granted? Write down one area where you will consciously acknowledge Allah as the Sustainer and thank Him specifically, not just generally.

Attribution of Influence to the Essence, Not to Qudra
Clarifies that the effect is really from Allah's essence, and qudra is only a cause; warns against implying qudra acts independently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing the effect directly to divine power (qudra) is metaphorical and why implying that power acts independently can lead to unbelief.

Core Idea
True agency belongs to Allah's essence; power is only a cause.

The phrase 'by it' in the definition of qudra is carefully chosen. It points to a crucial theological distinction: the real effect comes from Allah's essence, not from power as an independent agent. This lesson unpacks that distinction and explains the prohibition against misleading phrases that might suggest otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the core claim that effect is from the essence, not from qudra directly.
2
Understand the metaphorical attribution
The passage states that attributing effect to qudra is metaphorical because qudra is only a cause. Reflect on what this means for how we speak about divine actions.
Metaphor here means the term is used by extension of its literal meaning.
3
Identify the prohibited phrases
The passage explicitly forbids saying 'power is effective' or 'look at the action of power'. Find these in the text and understand why they are problematic.
4
Grasp the consequence: kufr
If someone intends to imply that qudra influences by itself, that belief is considered unbelief. Recognize the gravity of this warning.
This shows the high stakes in precise theological language.
5
Relate to the broader mutakallimun principle
Connect this lesson to the overarching principle that only Allah's essence is the true agent. Power is an attribute that describes the essence but does not act independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قولنا : (بها) إشارة إلى أن التأثير حقيقة للذات وإسناد التأثير إلى القدرة مجاز لكونها سبباً فيه، ويحرم أن يُقال : القدرة فَعَّالةٌ، أو انظر فعل القدرة، أو نحو ذلك، لما فيه من إيهام أنها المؤثرة بنفسها ، فإن قصد ذلك كفر، والعياذ بالله تعالى.
And in our saying 'by it' is an indication that the effect is in reality for the Essence, and attributing the effect to power is metaphorical because it is a cause in it. It is prohibited to say: 'Power is effective' or 'look at the action of power' or the like, because it suggests that it influences by itself. If that is intended, it is unbelief (kufr), and Allah forbid.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of the phrase 'بها' (by it) in the definition of qudra. The particle 'بها' indicates that the true agent of bringing into existence and annihilating is Allah's essence (al-dhat). Power (qudra) is only a cause (sabab) through which the effect occurs, so attributing the effect to power is metaphorical (majaz). The author then issues a strong warning: it is forbidden (yuhram) to say 'power is effective' (al-qudratu fa''ala) or 'look at the action of power' because such phrases may mislead people into thinking power acts by itself. If someone actually intends that meaning, it is kufr (unbelief). This shows the careful language required in discussing divine attributes.

Key Terms
التأثير
الذات
المجاز
السبب
فَعَّالة
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qudra itself is the agent, whereas it is only a cause; the true agent is Allah's essence.
Correction: qudra is only a cause; the true agent is Allah's essence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about calling qudra 'effective' (فعالة)?
A) It is permissible.
B) It is recommended.
C) It is prohibited because it may imply power acts by itself.
D) It is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The true agent of every effect is Allah's essence; power is merely a cause, and attributing agency to power is metaphorical and potentially dangerous.

The Scope of Divine Power: Only the Possible
Delimits the scope of qudra to possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power (qudra) only pertains to possible things and does not extend to the necessary existent or the impossible.

Core Idea
Divine power is limited to possibles; it cannot change what is necessary or impossible.

This passage from Sanusi clarifies a critical boundary of divine omnipotence. It explains that qudra (God's power) is only meaningful when applied to possibilities—things that can exist or not exist. It cannot be applied to Allah Himself (the Necessary Existent) or to logical impossibilities. Understanding this prevents theological errors about what God can and cannot do.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice how the argument flows: it first excludes the necessary and impossible, then explains why each is excluded.
The term 'tahsil al-hasil' will be explained in the commentary.
2
Identify the two exclusions
Identify why qudra cannot apply to the necessary existent (wajib) and why it cannot apply to the impossible (mustahil). Note the symmetrical reasoning: for the necessary, bringing it into existence is redundant; for the impossible, annihilating it is meaningless.
3
Understand 'tahsil al-hasil'
Reflect on the concept of 'obtaining the already obtained' (tahsil al-hasil). This is a logical absurdity: you cannot achieve something that already exists. This reinforces why qudra cannot apply to the necessary existent.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the reasoning from this passage correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخرج بقولنا : كلُّ ممكن الواجب والمستحيلُ فلا تتعلق بكلِّ منهما ؛ لأنَّها إِنْ تعلقت بالواجب فلا يصلح أن تعدمه لأنه لا يقبل العدم، ولا يصلح أن توجده لأنه يلزم منه تحصيل الحاصل، وإن تعلقت بالمستحيل فعلى العكس من ذلك.
And by our saying 'every possible' is excluded the necessary (wajib) and the impossible (mustahil), so it does not relate to either of them; because if it relates to the necessary, it is not valid to annihilate it because it does not admit of annihilation, nor is it valid to bring it into existence because that would result in obtaining the already obtained. And if it relates to the impossible, it is the opposite.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage delimits the scope of qudra: it applies only to the possible (mumkin). The necessary existent (wajib, i.e., Allah Himself) cannot be annihilated because it does not accept non-existence, nor can it be brought into existence because it eternally exists (that would be tahsil al-hasil – obtaining the already obtained). The impossible (mustahil, e.g., a partner to Allah) cannot be brought into existence because it is inherently impossible, and if qudra tried to annihilate it, that is meaningless since it does not exist. Thus, qudra is defined strictly over the domain of possibles.

Key Terms
الممكن
الواجب
المستحيل
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine power can apply to impossible things if Allah wills, ignoring logical impossibility.
Correction: The passage shows that qudra only applies to possible things. Impossible things are not possible even for divine power, because logical absurdities cannot be actualized.

Checkpoint
Why does qudra not apply to the necessary existent (wajib)?
A) Because it is not a possible.
B) Because the necessary cannot be annihilated or re-created.
C) Because the necessary is independent of qudra.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
Divine qudra is exclusively operative over the domain of possible things, not over the necessary or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's power, do you sometimes imagine that He could do absolutely anything, even contradictions? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and are you willing to accept that divine power is rationally bounded?
?
This teaching says Allah cannot do the impossible. Does that make you feel that Allah is limited? Or does it make you trust that His power is coherent and meaningful? What difference does this make in how you rely on Him?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's power only operates over possibles, how does that affect your du'a? Do you ask for things that are possible, or do you sometimes ask for the impossible? What does this passage teach you about the nature of your requests to Allah?

Refuting the Claim that Allah Can Create Impossibilities
Refutes attributed claim about divine power over impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that Allah can create logical impossibilities is both rationally unsound and doctrinally dangerous.

Core Idea
Divine power does not extend to intrinsic impossibilities.

This passage tackles a problematic attribution to Ibn al-Arabi, suggesting that Allah can create things that are logically impossible, such as a city made from the leftover clay of Adam. The author refutes this by labeling it as false and likely forged. We explore why such a view corrupts the concept of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author dismisses the claim without lengthy argument.
2
Identify the problem
What does 'المحال' mean? Why is claiming Allah can create it problematic?
Intrinsic impossibility vs. contingent impossibility.
3
Understand the refutation
The author says this belief is not permissible to hold and that the statement is likely forged. Why is that sufficient?
Because if taken literally, it contradicts the nature of divine power.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this view affects the doctrine of divine attributes. Why must power be limited to possibles?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما في اليواقيت (۱) للشعراني عن ابن العربي: أنه تعالى يقدر على خلق المحال عقلاً، وأنه دخل الأرض المخلوقة من بقية خميرة طينة آدم، وهي مدينة إنما تدخلها الأرواح، فرأى فيها ذلك بعينه ...) كلام لا يجوز اعتقاد ظاهره، وقد نُقل أنه مدسوس عليه . وقد شنّع السنوسي في شرح الصغرى على ابن حزم في قوله : (الله قادر أن يتخذ ولداً وإلا كان عاجزاً، ولم يعقل أن العجز إنما يكون إذا كان المتعلق من وظائف القدرة، بأن كان يقبلُ الوجود لذاته، ويلزم عليه أن المولى قادر على إعدام قدرته، بل وعلى إعدام ذاته، وفي ذلك غاية الفساد.
And what is in 'al-Yawaqit' (1) by al-Sha'rani from Ibn al-Arabi: that the Most High is able to create intellectual impossibility, and that he entered the created earth from the residue of the yeast of the clay of Adam, and it is a city that only souls enter, and he saw that with his own eyes... such speech is not permissible to believe its apparent meaning, and it has been reported that it is falsely attributed to him. And al-Sanusi in his commentary on al-Sughra denounced Ibn Hazm for his statement: 'Allah is able to take a son, otherwise He would be incapable.' He did not realize that incapability only occurs when the object (muta'allaq) is within the functions of power, being that it accepts existence by itself. And it would follow that the Master is able to annihilate His power, even to annihilate His essence, and that is utmost corruption.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents two deviant views and refutes them. The first, attributed to Ibn al-Arabi via al-Sha'rani, claims Allah can create logical impossibilities (e.g., interpenetration of bodies) and that Ibn al-Arabi saw a city made from Adam's clay. The author dismisses these as false and possibly forged. The second is Ibn Hazm's claim that Allah must be able to take a son, otherwise He would be incapable. Al-Sanusi refutes this by noting that incapability only applies to possibles; taking a son is impossible because it involves a contingent being that would accept existence by itself. Accepting this leads to the absurd consequence that Allah could annihilate His own power and essence, which is impossible and corrupt.

Key Terms
المحال
مدسوس
ابن العربي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah can do everything, including logical impossibilities like creating a square circle.
Correction: Divine power is limited to what is possible by itself; impossible things are not objects of power because they are intrinsically non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the proper response to the claim that Allah can create intellectual impossibilities?
A) It is a valid opinion among scholars.
B) It is not permissible to believe its apparent meaning and it may be forged.
C) It is a matter of interpretation.
D) It is acceptable as long as it is attributed to a saint.

Key Takeaway
The claim that Allah can create logical impossibilities is dismissed as false and likely forged, preserving the principle that divine power is limited to possibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a claim about Allah simply because it sounded profound or came from a respected figure, without checking whether it aligns with the clear principles of revelation and reason? What will you do to guard against this in the future?
?
This passage shows that some false ideas are not worth lengthy refutation—they are simply dismissed. Is there a false belief about Allah that you have entertained, giving it more attention than it deserves? Will you commit to rejecting it firmly and moving on as the author did?
?
The report mentioned here is dismissed as forged. How often do you check the authenticity of religious claims before adopting them? Will you resolve to verify narrations about Allah's attributes before allowing them to shape your belief?

Refuting Ibn Hazm on Allah Taking a Son
Refutes Ibn Hazm's claim about divine power to take a son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Hazm's argument that Allah must be able to take a son is flawed, and how it leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Allah's power is not measured by the absence of incapability regarding impossibles.

Ibn Hazm argued that if Allah cannot take a son, He would be incapable, thereby implying He must be able to do so. Al-Sanusi refutes this by clarifying the definition of incapability: it only applies to possible things. Taking a son is impossible because it would imply a contingent being that exists by itself, leading to the absurdity that Allah could annihilate His own power and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Hazm's claim
Read the passage where Ibn Hazm argues that Allah can take a son otherwise He would be incapable.
2
Understand al-Sanusi's refutation
Al-Sanusi points out that incapability only applies when the object is within the scope of power, i.e., possible. Since taking a son is impossible, it does not imply incapability.
3
Trace the absurd consequence
The passage concludes that if the argument were accepted, it would imply Allah could annihilate His own power and essence, which is utterly corrupt.
4
Reflect on the principle
This refutation upholds the principle that divine power pertains only to possibles, safeguarding tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما في اليواقيت (۱) للشعراني عن ابن العربي: أنه تعالى يقدر على خلق المحال عقلاً، وأنه دخل الأرض المخلوقة من بقية خميرة طينة آدم، وهي مدينة إنما تدخلها الأرواح، فرأى فيها ذلك بعينه ...) كلام لا يجوز اعتقاد ظاهره، وقد نُقل أنه مدسوس عليه . وقد شنّع السنوسي في شرح الصغرى على ابن حزم في قوله : (الله قادر أن يتخذ ولداً وإلا كان عاجزاً، ولم يعقل أن العجز إنما يكون إذا كان المتعلق من وظائف القدرة، بأن كان يقبلُ الوجود لذاته، ويلزم عليه أن المولى قادر على إعدام قدرته، بل وعلى إعدام ذاته، وفي ذلك غاية الفساد.
And what is in 'al-Yawaqit' (1) by al-Sha'rani from Ibn al-Arabi: that the Most High is able to create intellectual impossibility, and that he entered the created earth from the residue of the yeast of the clay of Adam, and it is a city that only souls enter, and he saw that with his own eyes... such speech is not permissible to believe its apparent meaning, and it has been reported that it is falsely attributed to him. And al-Sanusi in his commentary on al-Sughra denounced Ibn Hazm for his statement: 'Allah is able to take a son, otherwise He would be incapable.' He did not realize that incapability only occurs when the object (muta'allaq) is within the functions of power, being that it accepts existence by itself. And it would follow that the Master is able to annihilate His power, even to annihilate His essence, and that is utmost corruption.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents two deviant views and refutes them. The first, attributed to Ibn al-Arabi via al-Sha'rani, claims Allah can create logical impossibilities (e.g., interpenetration of bodies) and that Ibn al-Arabi saw a city made from Adam's clay. The author dismisses these as false and possibly forged. The second is Ibn Hazm's claim that Allah must be able to take a son, otherwise He would be incapable. Al-Sanusi refutes this by noting that incapability only applies to possibles; taking a son is impossible because it involves a contingent being that would accept existence by itself. Accepting this leads to the absurd consequence that Allah could annihilate His own power and essence, which is impossible and corrupt.

Key Terms
الابن
العجز
إعدام الذات
السنوسي
ابن حزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah must be able to do everything, including having a son, because otherwise He would be incapable.
Correction: Incapacity is defined as inability to perform a possible action; impossible things are not objects of power.

Checkpoint
What does al-Sanusi say about incapability?
A) It applies to all things.
B) It only occurs when the object is possible and accepts existence.
C) It does not exist in God.
D) It is a quality of created beings.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Hazm's argument is refuted by clarifying that incapability only applies to possibles, and accepting it leads to the absurd conclusion that Allah could annihilate Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Hazm's reasoning seemed logical to him, yet it led to a conclusion that undermines Allah's perfection. Where in your own life have you trusted your own reasoning so strongly that it subtly distorted your understanding of Allah, even if you didn't realize it? Today, will you repent from intellectual pride and submit to what the scholars have clarified based on revelation?
?
This lesson shows that divine power does not extend to impossible things. Have you ever, even in your heart, demanded that Allah be 'able to do everything' in an absolute sense, including things that contradict His nature? What does that demand reveal about your concept of servanthood? Ask Allah to purify your understanding of His majesty.
?
If someone were to argue that 'a God who cannot do everything is not truly God,' and you now know the refutation – why does your heart still sometimes feel uneasy with the idea that Allah cannot do logical absurdities? Is that unease coming from a desire to exalt Allah, or from a hidden reliance on your own standards of power? Commit today to internalize that Allah's power is perfect precisely because it is defined by His wisdom and nature, not by human imagination.

The Story of Iblis and Idris on Divine Power
Narrates a story illustrating the proper response to obstinate questions about divine power over impossible scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some questions about divine power are best met with rebuke rather than detailed theological argument.

Core Idea
Obstinate questions deserve rebuke, not detailed answers.

This story from Islamic tradition illustrates a critical principle in theological discourse: not every question is sincere. When the questioner intends to undermine faith, the appropriate response is to cut off the debate, as Prophet Idris did with Iblis. The story also demonstrates the principle that recompense fits the crime.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to understand the narrative flow.
Notice the direct action and the later explanation.
2
Identify the question and response
Iblis asked whether Allah can put the world in a hazelnut shell. Idris responded by poking out his eye. Understand the symbolic meaning.
3
Understand why no detailed answer was given
The text says Idris did not give a detailed answer because Iblis was obstinate (muta'annit). The way of the obstinate is rebuke.
This is the central lesson.
4
Reflect on the principle of recompense
Iblis aimed to extinguish the light of faith, so his physical sight was extinguished. The punishment matched the crime.
This reinforces divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سأل إبليس إدريس : هل يقدر المولى أن يدخل الدنيا في قشرة البندقة؟ فنخسه في عينه بالإبرة ،ففقأها قال بعضهم : وأرجو أن تكون اليمنى، وقال له : إن المولى قادر أن يدخل الدنيا في سَمِّ الخياط، بمعنى أنه يُصغر الدنيا أو يُوسع سَمَّ الخياط وإلا كان مُحالاً، فإنَّ تداخل الأجرام المتكاثفة واجتماعها في حيز واحدٍ مستحيل، وإنما لم يفصل سيدنا إدريس الجواب لإبليس لأنه متعنت وشأن المتعنّت الزجرُ ، وإنما فقأ عينه لأنه أراد بهذا السؤال إطفاء نور الإيمان، فأطفأ نور بصره ؛ الجزاء من جنس العمل.
And Satan (Iblis) asked Idris: 'Is the Master able to put the world into the shell of a hazelnut?' So he poked his eye with a needle and gouged it out. Some said: 'I hope it was the right eye.' He said to him: 'The Master is able to put the world into the eye of a needle, meaning He makes the world small or the eye of the needle wide, otherwise it would be impossible. For the interpenetration of dense bodies and their gathering in one space is impossible. Rather, our master Idris did not give a detailed answer to Iblis because he was obstinate, and the way of the obstinate is to be rebuked. He gouged out his eye because he intended by this question to extinguish the light of faith, so he extinguished the light of his sight; the recompense is of the same kind as the deed.'
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage recounts a story from Islamic tradition. Iblis (Satan) asked Prophet Idris whether Allah can put the entire world into a hazelnut shell. Idris did not answer the question directly; instead, he poked out Iblis' eye with a needle. He then explained that Allah is able to do such a thing by making the world small or the shell large, but if the question implied the interpenetration of dense bodies in one space, that is impossible. However, Idris' initial action was a rebuke because Iblis was being obstinate (muta'annit) and intended to mislead and extinguish faith. The punishment fit the crime: Iblis wanted to extinguish the light of faith, so his physical light was extinguished. This story teaches that not every question deserves a detailed theological response; sometimes the interlocutor's intent is malicious, and the appropriate response is to cut off the debate.

Key Terms
إبليس
إدريس
تداخل الأجرام
المتعنت
الجزاء من جنس العمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every question about divine power must be answered in detail for it to be a valid test of faith.
Correction: The story shows that obstinate questions with malicious intent warrant rebuke, not detailed answers. The response should match the intention.

Checkpoint
Why did Idris not give a detailed answer to Iblis?
A) He did not know the answer.
B) Iblis was being obstinate and deserved rebuke.
C) The question was too difficult.
D) He wanted to test Iblis.

Key Takeaway
The story of Iblis and Idris teaches that obstinate questions aimed at undermining faith should be met with rebuke, and that divine justice ensures the punishment matches the crime.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you question God's power or wisdom, is your heart genuinely seeking understanding, or is there a hint of obstinacy—a desire to argue or justify your own doubts? What would it take for you to sincerely submit even when you don't have all the answers?
?
Idris rebuked Iblis harshly because his question was aimed at extinquishing faith. Have you ever asked a question about Islam not to learn but to challenge or weaken your own or others' faith? If that question is never answered to your satisfaction, will you still obey?
?
Consider the phrase 'the recompense is of the same kind as the deed.' In your own life, have you experienced a situation where your attitude or intention in seeking knowledge was mirrored back to you—either in clarity or in confusion? What does that teach you about the condition of your heart when you approach Allah's signs?

Order Between Will and Power
Explains the relationship between will and power, and begins a proof of the necessity of qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relationship between divine will (irada) and power (qudra) and why the ordering of their relations does not imply temporal sequence in the attributes themselves.

Core Idea
The divine attributes are eternal; only their relations to creation show order.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a subtle point about God's will and power. He explains how the phrase 'on the basis of will' means that what Allah wills, He brings into existence through His power. The key insight is that while the relation of will is eternal and prior, the relation of power is temporal and subsequent, yet this ordering does not affect the attributes themselves, which are both eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'on the basis of will' and how it connects to the rest.
2
Understand the meaning of 'on the basis of will'
The passage states that what Allah specifies with His will, He manifests with His power. So will decides what happens, power brings it about.
This shows the functional relationship between the two attributes.
3
Examine the ordering of relations
The relation (ta'alluq) of will is eternal (azali) and therefore prior to the relation of power, which is actualized (tanjizi) and temporal (hadith). This order is only between the relations, not between the attributes themselves.
Attributes are eternal and beyond temporal succession.
4
Clarify the common misunderstanding
Some might mistakenly think there is temporal ordering between the attributes. The passage corrects this by distinguishing between attributes and their relations.
The attributes are co-eternal; only their relations to creation are sequential.
5
Note the incomplete proof
The passage begins a proof for the necessity (wujud) of qudra but is cut off. Realize that this argument is continued elsewhere or requires further study.
This lesson focuses on the relationship, not the full proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى قولنا : على وفق (الإرادة أن ما خصَّصه الله بإرادته أبرزه بقدرته ، فتعلق الإرادة لكونه أزليّا ، سابق على تعلّق القدرة لكونه تنجيزيا حادثاً، فالترتيب بين التعلقين الصفتين؛ لأن القديم لا ترتيب فيه وإلا كان المتأخر حادثاً، ودليل وجوب
And the meaning of our saying 'in accordance with will' is that what Allah specifies with His will, He manifests with His power. So the relation of will, being eternal, is prior to the relation of power, being actualized and temporal. So there is ordering between the two relations, not between the two attributes, because the eternal has no ordering in it, otherwise the latter would be temporal. And the proof of the necessity...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'on the basis of will' in the definition of qudra. Allah's will (irada) specifies what will happen, and then power brings it about. The relation (ta'alluq) of will is eternal (azali) and thus precedes the relation of power, which is actualized (tanjizi) and temporal (hadith). However, this ordering is between the relations, not between the attributes themselves (irada and qudra are both eternal and uncreated). If one were to think there is order between the attributes, the 'later' would be temporal, which is impossible for divine attributes. The passage then begins to present a proof (dalil) for the necessity (wujud) of qudra, but the text is cut off at 'dilil wujud' (proof of the necessity). This leaves the argument incomplete, but the main point about the relationship between will and power is clear.

Key Terms

التعلق

التنجيزي
الترتيب
دليل الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there is temporal ordering between the divine attributes themselves.
Correction: The passage clarifies that ordering is between the relations (ta'alluq) of the attributes, not between the attributes. Both will and power are eternal and uncreated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the relations of will and power?
A) They are simultaneous.
B) The relation of will is prior because it is eternal.
C) The relation of power is prior.
D) There is no ordering.

Key Takeaway
The ordering between will and power is only in their relations to creation, not between the eternal attributes themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the relation of power is temporal and brings your existence into being moment by moment, do you truly live with the awareness that every breath is a direct act of Allah's power? How would your day change if you reminded yourself of this before every action?

Proper Attribution of Effect to Divine Power
Definition and caution on attribution of effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why effect (ta'thīr) is truly attributed to Allah's essence, not to His attribute of power, and the grave error in implying that power acts independently.

Core Idea
Effect belongs to Allah's essence; power is only a means.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed addresses a subtle but crucial point in Islamic theology: the true source of divine action. While we commonly say 'by His power,' the author clarifies that the real effect originates from the essence itself, and power is merely a cause. Misunderstanding this can lead to serious theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: قدرة (power), تأثير (effect), مجاز (metaphor), سبب (cause).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Distinguish between essence and attribute
Identify the two parties: the essence (dhāt) and the power (qudra). The passage says effect is truly (haqīqa) for the essence, and attributing it to power is metaphorical.
This distinction is the core of the lesson.
3
Understand the prohibition and its severity
Note the forbidden phrases and the consequence of believing power acts independently. Why is this considered disbelief?
The prohibition guards against shirk in attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنفسها. وفي قولنا : (بها) إشارة إلى أن التأثير حقيقة للذات وإسناد التأثير إلى القدرة مجاز لكونها سبباً فيه، ويحرم أن يُقال : القدرة فَعَّالةٌ، أو انظر فعل القدرة، أو نحو ذلك، لما فيه من إيهام أنها المؤثرة بنفسها ، فإن قصد ذلك كفر، والعياذ بالله تعالى.
By itself. And in our saying: (by it) is an indication that the effect is in reality for the essence, and the attribution of effect to power is metaphorical because it is a means in it. And it is forbidden to say: 'Power is an agent,' or 'consider the act of power,' or the like, because of the implication that it is the one effecting by itself. If one intends that, it is disbelief, and we seek refuge with Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and cautionary note on how to properly speak about divine power (qudra). It explains that when we say 'by it' (i.e., 'by His power') in reference to an act, this expression indicates that the true effect (ta'thīr) actually belongs to the divine essence (dhāt), not to the attribute of power. Attributing the effect to power is only metaphorical (majāz) because power is merely a cause (sabab) in the process. Consequently, it is forbidden to say 'Power is an agent (fa''āl)' or 'look at the act of power' or similar phrases, as they falsely imply that power acts independently. If someone intends this meaning, it constitutes kufr (disbelief). The passage thus guards against a common theological error of attributing independent agency to an attribute.

Key Terms
قدرة
تأثير
مجاز
سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the attribute of power (qudra) independently brings about effects without the essence being the true agent.
Correction: The effect is truly from the essence; power is only a means. Attributing independent agency to any attribute is a form of shirk.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of effect (ta'thīr)?
A) It belongs to power (qudra) as the true agent.
B) It belongs to the divine essence (dhāt) in reality.
C) It belongs to the act itself.
D) It is metaphorical in all cases.

Key Takeaway
Only Allah's essence is the true agent; power is merely a means, and attributing independent agency to any attribute is a serious error.

Scope of Divine Power: Excluding the Necessary and Impossible
Sanusi excludes necessary and impossible from power's scope and gives reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power pertains only to possible things and not to the necessary or impossible.

Core Idea
Divine power is not arbitrary; it is limited to what is logically possible.

This passage clarifies the scope of Allah's power. When theologians say power applies to 'every possible thing,' they deliberately exclude both the necessary (Allah Himself) and the impossible. The reasoning is precise: power cannot act on what must exist or what cannot exist. Let's explore this logical boundary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the phrase 'every possible thing' and the two exclusions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two excluded categories
Find where the necessary (wājib) and the impossible (mustaḥīl) are mentioned. Note the reasons given for each.
3
Understand the reasoning for the necessary
The text argues that power cannot annihilate the necessary (it does not accept non-existence) nor bring it into existence (redundant). This is key.
The term 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' means obtaining what is already obtained.
4
Understand the reasoning for the impossible
For the impossible, the opposite applies: power cannot bring it into existence (by definition) and annihilating it is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخرج بقولنا : كلُّ ممكن الواجب والمستحيلُ فلا تتعلق بكلِّ منهما ؛ لأنَّها إِنْ تعلقت بالواجب فلا يصلح أن تعدمه لأنه لا يقبل العدم، ولا يصلح أن توجده لأنه يلزم منه تحصيل الحاصل، وإن تعلقت بالمستحيل فعلى العكس من ذلك
And by our saying: 'every possible thing,' the necessary and the impossible are excluded, so it (power) does not pertain to either of them. Because if it pertained to the necessary, it would not be suitable to annihilate it, for it does not accept non-existence, nor would it be suitable to bring it into existence, for that would entail obtaining what is already obtained. And if it pertained to the impossible, the opposite of that.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the scope of divine power (qudra). It explains that when we say power pertains to 'every possible thing' (kull mumkin), this automatically excludes both the necessary (wājib, i.e., Allah Himself) and the impossible (mustaḥīl). The reasoning is twofold: if power were to pertain to the necessary, it could not annihilate it because the necessary does not accept non-existence, nor could it bring it into existence because that would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). As for the impossible, the opposite applies: power cannot bring it into existence because it is inherently impossible, and annihilating it is irrelevant. Thus, divine power is limited to the realm of possibles (mumkināt).

Key Terms
قدرة
ممكن
واجب
مستحيل
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine power can do absolutely anything, even logical contradictions.
Correction: Divine power only applies to possible things; it cannot act on what is logically necessary or impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does divine power not pertain to the necessary (wājib)?
A) Because the necessary does not exist.
B) Because annihilating it is impossible and bringing it into existence is redundant.
C) Because power is too weak.
D) Because the necessary is not a possible thing.

Key Takeaway
Divine power (qudra) is only applicable to possible things (mumkināt), not to the necessary or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you ever ask Allah for something that is logically impossible—like changing the past or making a contradiction true? How does this lesson correct that tendency, and what does it teach you about the kind of requests that truly honor His power?
?
If divine power is limited to possibles, then your salvation and guidance are possible things. Are you living with the urgency that your faith is something He can give you, but only if you sincerely seek it? Or do you treat it as a passive inevitability?
?
The fact that Allah's power cannot 'obtain what is already obtained' means He never does the meaningless. How does this reshape your understanding of worship? Are there actions in your life that are 'obtaining what is already obtained'—rituals without heart, repetition without reflection—that you need to abandon?

The Story of Iblis and Idris: Impossibility of Interpenetration
Anecdote illustrating impossibility of interpenetration and divine power limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpenetration of dense bodies is inherently impossible and how this illustrates that divine power does not extend to logical impossibilities.

Core Idea
Divine power does not include making the impossible possible.

This lesson uses a striking anecdote from prophetic tradition to illustrate a key theological principle: that God's power is limited to possibilities. The story of Iblis questioning Prophet Idris about putting the world into a nutshell leads to a profound lesson about the nature of impossibility and the moral of punishing obstinate mockery.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the narrative. Notice the sequence: question, physical response, verbal explanation, and moral conclusion.
The story is concise but packs theological depth.
2
Identify the theological principle
Look for the explicit statement about interpenetration of dense bodies. Understand why this is impossible and how it relates to divine power.
The key phrase is 'tadākhul al-ajrām al-mutakāthifa'.
3
Reflect on the moral lesson
Consider why Idris responded with rebuke and gouging out the eye. Connect the punishment ('the recompense is of the same kind as the action') to the concept of just retribution.
This shows the importance of intention in the treatment of obstinate questioners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سأل إبليس إدريس : هل يقدر المولى أن يدخل الدنيا في قشرة البندقة؟ فنخسه في عينه بالإبرة ،ففقأها قال بعضهم : وأرجو أن تكون اليمنى، وقال له : إن المولى قادر أن يدخل الدنيا في سَمِّ الخياط، بمعنى أنه يُصغر الدنيا أو يُوسع سَمَّ الخياط وإلا كان مُحالاً، فإنَّ تداخل الأجرام المتكاثفة واجتماعها في حيز واحدٍ مستحيل، وإنما لم يفصل سيدنا إدريس الجواب لإبليس لأنه متعنت وشأن المتعنّت الزجرُ ، وإنما فقأ عينه لأنه أراد بهذا السؤال إطفاء نور الإيمان، فأطفأ نور بصره ؛ الجزاء من جنس العمل.
And Iblis asked Idris: 'Is the Lord able to put the world into a nutshell?' So he (Idris) jabbed him in his eye with a needle and gouged it out. Some said: 'I hope it is the right one.' And he said to him: 'The Lord is able to put the world into the eye of a needle, meaning He makes the world small or widens the eye of a needle; otherwise it would be impossible. For the interpenetration of dense bodies and their gathering in one place is impossible.' And our master Idris did not give a detailed answer to Iblis because he was obstinate, and the way to deal with the obstinate is rebuke. He gouged out his eye because he (Iblis) intended by this question to extinguish the light of faith, so he (Idris) extinguished the light of his sight; the recompense is of the same kind as the action.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a narrative example illustrating the principle that God's power does not extend to intrinsically impossible things. Iblis asked prophet Idris whether God can put the entire world into a walnut shell. Idris responded by poking out Iblis's eye with a needle, explaining that God can make the world small or enlarge the needle's eye, but interpenetration of dense bodies (tadākhul al-ajrām al-mutakāthifa) in one place is inherently impossible. The story also carries a moral: Iblis asked obstinately (mutaʿannit), aiming to extinguish faith, so his eye was gouged out as a punishment of like kind. The passage thus uses a vivid anecdote to reinforce the theological rule that impossibility (muḥāl) is not an object of divine power.

Key Terms
تداخل الأجرام
متعنت
جزاء من جنس العمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can do absolutely anything, including making two objects occupy the same space.
Correction: Interpenetration of dense bodies is logically impossible and therefore not within divine power, as the story illustrates.

Checkpoint
Why did Idris not answer Iblis's question with a detailed explanation?
A) He did not know the answer.
B) Iblis was obstinate, and the way to deal with the obstinate is rebuke.
C) The question was too difficult.
D) He wanted to test Iblis.

Key Takeaway
The interpenetration of dense bodies is inherently impossible, and divine power is limited to what is logically possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask questions about God's power, are you seeking truth like a sincere seeker, or are you being obstinate like Iblis, trying to justify doubt? What would it cost you to surrender to the limits of created intellect?
?
If Idris responded so harshly to a question aimed at extinguishing faith, how do you respond when doubts or questions arise within you? Do you nourish them or cut them off at the root?
?
The recompense is of the same kind—Iblis tried to extinguish light and had his light extinguished. Think of a time you tried to harm another's faith or your own: what fitting consequence might you already be reaping?

Will and Power: Eternal Decree, Temporal Execution
Sanusi explains the relationship between divine will and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine will and power relate in terms of their attachments, and why the order between them does not imply temporal sequence in the attributes themselves.

Core Idea
Will's eternal decree precedes power's temporal execution, but both attributes are timeless.

This passage clarifies a subtle point in the theology of divine attributes. When we say that everything happens 'in accordance with Allah's will,' we must understand how will and power interact. Sanusi explains that the priority of will over power is in their attachments (taʿalluq), not in the attributes themselves. Let's examine his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Get the literal meaning before interpretation. Notice the key terms: إرادة, قدرة, تعلق, أزلي, تنجيزي.
2
Identify the two attachments
See that will's attachment is eternal (azalī) and power's attachment is temporal (tanzīzī). This is the core distinction.
The word تعلق refers to the relationship of the attribute to its object.
3
Understand the 'order between attachments, not attributes'
Sanusi stresses that the order is between the attachments, not between the attributes themselves, because the eternal has no sequence.
This avoids implying change in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى قولنا : على وفق (الإرادة أن ما خصَّصه الله بإرادته أبرزه بقدرته ، فتعلق الإرادة لكونه أزليّا ، سابق على تعلّق القدرة لكونه تنجيزيا حادثاً، فالترتيب بين التعلقين الصفتين؛ لأن القديم لا ترتيب فيه وإلا كان المتأخر حادثاً،
And the meaning of our saying: 'in accordance with will' is that what Allah has specified by His will He manifests by His power. So the attachment of will, because it is eternal, precedes the attachment of power, because it is temporal and originated. Thus the order is between the two attachments, not between the two attributes; because the eternal has no order in it, otherwise the later would be originated.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage clarifies the relationship between divine will (irāda) and power (qudra). The phrase 'in accordance with will' means that what Allah has destined by His eternal will is brought into existence by His power. The 'attachment' (taʿalluq) of will is eternal (azalī), while the attachment of power is temporal (tanzīzī ḥādith), so there is a precedence in terms of the attachments, not in the attributes themselves. The commentator emphasizes that the order (tartīb) is between the two attachments (taʿalluqayn), not between the two attributes (ṣifatayn), because the eternal attributes have no sequence; otherwise, the later would be originated. This preserves divine timelessness.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
تعلق
أزلي
تنجيزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think will precedes power in time, implying a sequence in Allah's attributes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the precedence is in the attachments (taʿalluqayn), not in the attributes (ṣifatayn). Will's attachment is eternal; power's attachment is temporal. The attributes themselves are both eternal and without order.

Checkpoint
What is the order between the attachments of will and power?
A) The attachment of power is eternal and precedes.
B) The attachment of will is eternal and precedes.
C) There is no order between them.
D) Both attachments are temporal.

Key Takeaway
The attachment of divine will is eternal, while the attachment of power is temporal, so the order is between the attachments, not between the attributes themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face delays in seeing your prayers answered, do you truly believe that Allah's will has already decreed it from eternity, and that His power will execute it at exactly the right moment? What changes in your heart if you live with that certainty?
?
If Allah's will and power are both eternal and without sequence, how does that confront your tendency to grow impatient with His timing? Will you today surrender the timeline of your life to His eternal decree?
?
This lesson teaches that order exists only in the attachments (our created circumstances), not in Allah Himself. When you feel that God is 'taking too long', are you falsely projecting temporal sequence onto the Eternal? Repent from that and trust His timeless wisdom.

Divine Will: Definition, Categories, and Refutations
Defines will, lists six categories of possibles it specifies, and refutes deviant views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine will as an eternal attribute additional to the essence, its six categories of specification, and the refutation of deviant views.

Core Idea
Divine will specifies possibles; it is eternal, not created, and not identical to essence.

Sanusi begins by affirming that Allah has an attribute called will (irāda), which is eternal and distinct from His essence. This attribute is what specifies possible things among their permissible alternatives. He then lists six categories of possibles that are mutually exclusive. Finally, he refutes several heretical views that distort this concept. This passage is foundational for understanding how divine will relates to creation and predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the definition of will
Look for the technical definition: an eternal attribute additional to the essence by which possibles are specified. Note the linguistic and technical meanings.
The key phrase is 'صفة قديمة زائدة على الذات'.
3
Learn the six categories of possibles
The six categories are listed in a poem. Find each category (existence/non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, measures) and understand why they are mutually exclusive.
The text gives examples like white vs. black, Egypt vs. Bulaq, etc.
4
Understand the refutations
The passage refutes several deviant views: those who say will is originated (Karramiyya), identical to essence (Dirar), subsisting without a locus (al-Jubba'i), a negative attribute (al-Najjar), command or knowledge (al-Ka'bi), or pleasure. Note how each phrase in the definition refutes a specific group.
The refutations are organized by the key terms 'قديمة', 'زائدة على الذات', 'قائمة به'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِرَادَةٌ) معطوف على (الوجود) بحذف حرف العطف ؛ أي: واجب له إرادة، ويرادفها المشيئة وهي لغةٌ مطلقُ القصد، وعرفاً صفة قديمة زائدة على الذات به تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه وهو الممكنات الستة المنظومة في قول بعضهم : [من الرجز] المُمْكِنَاتُ المُتَقَابِلاتُ وجودنا والعَدَمُ الصفاتُ أَزْمِنَةٌ أَمْكِنَةٌ جِهاتُ كَذَا المَقَادِيرُ رَوى الثقاتُ ومعنى كونها متقابلات أنها متنافياتٌ؛ فالوجود يقابل العدم وبالعكس، فهما قسم أول وبعض الصفات يقابل بعضاً، فكونه أبيض مثلاً يقابل كونه أسود، وهذا قسم ثانٍ، وبعض الأزمنة تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في زمن الطوفان مثلاً يقابل كونه في زمن سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، وهذا قسم ثالث، وبعض الأمكنة تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في مكان كذا كـ ( مصر ) يقابل كونه في مكان غيره كـ (بولاق)، وهذا قسم رابع، وبعض الجهات تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في جهة المشرق يقابل كونه في المغرب، وهذا قسم خامس وبعض المقادير يقابل بعضاً، فكونه طويلاً مثلاً يقابل كونه قصيراً وهذا قسم سادس. وفي قولنا : (قديمة) ردُّ على الكرامية حيث قالوا بأنها صفة حادثة قائمة بالذات، وفي قولنا : (زائدة على الذات) ردُّ على ضرارٍ من المعتزلة، حيث قال: إنَّها نفس الذات، وفي قولنا : (قائمة (به رد على الجبائي من المعتزلة، حيث قال : إنَّها صفةٌ قائمة لا بمحل وفيه ردُّ أيضاً على النجار حيث قال: (إنها صفة سلبية)، وفسرها بعدم كون الفاعل ساهياً أو مكرهاً، والصفة السلبية لا قيام لها لكونها أمراً عدميًّا، وذهب الكعبي ومعتزلة بغداد إلى أن إرادته تعالى لفعل غيره أمره به، ولفعله: علمه به، وذهب بعضهم إلى أنها الرضا، وسيأتي الرد عليهم بقوله : (وغايرت أمراً . إلخ).
His saying: (will) is conjoined to (existence) with the omission of the conjunction; meaning: He necessarily has will. Its synonym is 'mashī'a', which in language is absolute intention, and in technical term is an eternal attribute additional to the essence, by which the possible is specified with some of what is permissible for it. They are the six mutually exclusive possibles, organized in the saying of some: [in rajaz meter] 'The possible things are mutually exclusive: existence and non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and likewise measures – the reliable have narrated.' And the meaning of their being mutually exclusive is that they are contradictory; existence opposes non-existence and vice versa, so they are the first division. Some attributes oppose each other, e.g., being white opposes being black, and this is the second division. Some times oppose each other, e.g., being in the time of the flood opposes being in the time of our master Muhammad (peace be upon him), and this is the third division. Some places oppose each other, e.g., being in a place like (Egypt) opposes being in another place like (Bulaq), and this is the fourth division. Some directions oppose each other, e.g., being in the east opposes being in the west, and this is the fifth division. Some measures oppose each other, e.g., being tall opposes being short, and this is the sixth division. And in our saying: (eternal) is a refutation of the Karramiyya, who said it is an originated attribute subsisting in the essence. And in our saying: (additional to the essence) is a refutation of Dirar of the Mu'tazila, who said it is the essence itself. And in our saying: (subsisting in Him) is a refutation of al-Jubba'i of the Mu'tazila, who said it is an attribute subsisting without a locus. And in it is also a refutation of al-Najjar, who said it is a negative attribute, and interpreted it as the absence of the agent being forgetful or compelled. And the negative attribute has no subsistence, being a non-existent matter. And al-Ka'bi and the Mu'tazila of Baghdad held that His will for another's act is His command to it, and for His own act is His knowledge of it. And some held that it is pleasure. And the refutation of them will come in his saying: (and it differs from command, etc.).
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive definition and defense of the divine attribute of will (irāda). It begins by stating that 'will' is conjoined to 'existence' in the creed text, meaning will is a necessary attribute of Allah. The synonym is 'mashī'a', which in language means absolute intention. In technical terms, it is an eternal attribute, additional to the essence (zā'ida 'alā al-dhāt), by which a possible thing is specified among its permissible alternatives. The possibles are then enumerated in six categories that are mutually exclusive (mutqābilāt): existence vs. non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and measures. Each category is explained with examples (e.g., white vs. black, time of flood vs. time of Prophet Muhammad, Egypt vs. Bulaq, east vs. west, tall vs. short). The passage then refutes several deviant views: (1) the Karramiyya, who said will is originated; (2) Dirar, who said it is identical to the essence; (3) al-Jubba'i, who said it subsists without a locus; (4) al-Najjar, who said it is a negative attribute (absence of forgetfulness etc.); (5) al-Ka'bi and Baghdad Mu'tazila, who said will for another's act is command, and for His own act is knowledge; (6) some who said it is pleasure. The commentator notes that the refutation of the last groups will come later in the poem with the line 'and it differs from command'.

مشيئة

وجود
عدم
صفات
أزمنة
أمكنة

مقادير
كرامية
ضرار
جبائي
نجار
كعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine will is a created attribute, or identical to the essence, or a mere negative attribute.
Correction: The text asserts will is eternal, additional to the essence, and subsisting in Him — refuting all these misconceptions.

Checkpoint

A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
Divine will is an eternal, non-creaturely attribute that specifies possibles, and any view denying its eternity, essence-addition, or subsistence is rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that divine will specifies every detail of your existence—your time, place, and attributes. Do you live as if your circumstances are intentional choices of Allah, or do you often feel they are random? What would change in your daily gratitude and patience if you fully believed that nothing in your life happens without His specific will? Today, pick one situation you usually complain about and consciously affirm that Allah willed it for you.
?
Some say will is just the absence of forgetfulness—as if Allah is passive. Have you ever acted as if Allah's will is indifferent to your choices, especially in moments of sin? If you truly believed that Allah's will is an active, eternal, specific decision about every moment, would you still persist in that sin with the excuse that 'Allah willed it'? What step will you take today to align your actions with the belief that His will is actively engaged in your life?
?
The text distinguishes divine will from command. Have you ever frustrated yourself by confusing what Allah wills with what He commands—for example, thinking that because He commanded something, He must also will your success in it? How does this clarity free you from despair when obedience is difficult? Will you now obey His commands even when they seem impossible, trusting that His will is at work behind them?

Proof of Divine Power (Qudra)
Sanusi presents a syllogistic proof for the necessity of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic proof that God necessarily possesses the attribute of power (qudra).

Core Idea
God's creation of the temporal proves His eternal power.

This lesson presents a concise syllogism from classical kalam that demonstrates why God must possess the attribute of power. The argument is built on two premises: God is the eternal maker of a temporal creation, and any such maker must have power. From this, we conclude that power is necessary for God. Understanding this proof helps anchor the concept of divine omnipotence in rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam works.
2
Identify the premises and conclusion
Identify the two premises: that God is an eternal maker of a temporal creation, and that any such being must have power. Then see how the conclusion follows.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Consider why the attribute of power is necessarily required for an eternal creator of temporal things. This connects to the broader kalam principle of cause and effect.
This proof is a building block for understanding other divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة له تعالى أن تقول: الله صانع قديمٌ له مصنوع حادث، وكلُّ من كان كذلك تجب له القدرة فالله تجب له القدرة.
Power: for Him, exalted, you say: 'God is an eternal maker, He has a temporal made thing, and everyone who is like that, power is necessary for him, so God power is necessary for Him.'
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage provides a brief syllogistic proof for the attribute of power (qudra) for God. The argument: God is an eternal creator (ṣāni‘ qadīm) who has a temporal creation (maṣnū‘ ḥādith). Any being that is an eternal creator of a temporal creation must necessarily possess the attribute of power (qudra). Therefore, God necessarily possesses power. This is a concise deductive proof typical of kalām.

Key Terms
القدرة (qudra)
صانع قديم (eternal maker)
مصنوع حادث (temporal creation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power here means physical strength
Correction: In kalam, 'power' (qudra) refers to the divine attribute of omnipotence, not physical strength.

Checkpoint
What is the middle premise in the proof of power?
A) God is eternal.
B) God has a temporal creation.
C) Everyone who is an eternal maker of a temporal creation must have power.
D) God is the creator of everything.

Key Takeaway
The existence of temporal creation necessitates that its eternal creator possesses the attribute of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the temporal world around you—your own existence, the changing seasons, the birth and death of living things—do you habitually trace those events back to a necessary, omnipotent Creator, or do you let your mind rest on secondary causes? What would it mean to truly live as if every temporal ‘thing’ demands an eternal Maker with absolute power?
?
If you genuinely believed that God’s power is not just an abstract doctrine but a present, active, and necessary reality, how would your posture of reliance (tawakkul) change today? In what specific area of your life do you functionally rely on your own power instead of God’s?
?
The proof concludes that power is ‘necessary’ for God—not optional, not contingent. How does that necessity confront your own attempts to downplay God's sovereignty when your life feels out of control? Will you let this logical certainty reshape how you face your next moment of helplessness?

Divine Will: Linguistic and Technical Definition
Definition of divine will and its role in specifying possibilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of divine will (irada) and its role in specifying possibilities.

Core Idea
Divine will is the eternal attribute by which God selects one permissible possibility.

After affirming divine attributes in the previous verse, the poet now turns to the attribute of will. This passage explains what 'will' means both linguistically and technically, highlighting its function of specifying which among many permissible possibilities becomes actual in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the grammatical note about the word 'إرادة' being a conjunct.
The passage begins with a grammatical observation.
2
Distinguish linguistic from technical definition
The text gives a linguistic definition ('absolute intention') and a technical definition ('eternal attribute... by which the possible is specified'). Compare them.
The technical definition is more precise for theological discussion.
3
Understand 'specifying the possible'
The will 'specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it.' This means for any possible thing, there are multiple permissible options; will selects one.
This connects will to the six categories in the next passage.
4
Reflect on the attribute being additional to essence
The technical definition says will is 'additional to the essence' (zā'idah 'alā al-dhāt). This is a key Ash'ari position distinguishing attributes from essence.
This counters the Mu'tazili view that attributes are identical to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِرَادَةٌ) معطوف على (الوجود) بحذف حرف العطف ؛ أي: واجب له إرادة، ويرادفها المشيئة وهي لغةٌ مطلقُ القصد، وعرفاً صفة قديمة زائدة على الذات به تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه وهو الممكنات الستة المنظومة في قول بعضهم : [من الرجز]
المُمْكِنَاتُ المُتَقَابِلاتُ وجودنا والعَدَمُ الصفاتُ أَزْمِنَةٌ أَمْكِنَةٌ جِهاتُ كَذَا المَقَادِيرُ رَوى الثقاتُ
ومعنى كونها متقابلات أنها متنافياتٌ؛ فالوجود يقابل العدم وبالعكس، فهما قسم أول وبعض الصفات يقابل بعضاً، فكونه أبيض مثلاً يقابل كونه أسود، وهذا قسم ثانٍ، وبعض الأزمنة تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في زمن الطوفان مثلاً يقابل كونه في زمن سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، وهذا قسم ثالث، وبعض الأمكنة تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في مكان كذا كـ ( مصر ) يقابل كونه في مكان غيره كـ (بولاق)، وهذا قسم رابع، وبعض الجهات تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في جهة المشرق يقابل كونه في المغرب، وهذا قسم خامس وبعض المقادير يقابل بعضاً، فكونه طويلاً مثلاً يقابل كونه قصيراً وهذا قسم سادس.
His saying: '(Will)' is a conjunct to (existence) with the omission of the conjunction; i.e., it is necessary for Him to have will, and it is synonymous with 'mashī'a'. Linguistically it is absolute intention. Technically it is an eternal attribute, additional to the essence, by which the possible is specified with some of what is permissible for it. And it (the possible) is of six categories arranged in the saying of some: [in rajaz meter] 'The possible things are mutual opposites: existence and non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and likewise measurements – the trustworthy narrated.' And the meaning of them being mutual opposites is that they are mutually exclusive. So existence opposes non-existence and vice versa, so they are the first division. Some attributes oppose some others, e.g., being white opposes being black, so this is the second division. Some times oppose some others, e.g., being at the time of the Flood opposes being at the time of our master Muhammad (SAW), so this is the third division. Some places oppose some others, e.g., being in a place like Egypt opposes being in another place like Būlāq, so this is the fourth division. Some directions oppose some others, e.g., being in the direction of the east opposes being in the west, so this is the fifth division. Some measurements oppose some others, e.g., being tall opposes being short, so this is the sixth division.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining the grammatical structure of the word 'إرادة' in the poem and gives its linguistic and technical definitions. It then presents the six categories of possibilities that the divine will specifies. The six categories are: existence vs. non-existence, attributes (such as colors), times, places, directions, and measurements. These are presented as mutually exclusive pairs, meaning that for any possible thing, it can be either one or the other of the pair, and the divine will chooses one. This enumeration is a foundational concept in Ash‘ari theology for understanding how God's will determines contingent realities.

Key Terms
الإرادة (irada)
المشيئة (mashi'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine will is the same as divine knowledge or power.
Correction: The passage defines will as an eternal attribute additional to the essence, distinct from knowledge and power, with the specific function of specification.

Checkpoint
What is the technical definition of divine will (irada) according to this passage?
A) Absolute intention
B) An eternal attribute additional to the essence by which the possible is specified
C) Knowledge of what is best for creation
D) Power to bring about existence

Key Takeaway
Divine will is an eternal attribute that specifies which permissible possibility becomes actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every detail of your existence was specifically chosen by God's will, or do you sometimes live as if things happen by chance? How would your daily anxiety change if you fully internalized that nothing in your life is an accident?
?
If divine will is an eternal attribute that specifies possibilities, then every situation you face is not random but selected by God. When you encounter hardship, do you resent God's choice or trust that His specification is wise? What does your reaction reveal about your belief in His will?
?
The passage says will is 'additional to the essence'—it is a distinct attribute. Do you treat God's will as a comfortable abstract concept, or do you actively submit to His specific choices for you today? What one decision are you making today that you need to align with His will?
