4 min
Six Categories of Possible Things
Enumeration of six mutually exclusive pairs of possibilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize the six mutually exclusive pairs of possibilities that the divine will distinguishes.

Core Idea
Every possible thing involves mutual opposites in six categories: existence, attributes, time, place, direction, and measurement.

Having defined divine will as the specifier of possibilities, the commentator now enumerates the six types of alternatives among which will chooses. This list, often versified, is a standard teaching tool in Ash'ari theology to illustrate the scope of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the rajaz verse
Read the Arabic passage including the rajaz verse that lists the six categories. Then read the translation.
The verse is a mnemonic for the six categories.
2
Understand 'mutual opposites'
The passage explains that these categories are 'mutually exclusive' (متنافيات). For each category, the two alternatives cannot both be true for a single possible thing.
For example, existence and non-existence are opposites.
3
Go through each of the six divisions
The text lists them in order: existence/non-existence, attributes (e.g., white/black), times (e.g., Flood vs. Prophet's era), places (e.g., Egypt vs. Bulaq), directions (east/west), measurements (tall/short). Note the examples.
Each division is illustrated with a concrete example.
4
Connect to divine will
Ask yourself: why are these categories important? Because for every created thing, God's will chooses one from each relevant pair. This shows the comprehensiveness of divine will.
This reinforces the definition of will as specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِرَادَةٌ) معطوف على (الوجود) بحذف حرف العطف ؛ أي: واجب له إرادة، ويرادفها المشيئة وهي لغةٌ مطلقُ القصد، وعرفاً صفة قديمة زائدة على الذات به تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه وهو الممكنات الستة المنظومة في قول بعضهم : [من الرجز]
المُمْكِنَاتُ المُتَقَابِلاتُ وجودنا والعَدَمُ الصفاتُ أَزْمِنَةٌ أَمْكِنَةٌ جِهاتُ كَذَا المَقَادِيرُ رَوى الثقاتُ
ومعنى كونها متقابلات أنها متنافياتٌ؛ فالوجود يقابل العدم وبالعكس، فهما قسم أول وبعض الصفات يقابل بعضاً، فكونه أبيض مثلاً يقابل كونه أسود، وهذا قسم ثانٍ، وبعض الأزمنة تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في زمن الطوفان مثلاً يقابل كونه في زمن سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، وهذا قسم ثالث، وبعض الأمكنة تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في مكان كذا كـ ( مصر ) يقابل كونه في مكان غيره كـ (بولاق)، وهذا قسم رابع، وبعض الجهات تقابل بعضاً، فكونه في جهة المشرق يقابل كونه في المغرب، وهذا قسم خامس وبعض المقادير يقابل بعضاً، فكونه طويلاً مثلاً يقابل كونه قصيراً وهذا قسم سادس.
His saying: '(Will)' is a conjunct to (existence) with the omission of the conjunction; i.e., it is necessary for Him to have will, and it is synonymous with 'mashī'a'. Linguistically it is absolute intention. Technically it is an eternal attribute, additional to the essence, by which the possible is specified with some of what is permissible for it. And it (the possible) is of six categories arranged in the saying of some: [in rajaz meter] 'The possible things are mutual opposites: existence and non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and likewise measurements – the trustworthy narrated.' And the meaning of them being mutual opposites is that they are mutually exclusive. So existence opposes non-existence and vice versa, so they are the first division. Some attributes oppose some others, e.g., being white opposes being black, so this is the second division. Some times oppose some others, e.g., being at the time of the Flood opposes being at the time of our master Muhammad (SAW), so this is the third division. Some places oppose some others, e.g., being in a place like Egypt opposes being in another place like Būlāq, so this is the fourth division. Some directions oppose some others, e.g., being in the direction of the east opposes being in the west, so this is the fifth division. Some measurements oppose some others, e.g., being tall opposes being short, so this is the sixth division.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining the grammatical structure of the word 'إرادة' in the poem and gives its linguistic and technical definitions. It then presents the six categories of possibilities that the divine will specifies. The six categories are: existence vs. non-existence, attributes (such as colors), times, places, directions, and measurements. These are presented as mutually exclusive pairs, meaning that for any possible thing, it can be either one or the other of the pair, and the divine will chooses one. This enumeration is a foundational concept in Ash‘ari theology for understanding how God's will determines contingent realities.

Key Terms
الممكنات المتقابلات (mutually exclusive possibles)
الوجود (existence)
العدم (non-existence)
الصفات (attributes)
الأزمنة (times)
الأمكنة (places)
الجهات (directions)
المقادير (measurements)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the six categories are exhaustive of all possibilities or that they apply to necessary beings.
Correction: The passage explicitly says these are categories of possibles (mumkināt), not necessary beings, and the list is a traditional enumeration covering typical types.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth division of possibilities according to this passage?
A) Attributes
B) Times
C) Places
D) Directions

Key Takeaway
Divine will selects among six types of mutually exclusive alternatives for every contingent thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a recent decision you made where you chose one option over its opposite (e.g., a place to live, a time for an event, a characteristic to develop). In light of this lesson, do you genuinely believe that Allah's will selected that specific alternative for you? How does that change your view of that choice?
?
The lesson lists six categories where every possible thing involves mutual opposites. Think about your own existence, attributes, time, place, direction, and measurements. Is there any aspect of your life from these categories that you have not consciously attributed to Allah's decree? What would it mean for you to acknowledge His will in that area?

How the Ash'ari Definition of Will Refutes Heresies
The commentator shows how each phrase of the Ash'ari definition of will refutes a specific heretical view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each clause of the Ash'ari definition of divine will is a deliberate refutation of deviant sects.

Core Idea
Every phrase in the Ash'ari definition of will excludes a deviant belief.

The Ash'ari definition of divine will is not merely descriptive; it is a precise theological tool that refutes multiple heresies. In this passage, the commentator shows how each phrase—'eternal', 'additional to the essence', 'subsisting in Him', and the negation of being a negative attribute—directly counters the claims of various sects. As you read, notice how the definition is crafted to exclude every wrong position.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Do not worry about details yet; get a sense of the refutational structure.
2
Identify each sect and their claim
List the sects mentioned: Karamiyya, Dirār, al-Jubbā'ī, al-Najjār, al-Ka'bī/Baghdadi Mu'tazila, and 'some'. Note what each said about divine will.
The passage refers to each via the clauses of the definition.
3
Connect each clause to its refutation
For each sect, see which clause of the definition refutes them. For example, 'eternal' refutes the Karamiyya who said will is temporal.
The phrase 'in our saying' signals the refutation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does the definition need to be so precise? How does this show the Ash'ari commitment to defending orthodoxy?
The definition is a shield against innovation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قولنا : (قديمة) ردُّ على الكرامية حيث قالوا بأنها صفة حادثة قائمة بالذات، وفي قولنا : (زائدة على الذات) ردُّ على ضرارٍ من المعتزلة، حيث قال: إنَّها نفس الذات، وفي قولنا : (قائمة به رد على الجبائي من المعتزلة، حيث قال : إنَّها صفةٌ قائمة لا بمحل وفيه ردُّ أيضاً على النجار حيث قال: (إنها صفة سلبية)، وفسرها بعدم كون الفاعل ساهياً أو مكرهاً، والصفة السلبية لا قيام لها لكونها أمراً عدميًّا، وذهب الكعبي ومعتزلة بغداد إلى أن إرادته تعالى لفعل غيره أمره به، ولفعله: علمه به، وذهب بعضهم إلى أنها الرضا، وسيأتي الرد عليهم بقوله : (وغايرت أمراً . إلخ).
And in our saying: (eternal) is a refutation of the Karamiyya, who said that it is a temporal attribute subsisting in the essence. And in our saying: (additional to the essence) is a refutation of Dirār of the Mu‘tazila, who said that it is the essence itself. And in our saying: (subsisting in Him) is a refutation of al-Jubbā’ī of the Mu‘tazila, who said that it is an attribute subsisting without a locus. And in it also is a refutation of al-Najjār, who said that it is a negative attribute, and interpreted it as the agent not being forgetful or coerced, and the negative attribute has no subsistence since it is a non-existential thing. And al-Ka‘bī and the Mu‘tazila of Baghdad held that His will (exalted) for the act of another is His command to it, and for His own act is His knowledge of it. And some held that it is approval. And the refutation of them will come with his saying: 'And it is distinct from command...' etc.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage systematically refutes various deviant positions on the attribute of will by highlighting the implications of each phrase in the standard Ash‘ari definition. The definition states that will is eternal, additional to the essence, subsisting in God, and not a negative attribute. Each clause refutes a specific sect: the Karamiyya who said it is created, Dirār who identified it with essence, al-Jubbā’ī who denied it a locus, al-Najjār who made it negative, and the Baghdadi Mu‘tazila who equated it with command or knowledge. The commentator also mentions the view that will is approval, and promises a future refutation.

Key Terms
الكَرَّامِيَّة (Karamiyya)
ضِرَار (Dirār)
الجبائي (al-Jubbā’ī)
النَّجَّار (al-Najjār)
الكعبي (al-Ka‘bī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari definition of will is simply a description of its nature.
Correction: It is a precise polemical tool; each clause is chosen to exclude a specific heresy.

Checkpoint
Which group said that the divine will is identical to the essence?
A) The Karamiyya
B) Dirār of the Mu‘tazila
C) Al-Jubbā’ī
D) Al-Najjār

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of divine will is a comprehensive refutation of multiple heresies, each clause targeting a specific deviant view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will, do you ever reduce it to a vague feeling or just His approval of what happens? This lesson shows that such a view (like that of the Baghdadi Mu'tazila) is a heresy. Are you willing to correct your understanding even if it feels challenging?
?
The heresies mentioned all stem from human reasoning that went beyond revelation. Where in your own faith might you be tempted to rely on your logic over the precise definitions handed down by the scholars? Will you commit today to learning the exact theological formulations that protect your creed?
?
Al-Najjār thought God's will is simply the absence of forgetfulness—a negative attribute. Do you ever treat God's attributes as merely 'not lacking' instead of affirming real, positive qualities in Him? How can you change this in your worship?

The Three Relations of Divine Will
The commentator enumerates three types of divine will relations and discusses a debated position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of relation of divine will: eternal decisive, potential, and temporal decisive, and the verified position regarding the temporal decisive.

Core Idea
God's will relates to creation in three ways: eternal decisive, potential, and temporal decisive.

This passage introduces key terminology for understanding how God's will relates to created things. These terms are essential for debates about divine foreknowledge and creativity. The commentator defines three types of relation and discusses a debated position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of relation
Read the passage and note the three distinct types of relation: eternal decisive, potential, and temporal decisive. Look for the terms 'تعلق تنجيزي قديم', 'تعلق صلوحي قديم', and 'تعلق تنجيزي حادث'.
The passage uses numbered footnotes to explain each.
2
Understand the eternal decisive relation
Focus on the first relation: God's eternal specification of a thing with the attributes it will have in existence. This is the foundational concept.
3
Understand the potential relation
The potential relation describes the will's eternal suitability to specify either existence or non-existence, while actually specifying one. Note that this does not imply change in God.
4
Understand the temporal decisive relation and verified position
Some scholars posit a new specification at the moment of creation. The verified position argues this is only a manifestation of the eternal specification, not an independent relation. Consider the critique that if it is different, it must be independent.
The commentary provides detailed arguments.
5
Reflect on the debate
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding God's unchanging nature and the act of creation. The debate shows the depth of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلقات الإرادة
وفي قولنا : (تخصص الممكن إشارة للتعلق التنجيزي القديم ؛ وهو تخصيص الله الشيء أزلاً بالصفات التي يعلم أنه يوجد عليها في الخارج.
ولها تعلق (۱) صلوحي قديم وهو صلاحيتها في الأزل للتخصيص مع ثبوت التخصيص بالفعل أزلاً أيضاً، وبعضهم (۲) جعل لها تعلقاً تنجيزيا حادثاً : وهو تخصيص الله الشيء بما تقدم عند إيجاده بالفعل، لكن التحقيق (۳) أن هذا إظهار للتعلق التنجيزي القديم، لا تعلق مستقل.
(۱) قوله : (ولها تعلق إلخ) معنى ذلك أن الله خصص في الأزل وجود الشيء على عدمه؛ أي: رجح وجوده على عدمه، وكان يتأتى له في الأزل أن يرجح بإرادته عدمه على وجوده، لكنه ترك ترجيح العدم على الوجود، ورجّح في الأزل الوجود على العدم، وحاصل ذلك أن إرادة الله في الأزل صالحة لترجيح كل من الوجود والعدم، وفي حال تلك الصلاحية ثابت بالفعل ترجيح الوجود على العدم، ولا منافاة بين الصلاحية وبين ثبوت الترجيح بالفعل؛ لأن معناها أنه تعالى كان يتأتى له أن يرجح العدم على الوجود، وهذا التأتي لا يمنع من ترجيح الطرف الآخر بالفعل،
وما تشعر به هذه العبارات من أن الترجيح الأزلي حادث ... غير مراد . انتهى أجهوري.
(۲) قوله : (وبعضهم ... إلخ) معنى ذلك أن الله تعالى يخصص الشيء عند وجوده، بأن يرجح وجوده على عدمه ترجيحاً آخر غير الترجيح الأزلي مع بقاء الترجيح الأزلي؛ لأن القديم لا يتعدم، فاجتمع عند وجوده ترجیحان وهذا نظير ما قالوه من أن الموجودات مكتشفة الله بعلمه انكشافاً ومع ذلك فهي مكنشفة له أيضاً بسمعه، وكذا بيصره انكشافاً تاماً ، فكما لم يُعْنِ الانكشاف بالعلم عن الانكشاف بالسمع والبصر، كذلك هنا لا يغني الترجيح الأزلي عن الترجيح الحادث، وإن كان بعيداً عن العقل. انتهى أجهوري.
تاما،
(۳) قوله : (لكن التحقيق ... إلخ) صاحب هذا التحقيق يُسلم أن هناك تخصيصاً حادثاً، لكنه ليس مستقلا عن الأول، بل هو مظهر له هذا معنى كلام المحشي، ويرد عليه أن هذا التخصيص الحادث مغاير للتخصيص القديم قطعاً، وحيث كان مغايراً له كان مستقلا، ولم يظهر المراد بعد استقلاله ، فهذا التحقيق غير ظاهر ؛ بل الظاهر إنكار التخصيص الحادث بالكلية، وإظهار التخصيص القديم إنما هو بتعلق القدرة الذي هو الإيجاد والإعدام، انتهى. ثم رأيت في «حاشية الشرقاوي على الهدهدي ما يُؤيد هذا الظاهر، حيث قال فيها : ( وبعضهم نفى التنجيزي الحادث استغناء عنه بالتنجيزي القديم). انتهى أجهوري.
Relations of will
And in our saying: (specification of the possible) is an indication of the eternal decisive relation (al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm); it is God's specification of the thing eternally with the attributes that He knows it will have in externality.
And it has (1) an eternal potential relation (ṣulūḥī qadīm), which is its suitability in eternity for specification with the actual specification also being established eternally. And some (2) made for it a temporal decisive relation (ta‘alluq tanjīzī ḥādith): it is God's specification of the thing at the time of its actual existence. But the verified position (3) is that this is a manifestation of the eternal decisive relation, not an independent relation.
(1) His saying: 'And it has a relation, etc.' means that God specified in eternity the existence of the thing over its non-existence; i.e., He gave preponderance to its existence over its non-existence, and it was possible for Him in eternity to give preponderance to its non-existence over its existence by His will, but He left off giving preponderance to non-existence over existence, and gave preponderance in eternity to existence over non-existence. The upshot is that God's will in eternity is suitable to give preponderance to both existence and non-existence, and in that state of suitability the actual preponderance of existence over non-existence is established. And there is no contradiction between suitability and the establishment of actual preponderance; because its meaning is that He, exalted, could have given preponderance to non-existence over existence, and this possibility does not prevent the actual preponderance of the other side. And what these expressions suggest, that the eternal preponderance is temporal, is not intended. End of al-Ujhūrī.
(2) His saying: 'And some, etc.' means that God specifies the thing at its existence, by giving preponderance to its existence over its non-existence with another preponderance besides the eternal preponderance, while the eternal preponderance remains; because the eternal does not become nonexistent. So at its existence two preponderances come together. This is analogous to what they said: that existents are disclosed to God by His knowledge with a disclosure, and yet they are also disclosed to Him by His hearing, and likewise by His sight with a complete disclosure. So just as disclosure by knowledge does not render disclosure by hearing and sight unnecessary, so here the eternal preponderance does not render the temporal preponderance unnecessary, even if it is far from reason. End of al-Ujhūrī.
(3) His saying: 'But the verified position, etc.' The holder of this verification concedes that there is a temporal specification, but it is not independent of the first; rather it is a manifestation of it. This is the meaning of the glossator's words. And it is objected to him that this temporal specification is definitely different from the eternal specification, and since it is different, it is independent, and the intended meaning is not clear after its independence. So this verification is not apparent; rather the apparent view is the denial of temporal specification entirely, and the manifestation of the eternal specification is only through the relation of power, which is bringing into existence and annihilation. End. Then I saw in the gloss of al-Sharqāwī on al-Hudhudī something supporting this apparent view, where he said: 'And some denied the temporal decisive relation, considering the eternal decisive sufficient.' End of al-Ujhūrī.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage discusses the relations (ta‘alluqāt) of divine will. It distinguishes three concepts: (1) the eternal decisive relation (al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm) – God's eternal specification of a thing with the attributes it will have when created; (2) the eternal potential relation (al-ṣulūḥī al-qadīm) – the will's eternal suitability to specify either existence or non-existence, while actually specifying one; (3) the temporal decisive relation (al-tanjīzī al-ḥādith) – a view by some scholars that at the moment of creation God gives a new specification distinct from the eternal one. The commentator then presents the 'verified position' that the temporal decisive is merely a manifestation of the eternal decisive, not an independent relation. He includes a critique from another source arguing that if the temporal specification is different, it must be independent, so the verification is flawed; the true position is to deny any temporal specification entirely, as the eternal decisive is sufficient. The passage ends with a supporting reference from al-Sharqāwī's gloss.

Key Terms
التعلق التنجيزي (decisive relation)
التعلق الصلوحي (potential relation)
المُحَشِّي (glossator)
التحقيق (verified position)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The potential relation implies that God's will changes or is temporally conditioned.
Correction: The potential relation is about abstract possibility; God's will is eternally suitable for both options, but the actual specification is eternal and unchanging.

Checkpoint
According to the 'verified position' (al-taḥqīq), what is the temporal decisive relation?
A) It is an independent new specification.
B) It is a manifestation of the eternal decisive relation.
C) It is the same as the potential relation.
D) It does not exist at all.

Key Takeaway
The divine will has three distinct types of relation to creation, with a debated view on temporal specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes feel that God's eternal will, set from eternity, is not enough for your specific situation today—that you need a new, immediate sign or intervention from Him? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it look like to trust His eternal decision for this very moment?
?
When you face a difficult trial, do you act as if God has to 'decide now' how to respond, or do you truly believe that He has already, from eternity, willed every detail of this trial and your response? How would your reliance on Him change if you fully embraced the sufficiency of His eternal decisive relation?
?
If God's will does not change or acquire new relations, what does that mean for your repentance? Does it make you more certain that He has already willed your return, or does it feel distant? How can you turn to Him now, knowing that your turning was already specified in His eternal decree?

The Eternal Attachments of the Divine Will
Defines the eternal determinate and potential attachments and explains their compatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two eternal attachments of the divine will: the determinate attachment and the potential attachment, and how they are compatible with God's timeless nature.

Core Idea
The divine will eternally specifies creation without temporal change.

This lesson introduces the first two categories of how Allah's will relates to possible things. The commentator explains that from eternity, Allah's will both determined the existence of things and remained capable of the opposite. Understanding these attachments helps clarify the timeless nature of divine volition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between the two eternal attachments: determinate and potential.
The passage uses technical terms; pay attention to the definitions.
2
Identify the two attachments
Find the definitions of the eternal determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanjizi al-qadim) and the eternal potential attachment (al-ta'alluq al-suluhi al-qadim). Understand how they differ.
The potential attachment emphasizes the suitability of the will to choose either existence or non-existence.
3
Reflect on the compatibility
Ask yourself: How can the will be both actually specified and potentially open in eternity? The commentary explains there is no contradiction.
This highlights the transcendence of God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قولنا : (تخصص الممكن إشارة للتعلق التنجيزي القديم ؛ وهو تخصيص الله الشيء أزلاً بالصفات التي يعلم أنه يوجد عليها في الخارج.
ولها تعلق (۱) صلوحي قديم وهو صلاحيتها في الأزل للتخصيص مع ثبوت التخصيص بالفعل أزلاً أيضاً، وبعضهم (۲) جعل لها تعلقاً تنجيزيا حادثاً : وهو تخصيص الله الشيء بما تقدم عند إيجاده بالفعل، لكن التحقيق (۳) أن هذا إظهار للتعلق التنجيزي القديم، لا تعلق مستقل.
(۱) قوله : (ولها تعلق إلخ) معنى ذلك أن الله خصص في الأزل وجود الشيء على عدمه؛ أي: رجح وجوده على عدمه، وكان يتأتى له في الأزل أن يرجح بإرادته عدمه على وجوده، لكنه ترك ترجيح العدم على الوجود، ورجّح في الأزل الوجود على العدم، وحاصل ذلك أن إرادة الله في الأزل صالحة لترجيح كل من الوجود والعدم، وفي حال تلك الصلاحية ثابت بالفعل ترجيح الوجود على العدم، ولا منافاة بين الصلاحية وبين ثبوت الترجيح بالفعل؛ لأن معناها أنه تعالى كان يتأتى له أن يرجح العدم على الوجود، وهذا التأتي لا يمنع من ترجيح الطرف الآخر بالفعل،
وما تشعر به هذه العبارات من أن الترجيح الأزلي حادث ... غير مراد . انتهى أجهوري. (۲) قوله : (وبعضهم ... إلخ) معنى ذلك أن الله تعالى يخصص الشيء عند وجوده، بأن يرجح وجوده على عدمه ترجيحاً آخر غير الترجيح الأزلي مع بقاء الترجيح الأزلي؛ لأن القديم لا يتعدم، فاجتمع عند وجوده ترجیحان وهذا نظير ما قالوه من أن الموجودات مكتشفة الله بعلمه انكشافاً ومع ذلك فهي مكنشفة له أيضاً بسمعه، وكذا بيصره انكشافاً تاماً ، فكما لم يُعْنِ الانكشاف بالعلم عن الانكشاف بالسمع والبصر، كذلك هنا لا يغني الترجيح الأزلي عن الترجيح الحادث، وإن كان بعيداً عن العقل. انتهى أجهوري.
(۳) قوله : (لكن التحقيق ... إلخ) صاحب هذا التحقيق يُسلم أن هناك تخصيصاً حادثاً، لكنه ليس مستقلا عن الأول، بل هو مظهر له هذا معنى كلام المحشي، ويرد عليه أن هذا التخصيص الحادث مغاير للتخصيص القديم قطعاً، وحيث كان مغايراً له كان مستقلا، ولم يظهر المراد بعد استقلاله ، فهذا التحقيق غير ظاهر ؛ بل الظاهر إنكار التخصيص الحادث بالكلية، وإظهار التخصيص القديم إنما هو بتعلق القدرة الذي هو الإيجاد والإعدام، انتهى. ثم رأيت في «حاشية الشرقاوي على الهدهدي ما يُؤيد هذا الظاهر، حيث قال فيها : ( وبعضهم نفى التنجيزي الحادث استغناء عنه بالتنجيزي القديم). انتهى أجهوري.
And in our saying: (the specification of the possible is an indication of the eternal determinate attachment; which is God's specification of a thing eternally with the attributes which He knows that it will exist upon in the external world. And it (the will) has an eternal potential attachment (1), which is its suitability in eternity for specification along with the actual establishment of specification from eternity as well. And some (2) made for it a temporal determinate attachment: which is God's specification of a thing by what He previously intended at the time of its actual bringing into existence. But the verification (3) is that this is a manifestation of the eternal determinate attachment, not an independent attachment. (1) His saying: (And it has an attachment etc.) means that God specified in eternity the existence of a thing over its non-existence; i.e., He preponderated its existence over its non-existence, and it was possible for Him in eternity to preponderate its non-existence over its existence by His will, but He left off preponderating non-existence over existence, and preponderated in eternity existence over non-existence. The conclusion is that God's will in eternity is suitable for preponderating both existence and non-existence, and in that state of suitability, the preponderance of existence over non-existence is actually established. There is no contradiction between suitability and the establishment of preponderance in actuality, because its meaning is that He, exalted, could have preponderated non-existence over existence, and this possibility does not prevent the preponderance of the other side in actuality. And what these expressions suggest, that the eternal preponderance is temporal... is not intended. End of al-Ujhuri. (2) His saying: (And some etc.) means that God specifies the thing at its existence, by preponderating its existence over its non-existence with another preponderance other than the eternal preponderance, while the eternal preponderance remains, because the eternal does not become non-existent. Thus, at its existence, two preponderances come together. This is analogous to what they said: that existents are disclosed to God by His knowledge as a disclosure, and yet they are also disclosed to Him by His hearing, and likewise by His sight as a complete disclosure. So just as disclosure by knowledge does not dispense with disclosure by hearing and sight, so here the eternal preponderance does not suffice for the temporal preponderance, even if it is far from reason. End of al-Ujhuri. (3) His saying: (But the verification etc.) The proponent of this verification admits that there is a temporal specification, but it is not independent of the first; rather, it is a manifestation of it. This is the meaning of the marginalist's words. It is objected to him that this temporal specification is different from the eternal specification definitively, and since it is different from it, it is independent. The intended meaning does not appear after its independence. So this verification is not manifest; rather, the manifest view is the denial of the temporal specification entirely, and the manifestation of the eternal specification is only by the attachment of power (qudra), which is bringing into existence and annihilation. End. Then I saw in the marginal gloss of al-Sharqawi on al-Hudhudi what supports this manifest view, where he said: 'And some denied the temporal determinate attachment, deeming it unnecessary due to the eternal determinate attachment.' End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the different types of attachment (ta'alluq) of the divine will to possible things. The commentator first defines the eternal determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanjizi al-qadim) as God's eternal specification of a thing with the qualities He knows it will have in existence. Then he introduces the eternal potential attachment (al-ta'alluq al-suluhi al-qadim): the will is eternally capable of specifying, but specification is actually established from eternity. Some scholars also posit a temporal determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanjizi al-hadith): a new specification at the moment of creation. The commentator then provides the verification (tahqiq): this temporal attachment is not independent but merely a manifestation of the eternal one. He then expands on each point by quoting al-Ujhuri's commentary. For the potential attachment, it means God could have chosen non-existence but actually preponderated existence from eternity; there is no contradiction between potentiality and actual determination. For the temporal attachment, some argue that at the moment of existence, a new preponderance arises alongside the eternal one, using the analogy of God's knowledge and His hearing/sight both disclosing objects without one dispensing with the other. The verification counters that the temporal specification is not independent; however, a rebuttal states that since it is different from the eternal one, it must be independent. The preferred view according to the commentator is to deny the temporal specification altogether, and the eternal specification is manifested through the attachment of power (qudra) which creates and annihilates. The passage concludes with support from another commentary that some scholars explicitly denied the temporal determinate attachment as redundant. The key point is that the divine will attaches to possible things in a way that preserves both God's eternal will and the actual coming into existence of things.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التعلق التنجيزي القديم
التعلق الصلوحي القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The potential attachment implies that God's will changes or becomes actual over time.
Correction: The potential attachment is eternal, not temporal. It is a real attribute of the will that does not imply change.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the eternal potential attachment (al-ta'alluq al-suluhi al-qadim) refer to?
A) God's specification of a thing at the moment of creation
B) The will's suitability in eternity to specify either existence or non-existence
C) The actual preponderance of non-existence over existence
D) A temporal change in God's will

Key Takeaway
The divine will attaches to possible things from eternity in two ways: a determinate specification and a potential suitability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's will for your life—every detail, hardship, and blessing—was eternally determined and perfect? How does this conviction change the way you face today's uncertainties? Commit to surrendering one specific worry to His eternal will right now.
?
The potential attachment means God could have willed otherwise—every blessing you have is a free gift, not a necessity. How does this make you feel about your relationship with Him? Will you pause today to thank Him for one blessing you usually take for granted, acknowledging His absolute freedom?
?
If God's will is both determined and free, are you living as though your choices matter under His sovereignty, or do you use predestination as an excuse for spiritual passivity? Identify one area where you have been passive and decide on a concrete action this week that affirms both divine control and your personal responsibility.

The Temporal Determinate Attachment: Debate and Verification
Presents the view of a temporal determinate attachment and critically examines its independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of a temporal determinate attachment of the divine will and the scholarly debate about its independence.

Core Idea
The temporal attachment is either a manifestation of the eternal will or entirely unnecessary.

Some theologians posited a new specification of the will at the moment of creation. This lesson examines that view, the reasoning behind it, and the verification that such an attachment is not independent but a mere manifestation of the eternal will. The discussion also touches on why others deny it entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the three parts: the presentation of the temporal attachment view, the verification that it is a manifestation, and the rebuttal.
Pay attention to the analogy with God's hearing and sight.
2
Understand the analogy
The supporters of the temporal attachment liken it to God's knowledge and His hearing/sight — both disclose objects, but one does not replace the other. Reflect on this reasoning.
3
Analyze the verification and rebuttal
The verification claims the temporal attachment is a manifestation of the eternal one. The rebuttal argues it must be independent because it is different. Which view do you find stronger?
The commentator leans toward denial of the temporal attachment entirely.

Source Text

وفي قولنا : (تخصص الممكن إشارة للتعلق التنجيزي القديم ؛ وهو تخصيص الله الشيء أزلاً بالصفات التي يعلم أنه يوجد عليها في الخارج.
ولها تعلق (۱) صلوحي قديم وهو صلاحيتها في الأزل للتخصيص مع ثبوت التخصيص بالفعل أزلاً أيضاً، وبعضهم (۲) جعل لها تعلقاً تنجيزيا حادثاً : وهو تخصيص الله الشيء بما تقدم عند إيجاده بالفعل، لكن التحقيق (۳) أن هذا إظهار للتعلق التنجيزي القديم، لا تعلق مستقل.
(۱) قوله : (ولها تعلق إلخ) معنى ذلك أن الله خصص في الأزل وجود الشيء على عدمه؛ أي: رجح وجوده على عدمه، وكان يتأتى له في الأزل أن يرجح بإرادته عدمه على وجوده، لكنه ترك ترجيح العدم على الوجود، ورجّح في الأزل الوجود على العدم، وحاصل ذلك أن إرادة الله في الأزل صالحة لترجيح كل من الوجود والعدم، وفي حال تلك الصلاحية ثابت بالفعل ترجيح الوجود على العدم، ولا منافاة بين الصلاحية وبين ثبوت الترجيح بالفعل؛ لأن معناها أنه تعالى كان يتأتى له أن يرجح العدم على الوجود، وهذا التأتي لا يمنع من ترجيح الطرف الآخر بالفعل،

(۳) قوله : (لكن التحقيق ... إلخ) صاحب هذا التحقيق يُسلم أن هناك تخصيصاً حادثاً، لكنه ليس مستقلا عن الأول، بل هو مظهر له هذا معنى كلام المحشي، ويرد عليه أن هذا التخصيص الحادث مغاير للتخصيص القديم قطعاً، وحيث كان مغايراً له كان مستقلا، ولم يظهر المراد بعد استقلاله ، فهذا التحقيق غير ظاهر ؛ بل الظاهر إنكار التخصيص الحادث بالكلية، وإظهار التخصيص القديم إنما هو بتعلق القدرة الذي هو الإيجاد والإعدام، انتهى. ثم رأيت في «حاشية الشرقاوي على الهدهدي ما يُؤيد هذا الظاهر، حيث قال فيها : ( وبعضهم نفى التنجيزي الحادث استغناء عنه بالتنجيزي القديم). انتهى أجهوري.
And in our saying: (the specification of the possible is an indication of the eternal determinate attachment; which is God's specification of a thing eternally with the attributes which He knows that it will exist upon in the external world. Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the different types of attachment (ta'alluq) of the divine will to possible things. The commentator first defines the eternal determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanjizi al-qadim) as God's eternal specification of a thing with the qualities He knows it will have in existence. Then he introduces the eternal potential attachment (al-ta'alluq al-suluhi al-qadim): the will is eternally capable of specifying, but specification is actually established from eternity. Some scholars also posit a temporal determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanjizi al-hadith): a new specification at the moment of creation. The commentator then provides the verification (tahqiq): this temporal attachment is not independent but merely a manifestation of the eternal one. He then expands on each point by quoting al-Ujhuri's commentary. For the potential attachment, it means God could have chosen non-existence but actually preponderated existence from eternity; there is no contradiction between potentiality and actual determination. For the temporal attachment, some argue that at the moment of existence, a new preponderance arises alongside the eternal one, using the analogy of God's knowledge and His hearing/sight both disclosing objects without one dispensing with the other. The verification counters that the temporal specification is not independent; however, a rebuttal states that since it is different from the eternal one, it must be independent. The preferred view according to the commentator is to deny the temporal specification altogether, and the eternal specification is manifested through the attachment of power (qudra) which creates and annihilates. The passage concludes with support from another commentary that some scholars explicitly denied the temporal determinate attachment as redundant. The key point is that the divine will attaches to possible things in a way that preserves both God's eternal will and the actual coming into existence of things.

?

?

?

Divine Attributes

The Divine Will Encompasses All Possible Things, Including Evil
Sanusi states the scope of divine will and recounts a debate with the Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine will attaches to all possible things, including evil, and how the Ash'ari school defended this against Mu'tazili objections.

Core Idea
God's will covers everything that happens, good and evil alike.

Sanusi establishes a fundamental principle: God's will (al-irada) is like His power (al-qudra) in that it only applies to what is possible (al-mumkin), not to the necessary or impossible. He then goes further: the possible includes both good and evil, refuting the Mu'tazila who claimed God's will excludes evil. To illustrate the debate, Sanusi recounts a famous encounter between the Ash'ari theologian Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini and the Mu'tazili judge Abd al-Jabbar. Read on to see how the Ash'ari defended God's absolute will.

Guided Path
1
Read the doctrinal rule
Read the first sentence carefully. Notice how Sanusi defines the scope of divine will by exclusion and inclusion, and states his opposition to the Mu'tazila.
Focus on the categories: possible, necessary, impossible.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the three categories of al-mumkin, al-wajib, al-mustahil. Know that will attaches only to possible, and that possible includes good and evil.
3
Follow the debate
Read the story. Pay attention to each exchange. Abd al-Jabbar asks a series of questions designed to force the Ash'ari into a corner. Observe how al-Isfara'ini responds.
Note the use of counter-questions.
4
Reflect on the theodicy question
The final question about guidance and ruin brings up the problem of evil. How does al-Isfara'ini's answer defend God's justice while affirming His will over all?
Think about ownership and God's sovereignty.
5
Connect to the main point
The entire debate serves to demonstrate the Ash'ari position: God wills all things, including evil, but He is not unjust because He owes nothing to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج بالممكن الواجب والمستحيل، فلا تتعلق بهما الإرادة كالقدرة، وشمل الممكن : الخير والشر، خلافاً للمعتزلة القائلين بأن إرادة الله لا تتعلق بالشرور والقبائح. وحكي أن القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني دخل على الصاحب ابن عباد وعنده الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني، فلما رأى الأستاذ قال: سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء، فقال الأستاذ: سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء، فقال عبد الجبار : أفيريدُ ربنا أن يُعصى ؟ فقال :الأستاذ : أفيُعصى ربنا كرهاً؟ فقال عبد الجبار : أرأيت إن منعني الهدى وقضى عليّ بالردى، أحسن إليَّ أم أساء؟ فقال الأستاذ: إن منعك ما فقد أساء، وإن منعك ما هو له فهو يخص برحمته من يشاء .
And He excluded by 'possible' the necessary and the impossible, so the will does not attach to them like the power (qudra). And it encompasses the possible: good and evil, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that God's will does not attach to evils and ugly acts. And it was narrated that al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadani entered upon al-Sahib Ibn Abbad while al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini was with him. When he saw the ustadh, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is transcendent above obscenity.' The ustadh said: 'Glory be to the One who does not occur in His dominion except what He wills.' Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The ustadh said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed against His will?' Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Tell me, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' The ustadh said: 'If He prevents you from what is yours, He has done evil; but if He prevents you from what is His, then He bestows His mercy upon whom He wills.'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage first states a doctrinal rule: the divine will (al-irada) attaches only to possible things (al-mumkin), not to the necessary (al-wajib) or the impossible (al-mustahil), just like divine power (al-qudra). Moreover, it encompasses both good and evil within the possible. This is a refutation of the Mu'tazila who deny that God's will includes evil acts. To illustrate the debate, the author recounts a famous story. The Mu'tazili judge Abd al-Jabbar enters upon the vizier Sahib Ibn Abbad, in the presence of the Ash'ari theologian Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. Abd al-Jabbar begins by glorifying God for being transcendent above obscenity (fahsha'), implying that God's will is not involved in evil. Al-Isfara'ini responds by glorifying God for the fact that nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills, affirming God's absolute will over all events. Abd al-Jabbar then asks directly: does God will to be disobeyed? The Ash'ari replies with a counter-question: is God disobeyed against His will? (implying that since disobedience happens, it must be under God's will). Abd al-Jabbar then asks a question about theodicy: if God prevents guidance and decrees ruin, is that good or evil? Al-Isfara'ini answers with a distinction: if He prevents what is rightfully yours, He would have done evil; but what God gives is His own gift, not owed to anyone. He gives mercy to whom He wills. This shows that God's will encompasses all events, including evil, but He is not unjust because He owes nothing.

الممكن
الواجب
المستحيل
المعتزلة
الشرور
القبائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will does not include evil acts.
Correction: According to the Ash'ari school, God's will attaches to all possible things, including evil and good. The story illustrates that even disobedience occurs by God's will, yet God is not unjust because He owes nothing.

Checkpoint

God's will encompasses all possible events, including evil, and this does not contradict His justice because He owes nothing to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If God's will includes every sin you have committed, how does that affect the way you view your own disobedience? Does it make you more grateful for His mercy, or does it tempt you to excuse your sins? Be honest—what do you need to change in your attitude toward your own failures?

When Is It Permissible to Attribute Evil to God?
Ruling on attributing evil and base things to God, permitting only in teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on attributing evil and base things to God and why it is restricted to teaching contexts.

Core Idea
Speaking about God demands utmost respect—even when affirming His power.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid commentary addresses a sensitive point: can we say that God creates evil or base things? The answer is nuanced, showing the balance between theological accuracy and reverent speech. As you read, notice how the scholars prioritize adab (etiquette) in speech about Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Read the passage and note that the scholars differed on attributing evil acts to God. The preponderant view allows it only in a teaching context.
The key term is 'جواز النسبة' (permissibility of attribution).
2
Understand the condition
The same ruling applies to base things like creating monkeys and pigs. The condition 'مقام التعليم' (teaching context) is crucial.
Outside teaching, such statements are disrespectful.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives specific phrases that are prohibited unless used for teaching. Reflect on why these examples were chosen.
The examples highlight creatures often deemed lowly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف العلماء في جواز نسبة فعل الشرور والقبائح إليه تعالى، والراجح جواز ذلك في مقام التعليم لا في غيره، وهذا الخلاف جارٍ أيضاً في نسبة الأمور الخسيسة إليه تعالى والأصح الجواز في مقام التعليم لا في غيره، فلا يجوز أن يقال: الله خالق القردة والخنازير، وسبحان من رزق الهدهد ومن دبب الشوك، إن لم يكن في مقام التعليم.
And the scholars differed regarding the permissibility of attributing the act of evils and ugly acts to Him, exalted. The preponderant view is the permissibility of that in the context of teaching, not in other contexts. And this disagreement also applies regarding the attribution of base things to Him, exalted. The soundest view is the permissibility in the context of teaching, not in other contexts. So it is not permissible to say: 'Allah is the creator of monkeys and pigs,' and 'Glory be to the One who provided for the hoopoe and the shoka tree,' unless it is in the context of teaching.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage addresses a delicate theological question: can we attribute evil and base things to God? The scholars differ, but the preponderant (rajih) opinion is that it is permissible only in a teaching context (maqam al-ta'lim), i.e., when instructing students about God's all-encompassing will and power. Outside of such a context, it is disrespectful and not allowed. The same ruling applies to base things (al-umur al-khasisa) like creating monkeys and pigs, or providing for the hoopoe bird and the thorn tree. The author gives examples of phrases that should not be used unless for teaching. This shows the importance of adab (etiquette) in speaking about God, even when affirming His omnipotence.

Key Terms

الشرور
القبائح
مقام التعليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think it is always permissible to attribute evil to God because He creates everything.
Correction: The passage limits such attribution to teaching contexts only, out of respect.

Checkpoint

B) Only in a teaching context
C) Never, because it is disrespectful
D) Only when the animals are mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Attributing evil or base things to God is permissible only in a teaching context due to the need for reverent speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Rational Proof for the Necessity of the Divine Will
Rational proof for the necessity of divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that establishes the divine will as a necessary attribute of God.

Core Idea
God's will is proven by reason, consensus, and language.

This lesson presents a concise rational argument for the divine attribute of will. The author uses a syllogism based on God's creative act, supported by universal agreement and linguistic necessity. You will see how reason alone demands that God possesses will, and how the Mu'tazilite objection is addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the proof become clear.
Notice the two main parts: a syllogism and then additional evidence.
2
Map the syllogism
Identify the major premise (everything that acts by choice has will), the minor premise (God acts by choice), and the conclusion (God has will).
This is a classical Aristotelian syllogism.
3
Consider the consensus argument
The author notes that everyone agrees God is 'willing' (murid) and this is widespread in scripture. Why is this relevant?
Consensus (ijma) is a secondary proof.
4
Analyze the linguistic point
The word 'willing' linguistically implies the attribute of will. The Mu'tazilites deny this, but the author says it's inconceivable.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite position.
5
Synthesize the proof
Understand how reason, consensus, and language together affirm the necessity of God's will.
This is a comprehensive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على وجوب الإرادة له تعالى أن تقول: الله صانع للعالم بالاختيار، وكل كان كذلك يجب له الإرادة، فالله تجب له الإرادة، وأيضاً فقد اتفق كل على إطلاق القول بأنه تعالى مريد، وشاع ذلك في كلامه وكلام أنبيائه عليهم الصلاة والسلام. ولا يفهم من قولنا : (مريد) بحسب اللغة إلا ذات ثبت لها الإرادة، إذ لا يُتعقل مريد بلا إرادة وإن نازع في ذلك المعتزلة.
And the proof for the necessity of will for Him, exalted, is that you say: God is the Maker of the world by choice (bi'l-ikhtiyar), and everything that is such must have will; therefore God must have will. Also, everyone has agreed upon the application of the statement that He, exalted, is 'willing' (murid), and this is widespread in His speech and the speech of His prophets, peace be upon them. And nothing is understood from our saying 'willing' according to language but an essence that has will established for it, for it is inconceivable that one be willing without will, even if the Mu'tazila disputed that.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof (dalil) for the divine attribute of will (irada). The proof is a syllogism: (1) God is the maker (sani') of the world by choice (ikhtiyar), not by necessity. (2) Anyone who acts by choice must have will, because will is the faculty of choice. (3) Therefore, God must have will. The author also cites consensus (ittifaq) and scriptural evidence: everyone, including the Mu'tazila, calls God 'willing' (murid), and this is frequent in the Quran and prophetic traditions. The linguistic meaning of 'willing' necessarily implies the attribute of will in the essence, because it is inconceivable to speak of a willing being without will. The Mu'tazila, however, dispute this by interpreting 'willing' as not requiring an attribute, but the author rejects that.

Key Terms
وجوب الإرادة
الاختيار
مريد
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that calling God 'willing' does not imply a real attribute of will.
Correction: The author argues that linguistically and rationally, a being cannot be described as willing without possessing the attribute of will.

Checkpoint
The proof in this passage is based on the premise that God is:
A) the creator of the world by necessity
B) the maker of the world by choice
C) the provider of the world by compulsion
D) the governor of the world by chance

Key Takeaway
God must have will because He acts by choice, and language and consensus confirm this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life, even your struggles, occurs by God's will and choice, or do you sometimes think things happen by chance? How would your trust in God deepen if you lived every moment knowing that His will is behind all things?
?
The Mu'tazila denied the attribute of will to protect God's unity. Have you ever rejected a truth about God because it seemed to conflict with your reason? Will you accept God as He has described Himself, even when it challenges your intellect?
?
If God is the Maker of the world by choice, then your existence is not accidental. How does this confront your sense of purpose and accountability? What would it mean for your daily choices to align with His will?

Difference Between Divine Will and Command
The author distinguishes divine will from divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine will (irada) and divine command (amr), and why they are not identical nor mutually necessitating.

Core Idea
Divine will and command are distinct; one does not require the other.

The author now clarifies a crucial distinction in divine attributes: the will of Allah (irada) is not the same as His command (amr). This may seem subtle, but it has major theological implications, especially regarding divine justice and human responsibility. The passage provides a concise statement and an example to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to get the basic meaning.
Notice the concise style: the author states the distinction then gives an example.
2
Identify the two key terms
Find the terms 'will' (irada) and 'command' (amr) in the passage. They are the subject of the distinction.
3
Understand the three possibilities
The passage negates two possibilities: identity and necessary entailment. Consider what the relationship would be if they were identical or if one entailed the other.
The passage says they are neither identical nor does one necessitate the other.
4
Analyze the example of faith
Look at the example: God wills the faith of those He knows will believe, and He also commands faith. This shows a case where both exist, but they remain distinct.
The example illustrates that will and command can coincide but are not the same.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Think about why this distinction matters. For instance, it allows for divine commands that are not willed for certain individuals, which relates to predestination and accountability.
This connects to broader discussions of divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَغَايَرَتْ أَمْراً ) أي : خالفت وباينت الإرادة أمراً، بمعنى أنها ليست عينه ولا مستلزمة له، فقد يريد ويأمر كإيمان من علم الله منهم الإيمان، فإنه تعالى أراده
His saying: (And it differs from command) i.e., the will opposes and is distinct from command, in the sense that it is not identical to it nor does it necessitate it. For He may will and command, like the faith of those of whom God knew that they would believe, for He, exalted, wills it.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage explains that the attribute of divine will (irada) is distinct from divine command (amr). They are not the same, and one does not entail the other. An example: God wills the faith of those whom He knows will believe, and He also commands faith. But there are cases where He wills something without commanding it, or commands something without willing it (e.g., He commands obedience but may not will it from some individuals). The example given is that God wills the faith of the believers and also commands them to believe. The passage emphasizes that will and command are separate attributes with distinct implications.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الأمر
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will and command are the same thing or command necessarily implies will.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that will is not identical to command (laysat 'aynahu) and does not necessitate it (wa la mustalzimatan lahu).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between divine will and divine command?
A) They are identical
B) Command necessitates will
C) They are distinct and not mutually necessitating
D) Will is a subset of command

Key Takeaway
Divine will and command are distinct attributes; neither is identical to the other, and neither necessitates the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a sin you committed that you knew was forbidden. Did you ever justify it by thinking, 'If Allah didn't will it, it wouldn't have happened'? How does knowing that Allah's will is not the same as His command place the responsibility back on you, and what will you do differently now?
?
If Allah's command does not necessitate His will, then your obedience is a real choice. What command of Allah have you been avoiding, and what step will you take today to fulfill it regardless of your feelings?

The Distinction Between Divine Will and Knowledge
Proves will is not identical to knowledge, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will is distinct from divine knowledge and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Will and knowledge differ in their objects: knowledge encompasses all, will only the contingent.

In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction between two divine attributes: will (irada) and knowledge (ilm). He argues that they cannot be identical because they relate to different categories of things. This distinction is used to refute a Mu'tazilite claim that equates God's will for His own act with His knowledge. Read on to see the logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: ilm, irada, wajib, mustahil, ja'iz.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Identify the core argument
The author argues that will is not identical to knowledge because their objects differ. Find the sentence that states this.
The difference is that knowledge covers necessary, impossible, and contingent, while will only covers contingent.
3
Understand the refutation
See how this distinction is used to reject the Mu'tazilite view of al-Ka'bi. Why is their position untenable?
Because if will were knowledge, it would have to relate to impossible things, which it does not.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this matters for Islamic theology. Distinguishing attributes prevents misunderstanding God's nature.
It preserves the uniqueness of each attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَعِلْماً) أي: وغايرت الإرادة علماً بمعنى أنها ليست عين العلم، ولا مستلزمة له؛ لتعلق العلم بالواجب والمستحيل كالجائز، ولا تتعلق الإرادة إلا بالجائز، وغرضه بذلك الردُّ على من زعم من المعتزلة أن إرادته تعالى لفعله علمه به فرد بمغايرة الإرادة للأمر وللعلم على الكعبي ومعتزلة بغداد في قولهم : (إن إرادته تعالى لفعل غيره أمره به، وإرادته لفعله علمه به ، كما قاله المؤلف في «كبيره».
His saying: 'and knowledge' means that will is distinct from knowledge in the sense that it is not identical to knowledge nor entailed by it; because knowledge pertains to the necessary, the impossible, and the contingent, while will pertains only to the contingent. And his purpose with that is to refute those who claimed from the Mu'tazila that His will, Exalted is He, for His act is His knowledge of it. So he refuted by the distinctness of will from command and from knowledge against al-Ka'bi and the Mu'tazila of Baghdad in their saying: 'His will, Exalted is He, for another's act is His command to it, and His will for His act is His knowledge of it,' as the author said in his 'Kabir'.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the distinction between divine will (irada) and knowledge (ilm). The author explains that will is not identical to knowledge nor does it entail knowledge, because knowledge pertains to all three rational categories – wajib (necessary), mustahil (impossible), and ja'iz (contingent) – whereas will only relates to the contingent. This refutes the Mu'tazilite position of al-Ka'bi and the Baghdad school, who held that God's will for another's act is His command, and His will for His own act is His knowledge. The distinction is established by the difference in objects: knowledge includes what is impossible (e.g., God ceasing to exist) which cannot be willed, while will does not.

Key Terms
علم (ilm)
إرادة (irada)
واجب (wajib)
مستحيل (mustahil)
جائز (ja'iz)
الكعبي (al-Ka'bi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will and knowledge are the same because both are eternal attributes.
Correction: They are distinct because their objects differ: knowledge includes necessary and impossible, will only contingent.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is will distinct from knowledge?
A) Will is temporal while knowledge is eternal
B) Will only pertains to contingent things, while knowledge includes necessary and impossible
C) Knowledge is acquired while will is innate
D) Will can change while knowledge cannot

Key Takeaway
Divine will and knowledge are distinct because knowledge encompasses all rational possibilities, while will is limited to contingent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you know is wrong but still commit. How does your knowledge of its wrongness differ from your will to stop? What does that gap reveal about your heart's condition before Allah? Will you sincerely repent and ask Allah to align your will with His knowledge today?
?
You know that Allah knows everything about you—your secrets, your struggles. But do you truly believe that His will for you is distinct from His knowledge? How does that affect your trust in His decree? What if His will brings hardship while His knowledge saw it? Will you submit your will to His?
?
The Mu'tazila confused knowledge with will, thinking that if God knows something will happen, He must will it. Have you ever fallen into a similar confusion—assuming that because you know what is right, you automatically will to do it? When have you failed to act on your knowledge of a good deed? What will you change today to make your will follow your knowledge of truth?

Distinction Between Divine Will and Pleasure
Proves will is not identical to divine pleasure, with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine will and divine pleasure, and why they are not identical attributes.

Core Idea
God may will what He is not pleased with.

This lesson tackles a common confusion: if God wills everything that happens, does that mean He is pleased with everything? Through a crisp example, the author shows that will and pleasure are distinct. Disbelief is willed by God yet He abhors it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: irada (will) and rida (pleasure).
The passage is dense; reading it twice helps.
2
Identify the two attributes and their definitions
The text states that will is distinct from pleasure. Pleasure is defined as 'acceptance of a thing and rewarding for it'. Will is not defined here but is implied to be the decree of events.
Pleasure involves approval and reward; will involves decree regardless of approval.
3
Examine the example of disbelief
The author gives the example of the disbelief of disbelievers. God wills it (it occurs by His decree) but He is not pleased with it. This proves the attributes are not identical.
This example directly refutes those who equate will with pleasure.
4
Apply the distinction
Think of other events that God wills but is not pleased with, such as sins or tests. The distinction safeguards God's justice and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (وَالرِّضَا) أي: وغايرت الإرادة رضاه تعالى ؛ وهو قبولُ الشيء والإثابة عليه، وغرضه بذلك الردُّ على من فسّر الإرادة بالرضا، فإن الإرادة قد تتعلَّق بما يرضى به الله تعالى كالكفر الواقع من الكفار ؛ فإنه تعالى أراده ولا يرضى به .
And His saying: 'and pleasure' means that will is distinct from His pleasure, Exalted is He; and it is acceptance of a thing and rewarding for it. And his purpose with that is to refute those who interpret will as pleasure, for will may relate to something that God, Exalted is He, is not pleased with, like the disbelief occurring from the disbelievers; for He, Exalted is He, willed it and is not pleased with it.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine will (irada) is distinct from divine pleasure (rida). Pleasure is defined as acceptance of a thing and rewarding for it. The author refutes those who equate will with pleasure by pointing to an example: God wills the disbelief of the disbelievers (as part of His decree) but He is not pleased with it; in fact, He abhors it. Therefore, will and pleasure are distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Attribute of Divine Knowledge: One Eternal Attachment
Defines God's knowledge as eternal, all-encompassing, with one decisive attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge must have a single eternal decisive attachment, and why potential or temporal attachments are impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is a single timeless act encompassing all things eternally.

Al-Sanusi defines the divine attribute of knowledge and clarifies its attachment to all things. He emphasizes that how God knows does not change with the object's temporal state. This passage introduces the critical distinction between a single eternal decisive attachment and the rejected alternatives of potential or temporal attachments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get its rhythm, then the literal translation. Notice the matn line and the commentary's definition.
The matn line warns against saying knowledge is acquired.
2
Identify the three categories of objects
The passage says knowledge pertains to all wajibat, ja'izat, mustahilat. Identify what these categories refer to.
These are the same three rational judgments from earlier lessons.
3
Understand the single eternal decisive attachment
Focus on the argument: knowledge attaches timelessly; variations in the known do not change the attachment. Why are potential and temporal attachments rejected?
The key is that any change in knowledge would imply prior ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣. صفة العلم ودليلها] ٢٨ - وَعِلْمُهُ وَلا يُقَالُ: مُكْتَسَبْ فَاتَّبَعْ سَبِيلَ الحَقِّ وَاطرَح الرِّيَب قوله : (وَعِلْمُهُ) معطوف على الوجود أي وواجب له علمه، وما قاله الشارح فهو حلُّ معنى لا حل إعراب كما تقدم نظيره، وهو (صفةٌ أزلية متعلقة بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات على وجه الإحاطة على ما هي به من غير سبق خفاء). وقولنا : (متعلقة ... إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى تعلق العلم بجميع الأشياء تعلقاً تنجيزيا قديماً، فيعلمُ الله سبحانه وتعالى الأشياء أزلاً على ما هي عليه؛ وكونها وجدت في الماضي أو موجودة في الحال أو توجد في المستقبل، أطوار في المعلومات لا توجب تغيراً في تعلق العلم فالمتغير إنما هو صفة المعلوم لا تعلق العلم، وليس له تعلق صلوحي ولا تنجيزي حادث، وإلا لزم الجهل؛ لأنَّ الصالح لأن يعلم: ليس بعالم، والتنجيزي الحادث يستلزم سبق الجهل، هذا ما عليه السنوسي ومن تبعه، وهو الصحيح.
[3. The attribute of knowledge and its proof] 28 - And His knowledge – and it is not said: acquired – so follow the path of truth and abandon doubts. His saying: 'and His knowledge' is coordinated with existence, i.e., His knowledge is necessary for Him. What the commentator said is a semantic resolution, not a syntactic one, as its like preceded. And it is (an eternal attribute pertaining to all necessary, contingent, and impossible things in an encompassing manner as they are, without prior concealment). And our saying 'pertaining … etc.' contains an indication that knowledge pertains to all things with an eternal decisive (tajziziy) attachment. So God, Exalted and High, knows things eternally as they are: whether they existed in the past, exist in the present, or will exist in the future; variations in the known things do not necessitate a change in the attachment of knowledge. What changes is only the attribute of the known, not the attachment of knowledge. It has no potential (suluhi) attachment nor a temporal decisive attachment, otherwise ignorance would follow because the one fit to know is not knowing, and a temporal decisive attachment entails prior ignorance. This is the position of al-Sanusi and those who follow him, and it is correct.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of knowledge (ilm) and a statement of the correct position regarding its attachment (tawalluq). It begins with the matn line from the poem: 'And His knowledge – and it is not said: acquired – so follow the path of truth and abandon doubts.' The commentary explains that knowledge is an eternal (azali) attribute that encompasses all necessary (wajibat), contingent (ja'izat), and impossible (mustahilat) things comprehensively, without prior ignorance. Knowledge has a single eternal decisive (tajziziy) attachment, meaning that God knows all things from eternity in a definitive manner. Temporal variations (past, present, future) in the known things do not change the attachment of knowledge; only the known object's state changes. The author rejects two alternative views: (a) a potential (suluhi) attachment – because something that is only potentially knowing is not actually knowledgeable, which implies ignorance; (b) a temporal decisive attachment – because if knowledge attaches at the time of the object's existence, prior ignorance would be entailed. Thus the correct position, held by al-Sanusi and his followers, is that there is only one eternal decisive attachment.

صفة أزلية (sifa azaliyya)

واجبات (wajibat)
جائزات (ja'izat)
مستحيلات (mustahilat)
تعلق صلوحي (ta'alluq suluhi)
سنوسي (al-Sanusi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes as events occur or is potential for future events.
Correction: Al-Sanusi argues that divine knowledge is a single timeless act; variations in the known do not affect the attachment, and a potential attachment would imply ignorance.

Checkpoint

A) A potential (suluhi) attachment for future things
B) A temporal decisive (tajziziy hadith) attachment
C) An eternal decisive (tajziziy qadim) attachment only
D) Three different types of attachment

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is an eternal attribute with a single decisive attachment to all things, unchanging with the states of the known.

?
The lesson states that God's knowledge has no potential attachment—it is always actual and decisive. Are there areas of your life where you act as if God's knowledge is incomplete or that you can hide something? How can you live with the constant awareness that God sees everything?

The Three Attachments to Divine Knowledge and the Objection
Enumerates three attachments to divine knowledge and records an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative view of three attachments to God's knowledge and the objection raised against the potential attachment.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge cannot be merely potential—it is actual or not knowledge at all.

This passage introduces a nuanced classification of how God's knowledge relates to different objects: His essence, His attributes, and created things before they exist. The author presents an alternative view and then records a critical objection that challenges the coherence of a 'potential' attachment. Pay close attention to the distinction between decisive and potential attachments, as it is central to the debate.

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative view that divine knowledge has three attachments: (1) an eternal decisive attachment to God's essence and attributes; (2) an eternal potential attachment to other beings before they exist (since knowledge is suitable to pertain to them but does not actually pertain before existence, because knowing something before it exists would be ignorance); and (3) a claim that knowledge of future existence is an eternal decisive attachment (this seems to correct a mischaracterization). The author then records an objection from 'the earlier ones' (al-awwalun) that a potential attachment would imply ignorance, because something only capable of knowing is not actually knowing. The answer to that objection is cut off, but it begins by saying that the actual existence of Zayd (as an example) is not fit to be... (likely 'known before its existence' or similar). The argument appears to be that the object of knowledge must exist for actual knowledge, but potential knowledge is not actual.
