The Eternal Attachment of Divine Knowledge
Defines divine knowledge and explains its single eternal attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge has a single eternal actual attachment to all things, and how this preserves God's perfection.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is a single eternal act covering all things without change.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational doctrine of Sunni theology: the nature of God's knowledge. The commentator defines it as an eternal attribute that attaches to everything — past, present, future — in one timeless act. This teaching refutes any notion that God's knowledge changes or develops over time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the definition and key arguments.
Notice the technical terms: تعلق, تنجيزي, صلوحي.
2
Identify the core definition
Find where the commentator defines divine knowledge as an eternal attribute. Note that it encompasses all necessary, possible, and impossible things.
3
Understand the attachment types
The passage discusses potential (صلوحي) and temporal (حادث) attachments and why they are rejected. Focus on the reasoning: they would imply ignorance.
The correct view is one eternal actual (تنجيزي قديم) attachment.
4
Reflect on the alternative view
The passage mentions a three-attachment view and refutes it. Consider why the refutation hinges on the concept that non-attachment to something unknowable is not ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَعِلْمُهُ) معطوف على الوجود أي وواجب له علمه، وما قاله الشارح فهو حلُّ معنى لا حل إعراب كما تقدم نظيره، وهو (صفةٌ أزلية متعلقة بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات على وجه الإحاطة على ما هي به من غير سبق خفاء). وقولنا : (متعلقة بجميع ... إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى تعلق العلم بجميع الأشياء تعلقا تنجيزيا قديما، فيعلمُ الله سبحانه وتعالى الأشياء أزلاً على ما هي عليه؛ وكونها وجدت في الماضي أو موجودة في الحال أو توجد في المستقبل، أطوار في المعلومات لا توجب تغيراً في تعلق العلم فالمتغير إنما هو صفة المعلوم لا تعلق العلم، وليس له تعلق صلوحي ولا تنجيزي حادث، وإلا لزم الجهل؛ لأنَّ الصالح لأن يعلم: ليس بعالم، والتنجيزي الحادث يستلزم سبق الجهل، هذا ما عليه السنوسي ومن تبعه، وهو الصحيح، وجعل بعضهم له ثلاث تعلقات : تنجيزي قديم بالنسبة لذات الله وصفاته . وصلوحي قديم بالنسبة لغيره تعالى قبل وجوده فإن العلم صالح لأن يتعلق بوجوده ولم يتعلق بوجوده بالفعل ; لأنَّ علم وجود الشيء قبل وجوده جهل; نعم بأنه سيكون تنجيزي قديم، وأما قول الأولين : لو كان له تعلّق صلوحي لزم الجهل لأن الصالح لأن يعلم ليس بعالم ، فجوابُهُ أن ثبوت الوجود لزيد بالفعل لا يصلح أن يكون معلومًا قبل وجوده بالفعل، وعدم تعلقِ العلم بشيء لا يصلح أن يكون معلوماً، لا يعد جهلاً، كما أن عدم تعلق القدرة بالمستحيل، لا يعد عجزاً. وتعلق تنجيزي حادث بالنسبة لغيره تعالى بعد وجوده بالفعل، لكن الحق أنه ليس له إلا تعلق تنجيزي قديم .
His statement: (and His knowledge) is a conjunction to existence, meaning: His knowledge is necessary for Him. What the commentator said is a solution of meaning, not a solution of parsing, as its equivalent preceded. It is (an eternal attribute connected to all necessary, possible, and impossible things comprehensively as they are without prior concealment). And our statement: (connected to all ... etc.) contains an indication that the attachment of knowledge to all things is an actualizing eternal attachment. So God, exalted, knows things eternally as they are; whether they existed in the past, exist in the present, or will exist in the future — modalities in the known things do not necessitate a change in the attachment of knowledge. The changing is only the attribute of the known, not the attachment of knowledge. He has no potential attachment nor a temporally originated actualizing attachment. Otherwise ignorance would follow, because the one who is potent to know is not knowing, and temporally originated actualizing attachment necessitates prior ignorance. This is the view of Sanusi and those who follow him, and it is correct. Some people assigned to Him three attachments: an actualizing eternal attachment with respect to God's essence and attributes; a potential eternal attachment with respect to other than Him before their existence — for knowledge is potent to attach to their existence but did not actually attach to their existence because knowledge of a thing's existence before its existence is ignorance; yes, that it will be is an eternal actualizing attachment; and the answer to the first ones who said: if there were a potential attachment, ignorance would follow because the one potent to know is not knowing — the answer is that the actual existence of Zayd is not suitable to be known before its actual existence, and non-attachment of knowledge to something that is not suitable to be known is not considered ignorance, just as non-attachment of power to the impossible is not considered incapacity. And a temporally originated actualizing attachment with respect to other than Him after their actual existence. But the truth is that He has only one actualizing eternal attachment.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine attribute of knowledge. It begins by explaining the phrase 'وَعِلْمُهُ' from the poem, stating that it is a necessary attribute of God. The author then defines divine knowledge as an eternal attribute (صفة أزلية) that encompasses all necessary, possible, and impossible things comprehensively, with no prior concealment. He then explains the correct understanding of the attachment (تعلق) of this knowledge: it is a single, eternal, actual (تنجيزي قديم) attachment that covers all things — past, present, and future — without change, since change is in the known objects, not in the knowledge itself. The author rejects any potential (صلوحي) or temporally originated (حادث) attachment, because that would imply ignorance or prior ignorance. This is the position of Sanusi and his followers. However, the author notes that some have proposed three types of attachments: (1) an eternal actual attachment regarding God's essence and attributes, (2) an eternal potential attachment regarding other things before they exist, and (3) a temporally originated actual attachment after things come into existence. The author refutes the potential attachment by arguing that non-attachment to something that is not yet knowable is not ignorance, just as non-attachment of power to the impossible is not weakness. Then he concludes that the correct view is that there is only one attachment: the eternal actual one.

Correction: God's knowledge is one eternal actual attachment covering all times without change. Temporal attachment would imply ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God's knowledge had a beginning as the temporal attachment suggests, He would have been ignorant before. Do you ever secretly assume God doesn't know what's best for you in a certain area because of your current pain? The Triple Heresy of the Philosophers
The commentator enumerates three points of unbelief among philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that denying God's knowledge of particulars, the temporal origination of the world, and the resurrection of bodies are three cardinal errors that amount to unbelief in Islam.

Core Idea
Three denials make the philosophers disbelievers: knowledge of particulars, creation, and resurrection.

In this passage, the commentator firmly declares that the philosophers who deny God's knowledge of particulars have committed unbelief. He links this denial to two other major heresies they are known for: denying that the world was created in time, and denying the bodily resurrection. Together, these three positions form a clear line between Islamic orthodoxy and philosophical deviation.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmation of God's knowledge
Start with the first line: 'فيعلم المولى الأشياء أزلاً إجمالاً وتفصيلاً' – notice that God's knowledge is eternal and covers both summary and detail, universals and particulars.
2
Identify the triple heresy
The commentator says 'وكفرت الفلاسفة حيث أنكروا علمه تعالى بالجزئيات' and then lists three matters. Underline them: knowledge of particulars, temporal origination of the world, resurrection of bodies.
The poetic verse reinforces these three.
3
Reflect on the seriousness
Consider why these three are considered kufr. They contradict core Islamic beliefs about God's omniscience, creation out of nothing, and the afterlife.
This helps connect doctrine to creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعلم المولى الأشياء أزلاً إجمالاً وتفصيلاً (۱) ، ويعلم الكليات والجزئيات وكفرت الفلاسفة حيث أنكروا علمه تعالى بالجزئيات، كما كفرت بإنكار حدوث العالم وحشر الأجساد، فقد كفرت بثلاثة (٢) ، كما قال بعضهم : [من الكامل] بِثَلائِةٍ كَفَرَ الفَلاسِفَةُ العِدًا إِذْ أَنْكَرُوها وَهْيَ حَقًّا مُنْبَهُ عِلْمٌ بِجُزْتِي، حُدُوتُ عَوالِمٍ حَشْرٌ لِأَجْسَادِ وَكَانَتْ مَيِّنَهُ ويعلم سبحانه وتعالى ما لا نهاية له ككمالاته، وأنفاس أهل الجنة، فيعلمها تفصيلاً، ويعلم أنه لا نهاية لها، وتوقف التفصيل على التناهي إنما هو بحسب عقولنا، ودخل في ذلك علمه، فيعلم بعلمه أن له علماً.
So the Master knows things eternally both in summary and in detail, and He knows universals and particulars. The philosophers committed unbelief (kufr) where they denied His knowledge of particulars, just as they committed unbelief by denying the temporal origination (huduth) of the world and the resurrection of bodies. Thus they committed unbelief in three matters, as one of them said: [in the meter of complete] 'In three matters the hostile philosophers disbelieved, when they denied them, while they are truth made known: knowledge of particulars, temporal origination of worlds, resurrection of bodies that were dead.' And He, exalted, knows what has no end, such as His perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise; He knows them in detail, and He knows that they have no end. The dependence of detailed knowledge on finitude is only with respect to our intellects. And included in that is His knowledge; so He knows by His knowledge that He has knowledge.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage affirms that God's knowledge encompasses all things, both universal and particular, in an eternal manner, both summarily and in detail. The author then declares that the philosophers who deny God's knowledge of particulars are guilty of unbelief (kufr). This denial is one of three major heresies of the philosophers, the other two being: denial of the temporal origination (حدوث) of the world, and denial of the resurrection of bodies. The author cites a poetic verse that lists these three: knowledge of particulars, temporal origination of worlds, and resurrection of dead bodies. The passage then addresses an objection: how can God know things that are infinite in number (e.g., His perfections, the breaths of Paradise)? The answer is that the limitation of detailed knowledge to finite things is only relevant to our intellects, not to God. God knows infinite things in detail. Moreover, included in His knowledge is His own knowledge — that is, He knows that He has knowledge, which is an aspect of His self-knowledge.

Key Terms
كفر
الفلاسفة
علم الجزئيات
حدوث العالم
حشر الأجساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying God's knowledge of particulars is a minor theological disagreement, not unbelief.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this denial is kufr, alongside denying creation and resurrection.

Checkpoint
How many matters of unbelief are attributed to the philosophers in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Denying God's knowledge of particulars is one of three cardinal sins that place philosophers outside the fold of Islam.

God's Knowledge of Infinite Things
The commentator explains God's detailed knowledge of infinite things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge is not limited by infinitude; He knows infinite things in detail, while our intellects require finitude for detailed comprehension.

Core Idea
Only our minds need finitude; God knows infinite details directly.

The previous lesson established that God knows particulars, but a philosophical objection arises: how can God know things that are infinite in number, like His own perfections or the breaths of Paradise? In this passage, the commentator resolves the objection by distinguishing between our limited intellects and God's unlimited knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about infinity
The passage says 'ويعلم سبحانه وتعالى ما لا نهاية له ككمالاته، وأنفاس أهل الجنة' – God knows things without end.
Examples: His attributes and the breaths of Paradise.
2
Understand the answer
The commentator states that the limitation of detailed knowledge to finite things is only true for our intellects. For God, detailed knowledge of the infinite is possible.
This is a key distinction.
3
Consider the included knowledge
Finally, note that God knows His own knowledge – 'فيعلم بعلمه أن له علماً' – this shows self-awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعلم المولى الأشياء أزلاً إجمالاً وتفصيلاً (۱) ، ويعلم الكليات والجزئيات وكفرت الفلاسفة حيث أنكروا علمه تعالى بالجزئيات، كما كفرت بإنكار حدوث العالم وحشر الأجساد، فقد كفرت بثلاثة (٢) ، كما قال بعضهم : [من الكامل] بِثَلائِةٍ كَفَرَ الفَلاسِفَةُ العِدًا إِذْ أَنْكَرُوها وَهْيَ حَقًّا مُنْبَهُ عِلْمٌ بِجُزْتِي، حُدُوتُ عَوالِمٍ حَشْرٌ لِأَجْسَادِ وَكَانَتْ مَيِّنَهُ ويعلم سبحانه وتعالى ما لا نهاية له ككمالاته، وأنفاس أهل الجنة، فيعلمها تفصيلاً، ويعلم أنه لا نهاية لها، وتوقف التفصيل على التناهي إنما هو بحسب عقولنا، ودخل في ذلك علمه، فيعلم بعلمه أن له علماً.
So the Master knows things eternally both in summary and in detail, and He knows universals and particulars. The philosophers committed unbelief (kufr) where they denied His knowledge of particulars, just as they committed unbelief by denying the temporal origination (huduth) of the world and the resurrection of bodies. Thus they committed unbelief in three matters, as one of them said: [in the meter of complete] 'In three matters the hostile philosophers disbelieved, when they denied them, while they are truth made known: knowledge of particulars, temporal origination of worlds, resurrection of bodies that were dead.' And He, exalted, knows what has no end, such as His perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise; He knows them in detail, and He knows that they have no end. The dependence of detailed knowledge on finitude is only with respect to our intellects. And included in that is His knowledge; so He knows by His knowledge that He has knowledge.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage affirms that God's knowledge encompasses all things, both universal and particular, in an eternal manner, both summarily and in detail. The author then declares that the philosophers who deny God's knowledge of particulars are guilty of unbelief (kufr). This denial is one of three major heresies of the philosophers, the other two being: denial of the temporal origination (حدوث) of the world, and denial of the resurrection of bodies. The author cites a poetic verse that lists these three: knowledge of particulars, temporal origination of worlds, and resurrection of dead bodies. The passage then addresses an objection: how can God know things that are infinite in number (e.g., His perfections, the breaths of Paradise)? The answer is that the limitation of detailed knowledge to finite things is only relevant to our intellects, not to God. God knows infinite things in detail. Moreover, included in His knowledge is His own knowledge — that is, He knows that He has knowledge, which is an aspect of His self-knowledge.

Key Terms
ما لا نهاية
تفصيل
عقولنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason that God can know infinite things in detail?
A) Because infinite things are not real
B) Because the limitation to finite objects is only for our intellects
C) Because God only knows things summarily
D) Because infinite things become finite in God's mind

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is not constrained by the finitude of objects; He knows infinite details directly, unlike human minds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you worry about the future, do you truly believe that God knows every detail of your tomorrow as vividly as the breaths of Paradise are known to Him? What would change if you let that belief shape your daily anxiety?
?
The passage says that the need for finitude is only in our intellects. Have you ever limited God's knowledge to what you can understand, thinking He can't grasp the complexity of your struggles? Will you now consciously surrender that limitation and trust His infinite comprehension?
?
God knows that He knows—He is fully aware of His own knowledge. How does this reality challenge you to seek knowledge of God not through your own reasoning alone, but through His self-disclosure in revelation? What step will you take today to prioritize learning from His words over your own theories?

The Eternal Nature of Divine Knowledge
Clarification on the modality of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge is eternal and that not knowing impossible things is not a deficiency.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is eternal; not knowing impossibles is not ignorance.

The author begins by addressing a potential objection: if God does not know something, is that ignorance? He clarifies that what cannot be known is not subject to knowledge, just as power cannot extend to the impossible. He then explains that although we speak of knowledge 'attaching' to things after they exist, in reality God's knowledge is one eternal determinative attachment. This lesson helps us grasp the unique modality of divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the first half of the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation covering the first part up to 'eternal determinative attachment'. Notice the analogy with power.
The key analogy: non-attachment to impossibles is not ignorance just as non-attachment of power is not incapability.
2
Understand the two types of attachment
The author contrasts a 'temporal determinative attachment' (which would be for others) with the truth that God has only an 'eternal determinative attachment'. Reflect on why God's knowledge cannot be temporal.
This is a crucial point for distinguishing divine from creaturely knowledge.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this understanding guards against anthropomorphism. God's knowledge is not acquired nor changes with events.

معلوماً قبل وجوده بالفعل، وعدم تعلقِ العلم بشيء لا يصلح أن يكون معلوماً، لا يعد جهلاً، كما أن عدم تعلق القدرة بالمستحيل، لا يعد عجزاً.
وتعلق تنجيزي حادث بالنسبة لغيره تعالى بعد وجوده بالفعل، لكن الحق أنه ليس له إلا تعلق تنجيزي قديم .

ويعلم سبحانه وتعالى ما لا نهاية له ككمالاته، وأنفاس أهل الجنة، فيعلمها تفصيلاً، ويعلم أنه لا نهاية لها، وتوقف التفصيل على التناهي إنما هو بحسب عقولنا، ودخل في ذلك علمه، فيعلم بعلمه أن له علماً.

And a temporal determinative attachment with respect to other than Him, the Exalted, after its existence in actuality, but the truth is that He has only an eternal determinative attachment. Thus the Master knows things from eternity summarily and in detail (1), and He knows universals and particulars. And the philosophers disbelieved when they denied His knowledge of particulars, just as they disbelieved by denying the origination of the world and the resurrection of bodies. And He, glorified and exalted, knows that which has no end, like His perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise, so He knows them in detail, and He knows that they have no end. And the dependence of detailed knowledge on finitude is only according to our intellects. And included in that is His knowledge, so He knows by His knowledge that He has knowledge.

Commentary
This passage deals with the modality and scope of God's knowledge. ?

?
If God's knowledge is one eternal determinative attachment, what does that mean for how you view your past, present, and future? Are you living as if God's knowledge is timeless and all-encompassing, or do you act as if He is unaware of your struggles?

The Scope of Divine Knowledge and the Error of the Philosophers
Affirmation of divine omniscience and refutation of philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge includes all things, both universal and particular, and that philosophers who deny this have disbelieved.

Core Idea
God knows all particulars; denying this is disbelief.

The author now extends the discussion to the scope of God's knowledge. He affirms that God knows all things from eternity in both a summary and detailed manner, including universals and particulars. He then issues a strong warning: the philosophers who deny God's knowledge of particulars have disbelieved, alongside their denial of the world's origination and bodily resurrection. This lesson underscores the importance of affirming divine omniscience without exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the text starting from 'Thus the Master knows...' up to the poetic line. Note the three issues mentioned.
The three issues are: knowledge of particulars, origination of the world, and resurrection of bodies.
2
Identify the three points of disbelief
The author lists three things that philosophers denied, and declares their disbelief. The poetic line summarizes them.
Memorizing this triad is common in Islamic theology.
3
Reflect on the gravity of denying God's knowledge
Why is denying God's knowledge of particulars so serious? Consider that it limits His omniscience.

Divine Knowledge of the Infinite
Explanation of God's knowledge of infinite things and self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God knows infinite things in detail and that the limitation of detailed knowledge to finite things applies only to human intellects.

Core Idea
God knows the infinite in detail; finitude is not a barrier for Him.

The author concludes the section on divine knowledge by addressing a subtle question: can God know infinite things in detail? He affirms that God knows His own perfections and the endless breaths of Paradise in detail. The reason detailed knowledge usually requires finitude is only relative to our created intellects. This lesson also touches on God's self-knowledge: He knows that He has knowledge, indicating a reflexive awareness.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Read the text from 'And He, glorified and exalted, knows that which has no end...' to the end. Notice the examples given.
Examples: God's perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise.
2
Understand the point about finitude
The author says the dependence of detailed knowledge on finite things is only according to our intellects. For God, no such restriction exists.
This preserves God's omniscience without limitation.
3
Consider the self-knowledge claim
The final sentence says 'He knows by His knowledge that He has knowledge.' Reflect on the reflexive nature of divine knowledge.
This implies a meta-knowledge.

Critique and Defense of a Definition of Divine Knowledge
Critique and defense of a definition of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical objections raised against a certain definition of divine knowledge and how classical kalam scholars resolved them.

Core Idea
A good definition must avoid circularity, tautology, and false implications.

This passage presents a rigorous kalam critique of a definition of divine knowledge offered by a commentator. The author first lists three objections—problems of prior ignorance, circularity (dawr), and redundancy (tahsil al-hasil)—then provides clear answers that refine the definition. Studying this debate sharpens your appreciation for precision in theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the author states his own definition is superior, then lists three objections, then answers them.
Pay attention to the technical terms like dawr and tahsil al-hasil.
2
Identify the three objections
The passage enumerates three objections starting with 'minha' (among them). Underline each one: (1) 'inkishaf' implies prior ignorance, (2) the term 'ma'lumat' leads to circularity, (3) 'information are disclosed' is a tautology.
Each objection attacks a different aspect of the definition.
3
Study the three answers
The answers begin with 'wa ujiba 'an al-awwal' etc. Note how each answer addresses the objection without altering the core definition.
The answer to the second objection uses the concept of 'jiha' (aspect) to break the circularity.
4
Reflect on the lesson for theology
Ask yourself: Why do kalam scholars care so much about definitions? How does this debate protect the transcendence of God?
This shows that divine attributes must be described without implying deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتعريف الذي ذكرناه أولى من التعريف الذي ذكره الشارح وغيره، وهو قوله: (صفةٌ أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تنكشف بها المعلومات عند تعلقها بها)؛ لأن هذا التعريف معترض من وجوه :
منها أن قوله : (تنكشف يقتضي سبق الجهل ؛ لأن الانكشاف ظهور الشيء بعد الخفاء،
ومنها أن المعلومات جمع معلوم ؛ وهو مشتق من العلم، والمشتق متوقف على المشتق منه، كما أن العلم متوقف على معرفة المعلوم؛ لأنه أخذ في تعريفه، فكل منهما متوقف على الآخر، فجاء الدور.
ومنها أن قوله : (المعلومات) يقتضي أنها منكشفة قبل الانكشاف، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل .

وأجيب عن الأول: بأن المراد بالانكشاف هنا ظهور الشيء من غير سبق خفاء.
وعن الثاني بأن المشتق منه هو العلم الذي هو المصدر، والمعرف بمعنى الصفة، وبأن الجهة منفكَّةٌ ؛ لأن توقف العلم على المعلوم من حيث المعرفة، وتوقف المعلوم على العلم من حيث الاشتقاق .
وعن الثالث بأن المراد بالمعلومات الأمور من غير نظر لوقوع العلم عليها، وبه يندفع الدورُ أيضاً، وبأن المراد بالمعلومات ما من شأنها أن تُعلم، وكان الأولى حذف قوله : ( عند تعلقها بها؛ لأنه يقتضي أن العلم تارة يتعلق بالمعلومات، وتارة لا يتعلق بها، وليس كذلك ؛ لأن علم الله متعلق بالمعلومات أزلاً وأبداً .
And the definition we mentioned is more worthy than the definition mentioned by the commentator (al-Sharih) and others, which is his statement: 'An eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, the Exalted, by which information are disclosed when they attach to it.' Because this definition is objectionable in aspects: Among them is that his statement 'disclose' implies prior ignorance, because disclosure is the appearance of a thing after concealment. Among them is that 'information' (al-ma'lumat) is the plural of 'known' (ma'lum), which is derived from 'knowledge' (al-'ilm), and the derived term depends on the source term, just as knowledge depends on the knowledge of the known because it is taken in its definition. Thus each depends on the other, resulting in circularity (al-dawr). Among them is that his statement 'the information' implies that they are disclosed before disclosure, necessitating the attainment of the attained (tahsil al-hasil).

And it was answered concerning the first: that what is meant by disclosure here is the appearance of a thing without prior concealment. Concerning the second: that the source term is 'knowledge' which is the infinitive (al-masdar), and the defined term is in the sense of the attribute, and that the aspect (al-jiha) is separate, because the dependence of knowledge on the known is from the aspect of recognition (al-ma'rifa), while the dependence of the known on knowledge is from the aspect of derivation. Concerning the third: that what is meant by 'information' is the things themselves without consideration of the occurrence of knowledge upon them, and by this the circularity is also repelled. And that what is meant by 'information' is that which is apt to be known. And it would have been better to omit his statement 'when they attach to it' because it implies that knowledge sometimes attaches to information and sometimes does not, and it is not so, because God's knowledge is attached to information eternally and perpetually.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a criticism of a definition of divine knowledge offered by the commentator and others, and then defends the preferred definition. The author first states his own definition is superior. He then lists three objections to the alternative definition: (1) the verb 'disclose' implies prior ignorance; (2) the term 'information' is derived from 'knowledge', leading to circularity; (3) saying 'information are disclosed' implies they were already disclosed, which is a tautology (tahsil al-hasil). Then the answers are given: (1) 'disclosure' here means appearance without prior concealment; (2) the derivation is from 'knowledge' as a source, and the dependence is from different aspects; (3) 'information' means the things themselves, not necessarily known, and it would be better to omit the phrase 'when they attach to it' because God's knowledge is always attached.

Key Terms
تعريف
انكشاف
دور
تحصيل الحاصل
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any derivation from a term automatically leads to vicious circularity in a definition.
Correction: Circularity can be avoided if the dependency is from different aspects (jiha), as explained: knowledge depends on the known from the aspect of recognition, but the known depends on knowledge from the aspect of derivation. These are not the same relation, so no circularity.

Checkpoint
What is the third objection to the definition of divine knowledge?
A) It implies circularity
B) It implies prior ignorance
C) It implies attaining what is already attained (tahsil al-hasil)
D) It is not eternal

Key Takeaway
This passage demonstrates the precision required in defining divine attributes to avoid any implication of imperfection, such as ignorance, circularity, or redundancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah's knowledge in your own words, do you ever use phrases that might unintentionally imply imperfection, like 'He learns' or 'He discovers'? Will you examine your speech today and correct any such expression?
?
The passage shows that even subtle wording can distort the meaning of divine attributes. Is there a belief you hold about Allah that, if pushed even slightly, could be tainted by human limitations? How will you guard against such slippage?
?
The author criticizes a definition because it uses 'when they attach to it', implying time-bound attachment. Do you ever think of Allah's knowledge as reacting to events in time, rather than being eternal and all-encompassing? What step will you take today to root out any temporal concept from your understanding of Allah's attributes?

Why God's Knowledge Is Not Acquired
The author explains why God's knowledge cannot be called acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impermissible to believe that Allah's knowledge is acquired, and the distinction between utterance and belief in this context.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is essential, not acquired through reasoning.

The author addresses a potential misunderstanding about the prohibition on saying God's knowledge is acquired. It might seem that only the verbal expression is forbidden while the underlying meaning is acceptable, but the text clarifies that the belief itself is impossible. This lesson explains why acquired knowledge is incompatible with Allah's omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'muktasab', 'kasbi', 'nazar', and 'istidlal'. Understand how they relate.
3
Understand the author's clarification
The author warns against thinking only the utterance is forbidden. The belief itself is invalid because acquired knowledge depends on reasoning.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider why Allah's knowledge cannot be acquired: He is the Creator of all things and does not need to reason to know.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلا يُقَالُ: مُكْتَسَبْ) أي : ولا يجوز شرعاً ولا عقلاً أن يطلق على علمه أنه مكتسب (۱) ، وهذا ربَّما يُوهم أن النهي عن القول والإطلاق مع صحة المعنى، وليس كذلك، ولعل تفسير القول بالاعتقاد هنا ،أحسن وعليه فالمعنى: ولا يجوز أن يعتقد أن علمه مُكتسب لاستحالته ؛ لأن الكسبي عرفاً هو العلم الحاصل عن النظر والاستدلال، فإذا أقمت دليلاً على حدوث العالم بأن قلت : العالم متغير، وكلُّ متغير
His statement: 'And it is not said: acquired (muktasab).' Meaning: It is not permissible legally or rationally to apply to His knowledge that it is acquired (1). And this might suggest that the prohibition is on the utterance and application while the meaning is correct, but it is not so. Perhaps the interpretation of 'saying' (al-qawl) as 'belief' (al-i'tiqad) here is better. Accordingly, the meaning is: It is not permissible to believe that His knowledge is acquired because of its impossibility. For 'acquired' (al-kasbi) conventionally is knowledge obtained through investigation (al-nazar) and inference (al-istidlal). So if you establish a proof for the origination of the world by saying: The world is changing, and everything changing...
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage explains why God's knowledge cannot be described as acquired (muktasab). It begins by quoting the phrase from the text: 'And it is not said: acquired.' The commentator clarifies that it is forbidden both by sharia and reason to call God's knowledge acquired. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding: one might think the prohibition is only on the utterance while the meaning is correct, but that is not the case. Instead, the prohibition is on the belief itself, because acquired knowledge is defined as knowledge obtained through investigation and inference, which cannot apply to God who is omniscient. The example of proving the world's origination is given to illustrate what kasbi entails, but the sentence is cut off.

Misconception: It is only prohibited to utter that Allah's knowledge is acquired, but the meaning itself is permissible.
Correction: The prohibition extends to the belief itself because acquired knowledge necessarily involves reasoning, which cannot apply to Allah.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is essential and not dependent on any process, so it cannot be described as acquired.

Proof of Allah's Knowledge from His Perfect Actions
Sanusi presents a syllogistic proof that Allah must have knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam syllogism that Allah's precise, intentional actions necessitate His knowledge.

Core Idea
Perfect action requires perfect knowledge—Allah's creation proves His omniscience.

Sanusi presents a concise syllogistic argument that Allah must possess knowledge. The argument is based on the observable fact that Allah's actions are precise and intentional. Any agent who acts with such precision must have knowledge, so Allah, the ultimate agent, necessarily has knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical structure: a major premise, a minor premise, and a conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises: (1) Allah acts precisely and intentionally, (2) Everyone who acts like that has knowledge. Then identify the conclusion: So Allah has knowledge.
This is a standard Aristotelian syllogism.
3
Reflect on the connection between action and knowledge
Why does precise action imply knowledge? Because an agent cannot produce a well-ordered, purposeful act without understanding what they are doing and why.
This connects to the broader kalam principle that attributes are inferred from effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على وجوب العلم له تعالى أن تقول : الله فاعل فعلاً متقناً محكماً بالقصد والاختيار، وكلُّ من كان كذلك يجب له العلم، فالله يجب له العلم . فإن قيل : إن هذا الدليل إنما يفيد علمه بالجائزات فقط، فما الدليل على علمه بالواجبات والمستحيلات؟ أجيب بأن دليل ذلك دليل عدم افتقاره للمخصص ؛ لأنه لو لم يعلم بالواجبات والمستحيلات لكان محتاجاً لمن يكمله، فيلزم أن يكون حادثاً فيفتقر للمخصص، وقد تقدَّم دليل عدم افتقاره للمخصص.
And the proof for the necessity of knowledge for Him, exalted, is that you say: Allah is an agent who does an act, precise, firm, with intention and choice, and everyone who is like that must have knowledge, so Allah must have knowledge. If it is said: This proof only yields His knowledge of contingent things, so what is the proof for His knowledge of necessary and impossible things? It is answered: the proof of that is the proof of His not needing a specifier; because if He did not know necessary and impossible things, He would be in need of someone to perfect Him, which would entail that He is originated, thus He would need a specifier, and the proof of His not needing a specifier has preceded.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage contains a proof for the necessity of divine knowledge and a response to an objection. First, the author states the proof: Allah performs actions that are precise, firm, intentional, and chosen; everyone who does such actions must have knowledge; therefore, Allah must have knowledge. The objection claims this proof only demonstrates knowledge of contingent beings (الجائزات), not of necessary or impossible things. The answer is that the same reasoning used to prove that Allah does not need a specifier (المخصص) also shows He must know necessities and impossibilities. Without such knowledge, He would be imperfect and dependent, contradicting His necessary existence. This argument references a previous proof for Allah's independence from a specifier.

Key Terms
دليل
وجوب العلم
فاعل متقن
جائزات
واجبات
مستحيلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second premise in the proof for Allah's knowledge?
A) Allah is a free agent.
B) Everyone who acts precisely and intentionally must have knowledge.
C) Knowledge is a necessary attribute of perfection.
D) The world is created with wisdom.

Key Takeaway
The precision and intentionality of Allah's actions logically require that He possesses knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate design in nature—like the human eye or the orbit of planets—do you immediately recognize that this perfection is only possible because Allah knows everything with complete knowledge? Or do you passively admire it without linking it to His omniscience? Choose one creation today and deliberately say to yourself: 'This exists only because Allah knows it perfectly,' and then thank Him for that knowledge.
?
This proof says that anyone who acts with precision and intention must have knowledge. How often do you act without intention or knowledge? In your daily worship, are your prayers precise and deliberate, or are they rushed and thoughtless? If Allah's actions are the epitome of precision, what one change will you make to bring more intentionality into your salah starting today?
?
The proof concludes that Allah's knowledge is necessary—He cannot be ignorant. Do you live as if Allah knows every hidden thought, every secret sin, every selfish motive? If you truly believed that nothing escapes His knowledge, would you change how you behave when you are alone? Right now, think of one secret you are afraid to expose, and then repent to the One who knows it and still calls you to Him.

Answering the Objection on the Scope of Divine Knowledge
Sanusi answers an objection about the scope of divine knowledge using the specifier argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from Allah's independence from a specifier proves His knowledge of necessary and impossible things.

Core Idea
Allah's self-sufficiency implies He knows all categories of reality.

The previous proof established that Allah knows contingent things. But an objector asks: what about necessary and impossible things? Sanusi answers by linking knowledge to the earlier proof that Allah does not need a specifier. Without knowledge of all categories, Allah would be imperfect and dependent, contradicting His necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the objection: the proof only covers contingents. Then read the answer that references the specifier argument.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims the previous proof only shows Allah knows contingent things (جائزات), not necessary (واجبات) or impossible (مستحيلات) things.
This reflects a concern that the argument might be limited.
3
Understand the answer's logic
The answer says the proof for Allah's not needing a specifier (المخصص) also proves His knowledge of all categories. Why? Because ignorance of any category would make Him dependent on someone to perfect Him, which would imply He is originated and needs a specifier—contradicting the established fact that He has no specifier.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the proof for the necessity of knowledge for Him, exalted, is that you say: Allah is an agent who does an act, precise, firm, with intention and choice, and everyone who is like that must have knowledge, so Allah must have knowledge. If it is said: This proof only yields His knowledge of contingent things, so what is the proof for His knowledge of necessary and impossible things? It is answered: the proof of that is the proof of His not needing a specifier; because if He did not know necessary and impossible things, He would be in need of someone to perfect Him, which would entail that He is originated, thus He would need a specifier, and the proof of His not needing a specifier has preceded.

The objection claims this proof only demonstrates knowledge of contingent beings (الجائزات), not of necessary or impossible things. How would your behavior shift if you internalized that He knows all, even the very thing you are trying to hide?

What is one concrete step you will take this week to acknowledge that He knows what is best for you more than you do?

The Prohibition of Calling Allah's Knowledge Acquired
Author states the prohibition and clarifies it is a matter of belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impermissible, both legally and rationally, to believe that Allah's knowledge is acquired, and why the prohibition is not merely verbal.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge cannot be acquired because acquisition implies prior ignorance.

The author begins by clarifying a statement from the matn: it is not permissible to say or believe that Allah's knowledge is acquired. He warns against a common misconception that the prohibition is only on the wording while the meaning is acceptable. Instead, the belief itself is impossible. This lesson unpacks that initial assertion.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part where the prohibition is stated.
Notice the author emphasizes both legal and rational impermissibility.
2
Understand the clarification
The author warns against thinking the prohibition is just about the word. Understand why it is about belief.
3
Grasp the implication
Reflect on why calling knowledge 'acquired' is impossible for Allah, setting the stage for the next lesson.
This lesson only covers the initial point; the reasons come next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلا يُقَالُ: مُكْتَسَبْ) أي : ولا يجوز شرعاً ولا عقلاً أن يطلق على علمه أنه مكتسب (۱) ، وهذا ربَّما يُوهم أن النهي عن القول والإطلاق مع صحة المعنى، وليس كذلك، ولعل تفسير القول بالاعتقاد هنا ،أحسن وعليه فالمعنى: ولا يجوز أن يعتقد أن علمه مُكتسب لاستحالته ؛ لأن الكسبي عرفاً هو العلم الحاصل عن النظر والاستدلال، فإذا أقمت دليلاً على حدوث العالم بأن قلت : العالم متغير، وكلُّ متغير حادث، ينتج العالم حادث، فالعلم بحدوث العالم حاصل عن نظر واستدلال، فهو : كسبي . وقيل : الكسبي هو ما تعلقت به القدرة الحادثة. وعلى هذا التعريف فيشمل العلم الضروري الحاصل بالحواس كالعلم الحاصل بالإبصار أو بالشم، بخلافه على التعريف الأول ؛ وعلى كل من التعريفين لا يُقال لعلم الله : كسبي ؛ لأنَّه يلزم منه قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى، ويلزم منه أيضاً سبق الجهل في حقه تعالى، وهو محال؛ وما ورد ممَّا يوهم اكتساب علمه تعالى كقوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : وثُمَّ بَعَثْنَهُمْ لِنَعْلَمَ أَيُّ الْحِزْبَيْنِ أَحْصَى [الكهف: (۱۱۲) مؤول على أن المراد والله أعلم ليظهر لهم متعلَّق ،علمنا ، أو أن المراد بـ (نَعْلَم) مفتوح (النون واللام (نُعْلِم) مضموم النون ومكسور اللام كما قاله الشيخ الملوي، ومما لا يقال : إنه من باب تنزيل المتكلم منزلة من لم يعلم، وإن ذكره في اليواقيت عن ابن العربي، ولا أظنه إلا مدسوساً على الشيخ. فإن قيل : ظاهر الآية التعليل مع أن أفعال الله لا تعلل أجيب بجعل لامه للعاقبة والفائدة، فالآية أوهمت أن علمه مكتسب، وقد علمت جوابه، وأوهمت تعليل فعله وقد علمت جوابه فالكلام في مقامين وإن أوهم كلام الشارح خلافَهُ .
His saying: 'and it is not said: acquired' means: it is not permissible, legally or rationally, to apply to His knowledge that it is acquired. This might give the impression that the prohibition is on the statement and application while the meaning is sound, but it is not so. Perhaps interpreting 'saying' as 'believing' here is better, and accordingly the meaning is: it is not permissible to believe that His knowledge is acquired because of its impossibility. Because 'acquired' (al-kasbī) conventionally is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference. So if you present a proof for the temporality of the world by saying: the world is changing, and everything changing is temporal, it follows that the world is temporal, then the knowledge of the world's temporality is obtained through reflection and inference, so it is acquired. And it is said that 'acquired' is what the originated power relates to. According to this definition it includes necessary knowledge obtained through the senses, like knowledge obtained by sight or smell, unlike the first definition. And on either definition, Allah's knowledge is not called acquired; because it would entail the occurrence of originated things in His essence, exalted, and would also entail prior ignorance in His right, exalted, and that is impossible. And what has come that suggests the acquisition of His knowledge, exalted, such as His saying, majestic is He: 'Then We awoke them that We might know which of the two parties was more accurate in counting' [al-Kahf 18:12], is interpreted to mean—and Allah knows best—that the intended meaning is to manifest to them a correlate of Our knowledge, or that the intended meaning of 'we know' (na‘lamu) is 'we make known' (nu‘limu) as Shaykh al-Mallawī said. And it is not to be said that it is of the type of putting the speaker in the position of one who does not know, even if it is mentioned in al-Yawāqīt on the authority of Ibn al-‘Arabī; I do not consider it but as interpolated upon the Shaykh. For if it is said: the apparent meaning of the verse is causation, while Allah's acts are not caused, it is answered by making its lām for consequence and benefit. Thus the verse suggested that His knowledge is acquired, and you have already learned its answer, and it suggested causation of His act, and you have already learned its answer. So the discourse is in two stations, even if the commentator's words suggest otherwise.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of why Allah's knowledge cannot be called 'acquired' (مكتسب). The author begins by clarifying the line from the matn: it is forbidden in both law and reason to apply the term 'acquired' to Allah's knowledge. He warns against the misconception that the prohibition is only on the wording while the meaning is acceptable; rather, it is the belief itself that is impossible. He then gives two definitions of 'acquired knowledge' (الكسبي). The first: knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, exemplified by inferring the world's temporality from change. The second: knowledge that relates to an originated power, which includes sensory knowledge. On both definitions, Allah's knowledge cannot be acquired because it would entail originated accidents (قيام الحوادث) in His essence and prior ignorance—both impossible. The author then addresses a Quranic verse (18:12) that appears to attribute acquisition to Allah. He cites two interpretations: either it means manifesting a correlate of His eternal knowledge, or the verb is read as 'nu‘limu' (We make known). He rejects a third interpretation (anthropomorphic projection) as likely spurious. Finally, an objection is raised that the verse implies causation (تعليل) for Allah's action, which is not allowed. The reply is that the lām in the verse indicates consequence (العاقبة) and benefit (الفائدة), not causation. Thus the verse does not imply that Allah's knowledge is acquired or that His action is caused.

Key Terms
مكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the prohibition is only about the word 'acquired' but the meaning is fine.
Correction: The author insists the belief itself is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it not permissible to say Allah's knowledge is acquired?
A) Because the word is disrespectful
B) Because the belief itself is impossible
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran
D) Because it implies Allah learns from humans

Key Takeaway
It is impermissible to believe that Allah's knowledge is acquired because acquisition is inherently impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty in your life, do you ever act as if Allah's knowledge is incomplete or that He needs to 'acquire' information about your situation? In what ways do your worries reveal a hidden belief that Allah's knowledge might be lacking?
?
Reflect on a moment you questioned Allah's wisdom or knowledge of your circumstances. Did that doubt stem from thinking that Allah's knowledge is like human knowledge—acquired over time? How can you repent from that implicit assumption?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's knowledge is not acquired, how must that change the way you pray, plan, and trust Him? What specific step can you take today to live that belief?

Two Definitions of Acquired Knowledge and Their Application to Allah
Author defines 'acquired' in two ways and rules it out for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of 'acquired knowledge' (الكسبي) and why Allah's knowledge cannot be described by either.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge means it is from reflection or relates to created power; both are impossible for Allah.

The author presents two conventional definitions of acquired knowledge: one based on how knowledge is obtained (through reflection and inference), and another based on relation to created power. He then examines each and demonstrates why applying either to Allah is impossible, listing the theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Learn the first definition
Read about knowledge from reflection and inference.
The author gives an example: inferring the world's temporality.
2
Learn the second definition
Read about knowledge related to originated power.
This includes sensory knowledge.
3
See the conclusion
Understand why both entail impossibility.
The consequences: originated accidents in Allah's essence and prior ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلا يُقَالُ: مُكْتَسَبْ) أي : ولا يجوز شرعاً ولا عقلاً أن يطلق على علمه أنه مكتسب (۱) ، وهذا ربَّما يُوهم أن النهي عن القول والإطلاق مع صحة المعنى، وليس كذلك، ولعل تفسير القول بالاعتقاد هنا ،أحسن وعليه فالمعنى: ولا يجوز أن يعتقد أن علمه مُكتسب لاستحالته ؛ لأن الكسبي عرفاً هو العلم الحاصل عن النظر والاستدلال، فإذا أقمت دليلاً على حدوث العالم بأن قلت : العالم متغير، وكلُّ متغير حادث، ينتج العالم حادث، فالعلم بحدوث العالم حاصل عن نظر واستدلال، فهو : كسبي . وقيل : الكسبي هو ما تعلقت به القدرة الحادثة. وعلى هذا التعريف فيشمل العلم الضروري الحاصل بالحواس كالعلم الحاصل بالإبصار أو بالشم، بخلافه على التعريف الأول ؛ وعلى كل من التعريفين لا يُقال لعلم الله : كسبي ؛ لأنَّه يلزم منه قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى، ويلزم منه أيضاً سبق الجهل في حقه تعالى، وهو محال؛ وما ورد ممَّا يوهم اكتساب علمه تعالى كقوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : وثُمَّ بَعَثْنَهُمْ لِنَعْلَمَ أَيُّ الْحِزْبَيْنِ أَحْصَى [الكهف: (۱۱۲) مؤول على أن المراد والله أعلم ليظهر لهم متعلَّق ،علمنا ، أو أن المراد بـ (نَعْلَم) مفتوح (النون واللام (نُعْلِم) مضموم النون ومكسور اللام كما قاله الشيخ الملوي، ومما لا يقال : إنه من باب تنزيل المتكلم منزلة من لم يعلم، وإن ذكره في اليواقيت عن ابن العربي، ولا أظنه إلا مدسوساً على الشيخ. فإن قيل : ظاهر الآية التعليل مع أن أفعال الله لا تعلل أجيب بجعل لامه للعاقبة والفائدة، فالآية أوهمت أن علمه مكتسب، وقد علمت جوابه، وأوهمت تعليل فعله وقد علمت جوابه فالكلام في مقامين وإن أوهم كلام الشارح خلافَهُ .
His saying: 'and it is not said: acquired' means: it is not permissible, legally or rationally, to apply to His knowledge that it is acquired. This might give the impression that the prohibition is on the statement and application while the meaning is sound, but it is not so. Perhaps interpreting 'saying' as 'believing' here is better, and accordingly the meaning is: it is not permissible to believe that His knowledge is acquired because of its impossibility. Because 'acquired' (al-kasbī) conventionally is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference. So if you present a proof for the temporality of the world by saying: the world is changing, and everything changing is temporal, it follows that the world is temporal, then the knowledge of the world's temporality is obtained through reflection and inference, so it is acquired. And it is said that 'acquired' is what the originated power relates to. According to this definition it includes necessary knowledge obtained through the senses, like knowledge obtained by sight or smell, unlike the first definition. And on either definition, Allah's knowledge is not called acquired; because it would entail the occurrence of originated things in His essence, exalted, and would also entail prior ignorance in His right, exalted, and that is impossible. And what has come that suggests the acquisition of His knowledge, exalted, such as His saying, majestic is He: 'Then We awoke them that We might know which of the two parties was more accurate in counting' [al-Kahf 18:12], is interpreted to mean—and Allah knows best—that the intended meaning is to manifest to them a correlate of Our knowledge, or that the intended meaning of 'we know' (na‘lamu) is 'we make known' (nu‘limu) as Shaykh al-Mallawī said. And it is not to be said that it is of the type of putting the speaker in the position of one who does not know, even if it is mentioned in al-Yawāqīt on the authority of Ibn al-‘Arabī; I do not consider it but as interpolated upon the Shaykh. For if it is said: the apparent meaning of the verse is causation, while Allah's acts are not caused, it is answered by making its lām for consequence and benefit. Thus the verse suggested that His knowledge is acquired, and you have already learned its answer, and it suggested causation of His act, and you have already learned its answer. So the discourse is in two stations, even if the commentator's words suggest otherwise.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of why Allah's knowledge cannot be called 'acquired' (مكتسب). The author begins by clarifying the line from the matn: it is forbidden in both law and reason to apply the term 'acquired' to Allah's knowledge. He warns against the misconception that the prohibition is only on the wording while the meaning is acceptable; rather, it is the belief itself that is impossible. He then gives two definitions of 'acquired knowledge' (الكسبي). The first: knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, exemplified by inferring the world's temporality from change. The second: knowledge that relates to an originated power, which includes sensory knowledge. On both definitions, Allah's knowledge cannot be acquired because it would entail originated accidents (قيام الحوادث) in His essence and prior ignorance—both impossible. The author then addresses a Quranic verse (18:12) that appears to attribute acquisition to Allah. He cites two interpretations: either it means manifesting a correlate of His eternal knowledge, or the verb is read as 'nu‘limu' (We make known). He rejects a third interpretation (anthropomorphic projection) as likely spurious. Finally, an objection is raised that the verse implies causation (تعليل) for Allah's action, which is not allowed. The reply is that the lām in the verse indicates consequence (العاقبة) and benefit (الفائدة), not causation. Thus the verse does not imply that Allah's knowledge is acquired or that His action is caused.

نظر
استدلال
قدرة حادثة
قيام الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sensory knowledge is not acquired, but the second definition includes it.
Correction: According to the second definition, sensory knowledge is also considered acquired because it relates to created power.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a consequence of calling Allah's knowledge acquired, according to the passage?
A) It would imply Allah's knowledge is infinite
B) It would entail originated accidents in Allah's essence
C) It would make Allah's knowledge dependent on creation
D) It would contradict the Quran

Key Takeaway
Acquired knowledge, by either definition, is impossible for Allah because it entails originated accidents and prior ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own knowledge of Allah comes from reflection and inference—you studied proofs and accepted them. But this lesson says that kind of knowledge is 'acquired' and cannot be true of Allah Himself. Does it disturb you that your way of knowing is impossible for the One you claim to know? How can you reconcile that your knowledge is dependent and finite, while His is simple and eternal? What should you do today to remind yourself that your faith rests not on your reasoning but on His self-disclosure?
?
The second definition ties 'acquired' knowledge to an originated power. Think of a piece of knowledge you are proud of—perhaps in your field of work or study. You earned it by your own effort. But do you ever feel that this knowledge belongs to you, as if you originated it? How does this lesson's denial of acquired knowledge for Allah—and the absurdity of attaching originated power to Him—expose the arrogance in claiming ownership of your own intellect? What practical step can you take to return that knowledge to its true Owner?

Interpreting Quranic Verses That Suggest Acquired Knowledge
Author interprets a problematic verse and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to interpret Quranic verses that seem to attribute acquired knowledge to Allah, and why the verse in al-Kahf does not imply causation for Allah's actions.

Core Idea
Apparent scriptural evidence for acquired knowledge must be interpreted in light of rational principles.

The author now addresses a potential objection from the Quran: the verse from Surah al-Kahf that says 'Then We awoke them that We might know...' (18:12). This might suggest that Allah's knowledge is acquired or that His actions are caused. The author provides two valid interpretations and refutes a third, and then answers the objection about causation.

Guided Path
1
Examine the Quranic verse
Read the verse and the author's first interpretation.
The verse is 18:12.
2
Consider the second interpretation
Read about the alternative reading (nu‘limu).
This interpretation avoids the implication of acquired knowledge.
3
Understand the response to causation
Learn how the lām is interpreted as consequence rather than causation.
Allah's acts are not caused by external events.

Why Allah's Knowledge Cannot Be Called Necessary, Theoretical, or Intuitive
Author explains the inapplicability of three epistemological terms to divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the terms ضروري (necessary), نظري (theoretical), and بديهي (intuitive) are not applied to Allah's knowledge despite their apparent suitability.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge defies the categories of human epistemology.

In the previous lesson, we learned that Allah's knowledge is not 'acquired.' Now the author extends this principle to three more terms that might seem applicable but are actually problematic. Each term carries a secondary meaning that implies imperfection or temporality. Understanding why these terms are avoided deepens our grasp of tanzih (transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the three terms
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Identify the three terms being rejected: ضروري, نظري, بديهي.
The passage begins with a general statement linking back to the previous lesson.
2
Understand the two meanings of ضروري
The author explains that 'necessary' has two meanings. The first (not depending on reflection) is true for Allah, but the second (accompanied by compulsion) is not. The term is avoided due to fear of the second meaning.
Notice that even though one meaning is correct, the risk of misunderstanding forces avoidance.
3
See why نظري is like كسبي
The author equates 'theoretical' with 'acquired' in its first definition, meaning it depends on reflection and inference. This entails temporality, so it cannot be applied to Allah.
This argument directly parallels the rejection of 'acquired' in the previous lesson.
4
Reflect on the omission of بديهي explanation
The term 'intuitive' is listed but not elaborated. Based on the pattern, it likely shares problematic connotations similar to 'necessary' (e.g., implying passivity or compulsion). Consider why the author might have left it unaddressed.
Perhaps the objection is obvious from the context, or it is simply repeated for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه كما لا يقال : علمه مكتسب، لا يقال : علمه ضروري ولا نظري ولا بديهي : أما الضروري فهو وإن كان يطلق على ما لا يتوقف على نظر واستدلال، وهو صحيح في حقه تعالى، لكن يطلق أيضاً على ما قارنته الضرورة، فيمتنع أن يُقال: علمه ضروري خوفاً من توهم هذا المعنى (۲) وأما النظري فهو ما توقف على النظر والاستدلال، فهو مرادف للكسبي على تعريفه الأول فيمتنع أن يقال علمه نظري لاستلزامه الحدوث كما مر في الكسبي.
And know that just as it is not said: His knowledge is acquired, it is not said: His knowledge is necessary, theoretical, or intuitive. As for the necessary (al-ḍarūrī), even though it is applied to what does not depend on reflection and inference, and that is correct with respect to Him, exalted, it is also applied to what is accompanied by compulsion, so it is prevented that His knowledge be called necessary for fear of this second meaning. As for the theoretical (al-naẓarī), it is what depends on reflection and inference, so it is synonymous with acquired according to its first definition, and thus it is prevented that His knowledge be called theoretical because it entails temporality, as passed in the case of acquired.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage classifies and refutes three further terms that should not be applied to Allah's knowledge: necessary (ضروري), theoretical (نظري), and intuitive (بديهي). The author argues that although 'necessary' can mean what is not based on reflection—which is true of Allah's knowledge—it also carries the connotation of being compelled (ما قارنته الضرورة), implying passivity, and thus is avoided to prevent misunderstanding. 'Theoretical' is synonymous with 'acquired' in its first definition (dependent on reflection), so it is rejected for the same reasons: it implies temporality and imperfection. The term 'intuitive' (بديهي) is mentioned but not elaborated, presumably because it shares problematic connotations similar to 'necessary'.

Key Terms
ضروري
نظري
بديهي
نظر
استدلال
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'necessary' (ضروري) is fine because it means self-evident, so it could apply to Allah's knowledge.
Correction: However, 'necessary' also implies 'accompanied by compulsion', which is inapplicable to Allah because it suggests passivity. Thus the term is avoided to prevent this misunderstanding.

Checkpoint
Why is the term 'theoretical' (نظري) not used for Allah's knowledge?
A) Because it implies that Allah learns through inference
B) Because it is synonymous with 'acquired' and entails temporality
C) Because it suggests that Allah's knowledge is incomplete
D) Because it is only used for human knowledge

Key Takeaway
The terms necessary, theoretical, and intuitive are avoided for divine knowledge because they carry connotations of compulsion or temporal acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that even terms like 'necessary' and 'theoretical' are too limited for Allah's knowledge. When you speak about Allah, do you catch yourself using human categories that diminish His perfection? How will you guard your tongue from now on?
?
The author avoids terms that could imply compulsion or temporality in Allah. Is there any belief you hold about Allah that, upon reflection, still carries an unworthy implication? Will you actively replace it with what befits Him?
?
You now know that divine knowledge defies human epistemology. Does this truth humble you in your pursuit of knowing Allah? What specific practice can you adopt to remind yourself that He is beyond your concepts?

The Path of Truth: Affirming Divine Attributes
Commentator explains the correct belief in God's attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and refutes the Mu'tazilite denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming God's attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) is essential to Sunni creed and how the commentator refutes the Mu'tazilite objection.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real and eternal, distinct yet not separate from His essence.

This lesson unpacks two key phrases from the poem: 'follow the path of truth' and 'cast away doubts.' The commentator explains that 'the path of truth' is the Sunni belief in God's necessary attributes—power, will, knowledge. 'Casting away doubts' means rejecting the Mu'tazilite argument that multiple attributes imply multiple eternal beings. The commentator shows why this objection fails and why the Mu'tazilite alternative is rationally incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the command
Read the explanation of 'follow the path of truth,' focusing on the definition of truth as correspondence to reality and the identification of that path with Ahl al-Sunna's creed.
The commentator interprets 'truth' as the judgment corresponding to reality, and the path as the Sunni belief in necessary attributes.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The passage says the Mu'tazila deny attributes to avoid 'multiplicity of eternals.' Note the commentator's refutation: multiplicity of attributes within one essence does not mean multiplicity of essences.
The Mu'tazilite fear is based on confusion between attributes and independent beings.
3
Evaluate the refutation
The commentator calls the Mu'tazilite phrase 'powerful by His essence' as raving (hadhayān) because it is unintelligible without power. See the footnote's nuance about linguistic vs. intended meaning.
The Sunni judgment stands on linguistic coherence, even if the Mu'tazila intend a different conceptual meaning.

قوله : (فَاتْبَعْ سَبِيلَ الحَقِّ ) أي : إذا علمت وجوب القدرة والإرادة والعلم له تعالى فاتبع طريقاً هو الحق وهو الحكم المطابق للواقع، فالفاء فاء الفصيحة، والسبيل بمعنى الطريق، وإضافته للحق للبيان ويصح أن يكون في الكلام حذف مضاف والتقدير : سبيل أهل الحق؛ أي: طريقهم ، والمراد به معتقد أهل السنة من وجوب صفات المعاني له تعالى .

وقوله : (وَاطْرَحِ الرِّيَب) أي: وألقِ عنك الشبه فالرّيب جمع ريبة بمعنى الشبهة التي لم تعلم صحتها ولا فسادها وهذا بحسب الأصل (۳) ، وإلا فالقصد هنا الرد على المعتزلة النافين لصفات المعاني لئلا يلزم تعدد القدماء، وهذه شبهة فاسدة؛ لأنَّه لا يضر إلا تعدد ذوات القدماء لا تعدد اتحاد الذات والصفات، ويصح أن يكون في الكلام حذف مضافين والتقدير واطرح سبيل أهل الريب والشكوك النافين لصفات المعاني ؛ لأنهم يقولون: قادر بذاته (۳) مريد بذاته، وهكذا، وهو هذيان؛ لأنَّه لا يُعقل قادر بلا قدرة، ومريد بلا إرادة، وهكذا.

(۳) قوله : ( قادراً بذاته) أي: متمكن من الإيجاد والإعدام بذاته، ومرجح بعض المتقابلات على بعض بذاته، ومنكشفة له جميع الأشياء بذاته، وهذا معقول ؛ فالحكم على كلامهم بأنه هذيان منظور فيه إلى أن معنى قادر ذات ثبتت لها القدرة وهكذا، وهذا المعنى ليس مراداً لهم، لكنه هو المعنى اللغوي لهذه الألفاظ، فهذا هو المسوغ لرد أهل السنة عليهم، وحكمهم بأن كلامهم هذيان.
انتهى أجهوري.
His saying: 'Then follow the path of truth [ḥaqq]' means: when you know the necessity of power (qudra), will (irāda), and knowledge (ʿilm) for Him, may He be exalted, then follow a path that is the truth—and it is the judgment (ḥukm) corresponding to reality (wāqiʿ). So the fā is fā al-faṣīḥa, and al-sabīl means 'path', and its annexation to al-ḥaqq is for explanation. And it is possible that in the speech there is an omitted genitive construct, and the estimation is: the path of the people of truth, i.e., their path. And the intended is the creed of Ahl al-Sunna concerning the necessity of attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) for Him, may He be exalted.
(1)
:
And His saying: 'And cast away doubts [ribab]' means: throw off from yourself the suspicions (shubah). So al-rayb (doubt) is the plural of rayba, meaning the suspicion whose correctness or corruption is not known—this is according to origin. Otherwise, the intent here is to refute the Mu'tazila, who negate (nāfīn) the attributes of meaning, to avoid the implication of multiplicity of eternals (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ). And this is a corrupt suspicion, because it harms only the multiplicity of eternal essences (dhawāt), not the multiplicity of the unity of essence and attributes. And it is possible that in the speech there is an omission of two genitive constructs, and the estimation is: 'and cast off the path of the people of doubt and suspicion, the negators of attributes of meaning'; because they say: (He is) powerful by His essence, willing by His essence, and so on. And this is raving (hadhayān), because it is not intelligible that one is powerful without power, willing without will, and so on.
(1) In (b): 'their way'. (2) His saying: 'this is according to origin' means: its original meaning is what was mentioned. As for terminology, it is what is thought to be a proof but is not a proof; in terminology it is known to be corrupt. End of Ajuhrī.
(3) His saying: 'powerful by His essence' means: able to bring into existence and nonexistence by His essence, and an incliner of some counterparts over others by His essence, and all things are unveiled to Him by His essence. And this is intelligible. So the judgment that their speech is raving is considered with respect to the meaning that 'powerful' is an entity to which power is established, and so on. And this meaning is not intended by them, but it is the linguistic meaning of these words. This is the justification for Ahl al-Sunna to refute them and judge their speech as raving.
End of Ajuhrī.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage provides a doctrinal explanation of two lines from the poem: 'Then follow the path of truth' and 'Cast away doubts.' The commentator interprets these as commands to adhere to the Sunni creed concerning God's attributes (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and to reject the Mu'tazilite position. He explains that 'path of truth' refers to the belief that power, will, and knowledge are necessarily attributes of God. The phrase 'cast away doubts' specifically targets the Mu'tazila who deny these attributes because they fear it would imply multiple eternal beings (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ). The commentator refutes this by arguing that multiplicity of attributes within one essence does not entail multiplicity of essences. He then criticizes the Mu'tazilite claim that God is 'powerful by His essence' without a distinct attribute of power, calling it 'raving' (hadhayān) because it is not rationally conceivable. The footnote adds nuance: the Sunni judgment of 'raving' is based on the linguistic meaning that a powerful being must have power, even if the Mu'tazila mean something else. This justifies the Sunni refutation while acknowledging a different intended meaning.

?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Divine Attributes
Commentator explains and refutes Mu'tazila on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazila who deny that God has attributes of meaning (sifaat al-ma'ani), and why their claim that God is capable by His essence is incoherent.

Core Idea
Denying God's attributes leads to logical nonsense.

This passage from the commentary of Jawharat al-Tawhid explains two crucial lines: 'So follow the path of truth' and 'and discard doubts.' The commentator clarifies that the truth is the Sunni creed affirming that God has essential attributes like power, will, and knowledge. The doubts to discard are the arguments of the Mu'tazila, who claim that God is capable by His essence alone. The commentator shows why this claim is linguistically and rationally indefensible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic passage once through. It is a commentary on two lines of poetry. Notice how the commentator explains each line.
The passage includes footnotes; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the two commands
The two lines are 'follow the path of truth' and 'discard doubts.' The commentator defines what truth and doubts refer to in this context.
Truth is the Sunni creed; doubts are the Mu'tazila's arguments.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila deny attributes of meaning because they fear multiplicity of eternal beings. They say God is capable by His essence, not by power.
This is their alternative to the Sunni position.
4
See the Sunni rebuttal
The commentator argues that the Mu'tazila claim is nonsense because, linguistically, 'capable' requires the attribute of power. One cannot be capable without power.
The rebuttal is based on literal meaning and reason.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this dispute matters for Islamic theology: the core issue is whether God has distinct attributes or is simply described by them.
This lesson establishes the Sunni position on divine attributes.

His saying: 'So follow the path of truth': meaning: when you know the necessity of power, will, and knowledge for Him, Most High, then follow a path that is the truth, which is the judgment conforming to reality. The fa' is the fa' of explanation, and 'sabil' means path, and its annexation to 'truth' is for specification. It is also possible that there is an omitted genitive construction, the estimation being 'the path of the people of truth': i.e., their way, and what is intended by it is the creed of the people of the Sunna regarding the necessity of attributes of meaning for Him, Most High. And his saying: 'And discard doubts': meaning: cast away from you uncertainties, for 'ar-riyab' is the plural of 'riba' meaning a doubt whose correctness or corruption is not known. This is according to the original meaning. But the intent here is to refute the Mu'tazila who deny the attributes of meaning, lest the multiplicity of the eternal be necessitated. This is a corrupt doubt, because only the multiplicity of eternal essences would be harmful, not the multiplicity of the union of essence and attributes. It is also possible that there is an omission of two genitive constructions: the estimation is 'and discard the way of the people of doubt and uncertainties, the deniers of the attributes of meaning', because they say: 'Capable by His essence, willing by His essence, and so on.' This is nonsense, because it is not conceivable that one be capable without power, or willing without will, and so on.
(1) In (B): 'their way'. (2) His saying: 'And this is according to the original meaning': i.e., its original meaning is what was mentioned; as for in terminology, it is what is thought to be a proof but is not a proof; in terminology it is known to be corrupt. End of al-Ujhuri. (3) His saying: 'Capable by His essence': i.e., enabled to bring about existence and nonexistence by His essence, and to incline one of the opposites over another by His essence, and all things are disclosed to Him by His essence. This is conceivable. So the judgment that their statement is nonsense is considered with the understanding that the meaning of 'capable' is a substance to which power is established, and so on. This meaning is not intended by them, but it is the linguistic meaning of these terms. This is the justification for the people of the Sunna to refute them and judge their statement as nonsense. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's explanation of two lines from the poem: 'So follow the path of truth' and 'and discard doubts.' The commentator first explains the meaning of the first line: when you have learned that God necessarily has power, will, and knowledge, you should follow the truth (the judgment that conforms to reality). He notes that 'the path of truth' can be interpreted as 'the path of the people of truth,' i.e., the Ahl al-Sunna, who affirm that God has attributes of meaning (sifaat al-ma'ani). Then he turns to the second line: 'and discard doubts.' He clarifies that the term 'ar-riyab' (doubts) refers to uncertain doubts. Specifically, the doubt being refuted is that of the Mu'tazila, who deny the attributes of meaning because they fear that affirming multiple attributes would necessitate a multiplicity of eternal beings. The commentator argues that this fear is unfounded, because multiplicity of attributes does not imply multiplicity of eternal essences; the essence is one with the attributes. The Mu'tazila claim that God is 'capable by His essence,' 'willing by His essence,' etc. The commentator calls this 'nonsense' because it is inconceivable to be capable without power or willing without will. The footnotes clarify that the term 'nonsense' is from a linguistic perspective: the literal meaning of the words 'capable' and 'willing' requires the presence of power and will. Thus, the Ahl al-Sunna are justified in their refutation.

Correction: The Sunni position holds that attributes are not separate essences; they are one with the essence, so only one Eternal exists.
